THE CLARVOY

**Bias-Control Reference Catalog**

*A field guide to the biases that distort judgment and the measures that
hold it to account*

**How to read this catalog**

The catalog is built around a single idea. Good judgment is not a matter
of character but of record. A claim that cannot be checked by a second
person is not yet knowledge, only confidence. Every entry that follows
turns a familiar way of going wrong into something a reviewer can look
for, and every measure turns a forecast into a number that the future
can confirm or deny.

Each entry holds four parts. The first says plainly what the bias or
measure is. The second gives the equation or control signal, with each
symbol named directly beneath it. The last two describe how the entry
serves a nonprofit board and a company board, since the same failure of
judgment visits the boardroom of a charity and a corporation alike.

A flag in this catalog is never an accusation. It is a request for
better evidence, or for an honest record of what was predicted before
the outcome was known. Greek symbols such as kappa and alpha denote
control coefficients and pressures. Latin symbols such as N and p denote
counts and probabilities. Each calculation defines its own terms beneath
the formula.

**Contents**

**Bias Entries (33)**

*This first part of the catalog names 33 ways a careful mind still goes
wrong. Each entry describes a single bias, the moment it tends to
strike, and a control signal that a reviewer can compute to see whether
a record has guarded against it. The biases are grouped by the kind of
reasoning they distort, from probability and social pressure to value
and evidence.*

**A1 - Anchoring Bias**

**What it measures**

Anchoring bias is the mind's habit of latching onto the first number it
hears and then adjusting too little from it. The mind treats that
opening figure as a gravitational center, even when it is arbitrary. A
board hears that a partner organization once valued a building at 4
million dollars, and every later estimate drifts toward that figure,
though the honest appraisal sits closer to 2.8 million. The danger
arrives early, in the first quote or the first deadline, before anyone
has gathered the evidence that should set the real range.

**How it is calculated**

κ_A = w_ππ + w_ρρ + w_ιι + w_δδ − w_αα

The control here is independent blind aggregation paired with a prior
distribution fixed before the first quote or valuation lands. The score
rises with genuine priors and falls as anchor pressure grows.

κ_A is the strength of the statistical or probability control.

π is the explicit prior or base-rate distribution.

ρ is the reference-class coverage.

ι is the independent blind estimate indicator.

δ is the documented dissent or counterfactual challenge.

α is the anchor or streak pressure strength.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

alt="How a starting number drags a final estimate." />

> **Figure 1.** *How a starting number drags a final estimate.* Two
> groups valued the same item after seeing a different opening figure.
> The group shown the higher anchor landed more than twice as high. The
> first number on the table pulls every later judgment toward itself,
> even when everyone knows it was arbitrary.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, anchoring bias is worth naming whenever the first figure on the table sets the tone for a lease, a salary band, or a fundraising target. The control asks each reviewer to record an independent estimate, and a prior range, before the opening number is spoken aloud. That small discipline keeps the mission and the budget answering to the evidence rather than to whoever quoted first. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, anchoring bias most often bites during pricing, capital allocation, and acquisition talks, where a seller's opening valuation can quietly become the reference for the whole negotiation. Gathering blind estimates and a stated prior before that first number arrives separates the evidence from the momentum of the conversation. The result is a deal judged on its own worth rather than on the anchor someone planted. |

**A2 - Availability Heuristic**

**What it measures**

The availability heuristic is the mind's shortcut of judging how common
something is by how easily an example comes to mind. Vivid or recent
events feel frequent, while quiet base rates stay invisible. After one
widely shared story of a grant being clawed back, a director starts
treating clawbacks as a looming threat, though the records show it has
happened twice in 300 grants. The bias bites hardest right after a
dramatic event, when the freshest memory crowds out the longer record.

**How it is calculated**

κ_A = w_ππ + w_ρρ + w_ιι + w_δδ − w_αα

The control is base-rate retrieval combined with a noise audit run
across the evaluators. The score rises as real frequencies enter the
room and falls as recall pressure takes over.

κ_A is the strength of the statistical or probability control.

π is the explicit prior or base-rate distribution.

ρ is the reference-class coverage.

ι is the independent blind estimate indicator.

δ is the documented dissent or counterfactual challenge.

α is the anchor or streak pressure strength.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the availability heuristic shows up when a single memorable incident reshapes a staffing or partnership decision out of proportion to how often such things actually occur. Pulling the real base rate, and checking how widely evaluators disagree, restores the longer record to the conversation. The board then weighs the threat by its true frequency rather than by how loudly the last example still echoes. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the availability heuristic distorts risk and product judgments when a recent failure or a rival's stumble dominates the discussion. Retrieving the underlying frequency, and auditing the spread of opinion before consensus is claimed, keeps a single vivid case from steering capital. Decisions then track what the data says is common rather than what memory says is recent. |

**A3 - Base Rate Neglect**

**What it measures**

Base rate neglect is the mind's tendency to fix on specific, vivid
evidence and forget the underlying prior probability. A detailed case
description feels so telling that the background frequency simply drops
out of the calculation. A program officer reads a glowing profile of an
applicant and judges success near certain, ignoring that programs of
this kind succeed roughly 30% of the time. The bias bites whenever a
compelling particular case arrives without its statistical backdrop.

**How it is calculated**

κ_A = w_ππ + w_ρρ + w_ιι + w_δδ − w_αα

The control is a Bayesian decomposition that blends the specific
evidence back into the reference class. The score climbs as priors and
reference coverage return to the reasoning.

κ_A is the strength of the statistical or probability control.

π is the explicit prior or base-rate distribution.

ρ is the reference-class coverage.

ι is the independent blind estimate indicator.

δ is the documented dissent or counterfactual challenge.

α is the anchor or streak pressure strength.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

<img

title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.65625in"
alt="Why a positive test can still be mostly wrong." />

> **Figure 2.** *Why a positive test can still be mostly wrong.* Each
> dot is one of 100 cases. One case is genuinely positive. A test that
> is 95% accurate still raises several false alarms among the 99
> negatives, so most positive results are false. The mind tends to
> forget the base rate and trust the test.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, base rate neglect appears when one persuasive application or proposal eclipses what similar efforts have historically achieved. Restating the prior, and the reference class it belongs to, sets the vivid case back inside its real odds. The board can then fund with eyes open to how often this kind of bet pays off. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, base rate neglect undermines forecasts when a striking pitch or a single data point overrides the historical success rate of comparable ventures. A Bayesian read that weighs the new evidence against the reference class keeps the prior in view. Capital then flows on the full picture rather than on the most arresting story in the deck. |

**A4 - Conjunction Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The conjunction fallacy is the mind's error of judging a detailed,
specific scenario as more likely than a broader one that contains it. A
rich story feels more probable than a plain statement, even though added
detail can only narrow the odds. A board guesses that a major donor will
both increase giving and join the gala committee is likelier than simply
increasing giving, though the joint event can never outrank its part.
The bias bites whenever a plausible narrative dresses up a claim with
extra conditions.

**How it is calculated**

κ_A = w_ππ + w_ρρ + w_ιι + w_δδ − w_αα

The control forecasts each conjunct on its own and checks that P(A and
B) never exceeds P(A). The score rises as separate estimates replace the
seductive combined story.

κ_A is the strength of the statistical or probability control.

π is the explicit prior or base-rate distribution.

ρ is the reference-class coverage.

ι is the independent blind estimate indicator.

δ is the documented dissent or counterfactual challenge.

α is the anchor or streak pressure strength.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the conjunction fallacy surfaces when a detailed plan, with several happy conditions all holding at once, feels safer than a simpler version. Estimating each piece separately reveals that more moving parts mean lower combined odds. The board then plans around the real probability rather than the comfort of a complete-sounding story. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the conjunction fallacy inflates confidence in elaborate market scenarios that require many conditions to align. Forecasting each condition on its own, and confirming the joint case stays below any single part, restores honest odds. Strategy then rests on probabilities that the arithmetic actually supports. |

**A5 - Gambler's Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The gambler's fallacy is the mind's belief that independent random
events owe a correction after a run. A streak of one outcome feels like
it must be balanced by the other, though chance has no memory. After
three failed grant applications, a development lead feels the fourth is
overdue to land, as if past rejections improved the next roll. The bias
bites wherever genuinely independent trials are read as though they are
tied together.

**How it is calculated**

κ_A = w_ππ + w_ρρ + w_ιι + w_δδ − w_αα

The control requires stating the assumed dependence structure before any
reasoning about streaks begins. The score rises once independence is
made explicit and falls under unexamined pattern pressure.

κ_A is the strength of the statistical or probability control.

π is the explicit prior or base-rate distribution.

ρ is the reference-class coverage.

ι is the independent blind estimate indicator.

δ is the documented dissent or counterfactual challenge.

α is the anchor or streak pressure strength.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the gambler's fallacy can creep into fundraising and hiring when a string of setbacks breeds a sense that good fortune is now due. Naming whether the events are truly independent settles whether the streak carries any signal at all. The board then acts on the underlying odds rather than on a felt sense of cosmic balance. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the gambler's fallacy distorts risk judgments when a run of wins or losses is read as a trend that must reverse. Declaring the assumed dependence structure up front shows whether the sequence means anything. Capital and risk decisions then rest on the actual structure of the events rather than on a craving for the table to even out. |

**A6 - Hot-Hand Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The hot-hand fallacy is the mind's habit of reading a recent run of
success as proof that more success will follow, when the underlying
events may be independent. A streak feels like momentum, a force that
carries forward. After a manager closes four strong quarters, the board
treats a fifth as nearly assured, mistaking a likely coincidence for a
sustained edge. The bias bites when a short run of good outcomes is
taken as evidence of a lasting pattern.

**How it is calculated**

κ_A = w_ππ + w_ρρ + w_ιι + w_δδ − w_αα

The control is an independence audit, joined by a runs test when the
sample is large enough to support one. The score rises as the streak is
tested rather than assumed.

κ_A is the strength of the statistical or probability control.

π is the explicit prior or base-rate distribution.

ρ is the reference-class coverage.

ι is the independent blind estimate indicator.

δ is the documented dissent or counterfactual challenge.

α is the anchor or streak pressure strength.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the hot-hand fallacy appears when a recent run of successful campaigns or placements is read as durable skill rather than possible luck. An independence check, with a runs test where the numbers allow, tells whether the streak holds real signal. The board then bets on demonstrated reliability rather than on the warmth of a recent winning spell. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the hot-hand fallacy inflates faith in a team or a product line riding a recent streak, which can pull capital toward momentum that may not persist. Auditing for independence, and testing the run when the sample permits, distinguishes genuine edge from chance. Investment then follows proven performance rather than the glow of a hot quarter. |

**A7 - Zero-Risk Bias**

**What it measures**

Zero-risk bias is the mind's pull toward wiping out one small risk
entirely rather than making a larger cut in overall danger. The clean
certainty of zero feels worth more than a bigger reduction that leaves
some risk standing. A board spends heavily to erase a 1% hazard
completely while passing on a measure that would have lowered a 12%
hazard to 4%. The bias bites whenever the comfort of eliminating a
category outweighs the larger good of reducing total exposure.

**How it is calculated**

κ_A = w_ππ + w_ρρ + w_ιι + w_δδ − w_αα

The control is a cost-loss analysis that compares the size of the risk
reduction rather than the size of the risk left behind. The score rises
as reasoning shifts from remaining risk to risk removed.

κ_A is the strength of the statistical or probability control.

π is the explicit prior or base-rate distribution.

ρ is the reference-class coverage.

ι is the independent blind estimate indicator.

δ is the documented dissent or counterfactual challenge.

α is the anchor or streak pressure strength.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, zero-risk bias shows up when scarce funds are spent to fully close one small hazard while a larger one goes underaddressed. A cost-loss view that ranks options by how much total risk each removes redirects effort to where it does the most good. The board then protects the people it serves by the weight of the reduction rather than by the appeal of a tidy zero. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, zero-risk bias misallocates safety and compliance spending toward eliminating minor exposures while greater ones persist. Comparing options by the magnitude of risk reduced, not the residue that remains, keeps capital aimed at the largest gain. Resources then buy the most overall protection rather than the satisfaction of a single risk reaching zero. |

**B1 - Authority Bias**

**What it measures**

Authority bias is the mind's tendency to weigh a powerful person's
opinion far more heavily than the evidence behind it. A title or a
reputation can quietly stand in for proof. When a respected board chair
voices a view first, the rest of the room nods, and a quiet analyst who
saw a flaw in the projection lets it pass. The bias bites whenever
standing in the hierarchy substitutes for the strength of the argument.

**How it is calculated**

κ_B = w_ιι + w_δδ + w_σσ_s − w_γγ_social − w_GG

The control gathers blind estimates before any authority speaks and
tracks an influence Gini to see how concentrated the sway has become.
The score rises with independence and dissent, and falls as social
pressure and influence concentration grow.

κ_B is the strength of the reviewer-independence control.

ι is the independent or blind judgment indicator.

δ is the dissent or challenge record.

σ_s is the observed score spread before consensus.

γ_social is the authority or crowd pressure.

G is the influence Gini concentration.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, authority bias is worth watching when a founder or a senior funder speaks early and shapes the room before quieter voices weigh in. Collecting independent judgments first, and measuring how concentrated the influence is, lets the evidence stand apart from the title. The board then hears the dissent it needs rather than the deference it is used to. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, authority bias can let a charismatic executive's view override sound analysis in strategy and capital decisions. Blind estimates taken before that voice enters, paired with a read on how concentrated the influence has grown, keep evidence separate from rank. Decisions then answer to the argument rather than to the corner office. |

**B2 - Bandwagon Effect**

**What it measures**

The bandwagon effect is the mind's drift toward whatever the crowd
already believes, rather than toward the evidence. Agreement feels like
safety, so opinions cluster around the majority. As most of a committee
leans toward approving a partnership, a skeptical member quietly drops
the objection and joins the consensus. The bias bites whenever the
comfort of the group's direction starts to outweigh what the facts would
say on their own.

**How it is calculated**

κ_B = w_ιι + w_δδ + w_σσ_s − w_γγ_social − w_GG

The control uses blind aggregation and a counterfactual prompt that asks
whether a flip in the crowd would actually move the estimate. The score
rises with independent judgment and falls under crowd pressure.

κ_B is the strength of the reviewer-independence control.

ι is the independent or blind judgment indicator.

δ is the dissent or challenge record.

σ_s is the observed score spread before consensus.

γ_social is the authority or crowd pressure.

G is the influence Gini concentration.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the bandwagon effect appears when a forming majority pulls undecided members along before the evidence has been fully aired. Gathering views blindly, and asking whether a reversal in the crowd would change anyone's number, keeps judgment anchored to the facts. The board then decides on the merits rather than on the momentum of the room. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the bandwagon effect can sweep a team toward a popular strategy or a hot market simply because everyone seems to agree. Blind aggregation, with a check on whether a crowd flip would truly shift the estimate, separates conviction from conformity. Capital then follows the evidence rather than the consensus of the moment. |

**B3 - Dunning-Kruger Effect**

**What it measures**

The Dunning-Kruger effect is the pattern in which the least skilled
judges are often the most confident, because the same gaps that cause
errors also hide them. Competence and calibration can run in opposite
directions. A new evaluator, sure of every call, rates forecasts with
bold certainty, while a seasoned one hedges and turns out far better
calibrated. The bias bites when loud confidence is mistaken for real
accuracy.

**How it is calculated**

κ_B = w_ιι + w_δδ + w_σσ_s − w_γγ_social − w_GG

The control publishes Brier and calibration curves built over repeated
forecasts, so confidence can be checked against the record. The score
rises as independent, well-calibrated judgment is documented.

κ_B is the strength of the reviewer-independence control.

ι is the independent or blind judgment indicator.

δ is the dissent or challenge record.

σ_s is the observed score spread before consensus.

γ_social is the authority or crowd pressure.

G is the influence Gini concentration.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:2.91667in"
alt="Confidence against real skill." />

> **Figure 3.** *Confidence against real skill.* The least skilled tend
> to rate themselves well above their true ability, while the most
> skilled often rate themselves slightly below. The gap between the two
> curves is the blind spot. It is widest exactly where competence is
> lowest.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the Dunning-Kruger effect matters when a confident but untested voice carries weight it has not earned. Tracking each evaluator's calibration over many forecasts shows whose certainty is backed by accuracy. The board then leans on judgment that the record has proven rather than on the volume of someone's conviction. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the Dunning-Kruger effect can place the boldest, least-tested forecaster at the center of strategy. Publishing Brier and calibration curves over repeated calls reveals who is actually reliable. Decisions then rest on a measured track record rather than on displayed confidence. |

**B4 - Egocentric Bias**

**What it measures**

Egocentric bias is the mind's habit of placing its own perspective at
the center, so memory and judgment tilt toward one's own role and
experience. We recall our contributions more vividly than others' and
assume the world sees what we see. A director remembers steering a
successful program almost single-handedly, though colleagues recall a
far more shared effort. The bias bites whenever the operator's own
vantage point is mistaken for the whole picture.

**How it is calculated**

κ_B = w_ιι + w_δδ + w_σσ_s − w_γγ_social − w_GG

The control applies an outside-view prompt and reference-class
forecasting, pulling judgment away from the personal vantage point. The
score rises as the wider class of cases re-enters the reasoning.

κ_B is the strength of the reviewer-independence control.

ι is the independent or blind judgment indicator.

δ is the dissent or challenge record.

σ_s is the observed score spread before consensus.

γ_social is the authority or crowd pressure.

G is the influence Gini concentration.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, egocentric bias can color how leaders read their own organization's role in a shared success or a setback. An outside view, drawn from how comparable organizations have fared, widens the lens beyond one perspective. The board then judges the work as an observer would rather than as its most invested participant. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, egocentric bias can inflate a team's sense of its own decisive role in a market outcome. Reference-class forecasting, set against how similar firms performed, corrects for the inside view. Strategy then rests on the broader pattern rather than on a self-centered account of past wins. |

**B5 - False Consensus Effect**

**What it measures**

The false consensus effect is the mind's tendency to assume that others
share our views far more widely than they actually do. We read our own
opinion as the obvious one, so agreement feels like the default. A
program lead, certain a new policy is welcome, is startled when a quiet
survey shows the staff sharply divided. The bias bites whenever private
agreement is presumed without ever being measured.

**How it is calculated**

κ_B = w_ιι + w_δδ + w_σσ_s − w_γγ_social − w_GG

The control is a noise audit that measures how far judgments spread
before anyone claims consensus. The score rises as that real spread is
surfaced and weighed.

κ_B is the strength of the reviewer-independence control.

ι is the independent or blind judgment indicator.

δ is the dissent or challenge record.

σ_s is the observed score spread before consensus.

γ_social is the authority or crowd pressure.

G is the influence Gini concentration.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the false consensus effect can lead leaders to assume staff or community support that has never been tested. Measuring the spread of views before declaring agreement reveals whether the consensus is real. The board then acts on evidence of where people actually stand rather than on a hopeful assumption. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the false consensus effect can make a team believe customers or colleagues share its enthusiasm for a product direction. A noise audit that captures the true spread of opinion before consensus is claimed exposes hidden disagreement. Bets then rest on measured sentiment rather than on assumed agreement. |

**B6 - Fundamental Attribution Error**

**What it measures**

The fundamental attribution error is the mind's habit of blaming others'
failures on their character while excusing its own on circumstance. When
someone else stumbles we see a flaw in them, but when we stumble we see
a hard situation. A board judges a struggling grantee as poorly run, yet
explains its own missed targets by pointing to a difficult funding
climate. The bias bites whenever the same conduct is read by one
standard for others and another for ourselves.

**How it is calculated**

κ_B = w_ιι + w_δδ + w_σσ_s − w_γγ_social − w_GG

The control applies one symmetric attribution rule to self and others
alike, so the same evidence is read the same way. The score rises as
that even-handed standard is documented.

κ_B is the strength of the reviewer-independence control.

ι is the independent or blind judgment indicator.

δ is the dissent or challenge record.

σ_s is the observed score spread before consensus.

γ_social is the authority or crowd pressure.

G is the influence Gini concentration.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the fundamental attribution error can make a partner's setback look like incompetence while a similar internal setback looks like bad luck. Holding self and others to one attribution rule keeps the judgment fair. The board then evaluates performance evenly rather than through a lens that flatters its own side. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the fundamental attribution error can lead to harsh reads of a competitor's misstep and gentle reads of an identical internal one. Applying a single attribution standard across both keeps the analysis honest. Decisions about talent and strategy then rest on consistent reasoning rather than on a double standard. |

**B7 - Groupthink**

**What it measures**

Groupthink is the pattern in which a group prizes harmony so highly that
it quietly silences doubt and lets decision quality slip. The wish to
stay united makes disagreement feel like betrayal. In a tight-knit
board, a member who senses a flaw in the plan stays quiet rather than
break the warm mood, and the flaw sails through unexamined. The bias
bites whenever the cost of dissent feels higher than the cost of a bad
decision.

**How it is calculated**

κ_B = w_ιι + w_δδ + w_σσ_s − w_γγ_social − w_GG

The control combines independent blind aggregation with a measurement of
the coordination tax the group pays for its cohesion. The score rises
with documented dissent and falls as social pressure mounts.

κ_B is the strength of the reviewer-independence control.

ι is the independent or blind judgment indicator.

δ is the dissent or challenge record.

σ_s is the observed score spread before consensus.

γ_social is the authority or crowd pressure.

G is the influence Gini concentration.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, groupthink is a real danger in close, mission-driven boards where unity can quietly outrank scrutiny. Collecting judgments independently before discussion, and noticing what cohesion costs in candor, protects the space for dissent. The board then reaches decisions that have been tested rather than merely agreed to. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, groupthink can let a confident leadership team march toward a flawed strategy because no one wants to break ranks. Independent blind aggregation, with attention to the coordination tax of consensus, keeps disagreement alive long enough to matter. Major bets then survive real challenge before capital is committed. |

**B8 - Halo Effect**

**What it measures**

The halo effect is the mind's tendency to let one strong impression
spill over into unrelated judgments. A single admired trait colors
everything else about a person or a proposal. A candidate who interviews
with great warmth is assumed to be equally strong on budgets and
strategy, though nothing in the record speaks to either. The bias bites
whenever one shining quality quietly raises the score on dimensions it
has nothing to do with.

**How it is calculated**

κ_B = w_ιι + w_δδ + w_σσ_s − w_γγ_social − w_GG

The control decomposes the judgment into separate dimensions and rates
each before any of them are combined. The score rises as the parts are
assessed on their own merits.

κ_B is the strength of the reviewer-independence control.

ι is the independent or blind judgment indicator.

δ is the dissent or challenge record.

σ_s is the observed score spread before consensus.

γ_social is the authority or crowd pressure.

G is the influence Gini concentration.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the halo effect can let one impressive quality in a hire or a partner inflate confidence across areas no one has actually examined. Rating each dimension separately before combining keeps a single strength from carrying the rest. The board then judges each quality on its own evidence rather than on the glow of a first impression. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the halo effect can let a charismatic founder or a sleek product win marks on competence it has not demonstrated. Breaking the assessment into distinct dimensions, scored independently, contains the spillover. Decisions on talent and acquisitions then rest on each merit in turn rather than on one dazzling trait. |

**C1 - Commission Bias**

**What it measures**

Commission bias is the mind's pull toward doing something rather than
nothing, even when acting is the worse choice. Action feels responsible
and visible, while restraint can feel like neglect. Under pressure to
respond to a funding dip, a director launches a costly new campaign,
though holding steady and waiting one quarter would have served the
mission better. The bias bites whenever the urge to act crowds out the
honest option of doing nothing.

**How it is calculated**

ΔU = E\[U(option)\] − E\[U(status quo)\]

κ_C = w_EΔU + w_RR_rev + w_XX_alt − w_λλ_loss − w_sS_sunk

The control runs a pre-mortem with a quantified register that explicitly
includes the choice to do nothing. The score rises as reversibility and
a real alternative comparison enter the decision.

ΔU is the expected-utility difference.

E\[U(·)\] is the expected utility under the stated option.

R_rev is the reversibility score.

X_alt is the explicit alternative or inaction comparison.

λ_loss is the loss-aversion weight.

S_sunk is the sunk-cost salience.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, commission bias can drive expensive new initiatives when patience would have been wiser. A pre-mortem that puts the do-nothing option on the same quantified footing as action keeps restraint a genuine candidate. The board then chooses to act only when the numbers favor acting over waiting. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, commission bias can push teams into deals or product moves simply to be seen doing something. Weighing each option against the status quo, with reversibility and inaction priced in, keeps the bar where it belongs. Capital then moves only when action clearly beats standing still. |

**C2 - Endowment Effect**

**What it measures**

The endowment effect is the mind's habit of valuing something more once
we own it than we would if we did not. Possession adds a premium that
the market never agreed to. A board clings to an aging building, pricing
it far above what any buyer would pay, because letting go of what is
theirs feels like a loss. The bias bites whenever ownership inflates
worth beyond what the same item would command if it were merely on
offer.

**How it is calculated**

ΔU = E\[U(option)\] − E\[U(status quo)\]

κ_C = w_EΔU + w_RR_rev + w_XX_alt − w_λλ_loss − w_sS_sunk

The control is a substitution test that asks whether the item would be
acquired today, at that price, if it were not already owned. The score
rises as that fresh-eyes comparison enters the decision.

ΔU is the expected-utility difference.

E\[U(·)\] is the expected utility under the stated option.

R_rev is the reversibility score.

X_alt is the explicit alternative or inaction comparison.

λ_loss is the loss-aversion weight.

S_sunk is the sunk-cost salience.

w\_\* is the set of pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the endowment effect can keep an organization holding onto property or programs long past their usefulness, simply because they are already theirs. Asking whether they would acquire the same asset today at its real price cuts through that attachment. The board then weighs what to keep by present value rather than by the comfort of ownership. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the endowment effect can inflate the perceived worth of an existing product line or business unit and delay a needed sale. A substitution test, asking whether the firm would buy the asset today at that price, restores an outsider's view. Divestment decisions then rest on current value rather than on the premium that ownership quietly adds. |

**C3 - Loss Aversion**

**What it measures**

Loss aversion is the mind's habit of feeling a loss far more sharply
than a gain of the same size, so a threatened 100 dollars hurts roughly
twice as much as a possible 100 dollars pleases. It belongs to the
economic and outcome family of biases, where the pain of giving
something up quietly distorts what a choice is really worth. Picture a
director who refuses to close a beloved but failing program because
shutting it down feels like a wound, even though the same money would do
more good elsewhere. The bias bites hardest when a decision is framed as
surrendering something already held rather than as choosing the better
of two futures.

**How it is calculated**

ΔU = E\[U(option)\] − E\[U(status quo)\]; κ_C = w_EΔU + w_RR_rev +
w_XX_alt − w_λλ_loss − w_sS_sunk

The control surface here is gain framing paired with a reference-point
sensitivity check, which restates the choice from a fresh starting point
so the fear of loss stops doing the deciding. The reading is that a
record earns a higher control score when the genuine expected-utility
gap leads and the loss-aversion weight is held in check.

ΔU is the expected-utility difference between the option and the status
quo.

E\[U(·)\] is the expected utility under the stated option.

R_rev is the reversibility score.

X_alt is the explicit comparison against an alternative or inaction.

λ_loss is the loss-aversion weight.

S_sunk is the salience of sunk cost.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/bf3339794e894670922f39bc198aa729f85ded1f.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.02083in"
alt="The value function behind loss aversion." />

> **Figure 4.** *The value function behind loss aversion.* The curve
> bends more steeply below zero than above it. A loss and a gain of the
> same size do not weigh the same in the mind. The factor here is 2.25,
> the value Kahneman and Tversky estimated, which is why people cling to
> losing projects far longer than the numbers justify.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, loss aversion is worth naming whenever the choice is to wind down a service, change a staffing model, or let a long-held partnership lapse. The reference-point check asks the board to weigh the option as if starting fresh today, so a program is kept because it serves people well rather than because ending it feels like defeat. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, loss aversion shows up when exiting a product line, retiring an aging asset, or releasing capital tied to a past bet. Restating the decision from a clean starting point keeps the team from pouring money into a position only because walking away feels like admitting a loss. |

**C4 - Status Quo Bias**

**What it measures**

Status quo bias is the mind's pull toward leaving things as they are,
treating the current arrangement as the safe default even when a
different path would plainly serve better. It sits in the economic and
outcome family, where inertia masquerades as prudence. Imagine a board
that renews the same insurance broker for the eighth year running, never
quite asking whether a fresh search would save 30% on premiums, because
changing feels like effort and risk. The bias bites whenever doing
nothing is allowed to pass as a decision rather than being judged as one
option among others.

**How it is calculated**

ΔU = E\[U(option)\] − E\[U(status quo)\]; κ_C = w_EΔU + w_RR_rev +
w_XX_alt − w_λλ_loss − w_sS_sunk

The control surface is a change-of-reference-point pre-mortem that
treats staying put as an active choice with its own costs and risks. The
reading is that the record scores well when the status quo must defend
itself on the same evidence as every rival option.

ΔU is the expected-utility difference between the option and the status
quo.

E\[U(·)\] is the expected utility under the stated option.

R_rev is the reversibility score.

X_alt is the explicit comparison against an alternative or inaction.

λ_loss is the loss-aversion weight.

S_sunk is the salience of sunk cost.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, status quo bias matters when renewing vendors, keeping a familiar program design, or holding to a long-standing staffing pattern. Treating the current arrangement as an active choice forces the same scrutiny on staying put that any new proposal would face, so continuity is earned rather than assumed. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, status quo bias surfaces in budget cycles that simply repeat last year and in markets defended out of habit. A pre-mortem that puts the existing strategy on trial alongside the alternatives keeps capital from sitting where it sits only because no one asked it to move. |

**C5 - Sunk Cost Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The sunk cost fallacy is the mind's urge to keep spending on a course of
action in order to honor money or effort already gone, as if past
investment created a debt the future must repay. It belongs to the
economic and outcome family, where yesterday's spending wrongly steers
tomorrow's choice. Think of a board that pours another 200,000 dollars
into a software build that has already swallowed 800,000 dollars and two
years, chiefly because abandoning it would mean admitting the first
800,000 was lost. The bias bites most when the cost already incurred is
large and visible and the decision is framed as finishing what was
started.

**How it is calculated**

ΔU = E\[U(option)\] − E\[U(status quo)\]; κ_C = w_EΔU + w_RR_rev +
w_XX_alt − w_λλ_loss − w_sS_sunk

The control surface is a forward-only cost-benefit analysis that
deliberately excludes whatever has already been spent. The reading is
that the record scores well when the choice rests on future value alone
and the sunk-cost term is driven toward zero.

ΔU is the expected-utility difference between the option and the status
quo.

E\[U(·)\] is the expected utility under the stated option.

R_rev is the reversibility score.

X_alt is the explicit comparison against an alternative or inaction.

λ_loss is the loss-aversion weight.

S_sunk is the salience of sunk cost.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the sunk cost fallacy appears when a capital project, a grant-funded initiative, or a building renovation runs over budget and the instinct is to finish it because so much has already gone in. A forward-only review asks only what the remaining dollars will buy from here, which protects the mission from being held hostage by past spending. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, sunk cost shows up in failing acquisitions and overrun development programs that keep drawing capital to justify earlier rounds. Judging the next dollar purely on the return it can still earn keeps good money from chasing bad. |

**D1 - Confirmation Bias**

**What it measures**

Confirmation bias is the mind's tendency to seek and trust evidence that
fits what it already believes while quietly discounting whatever does
not. It belongs to the structural and narrative family, where the shape
of the search, not the strength of the truth, decides the answer.
Picture a director convinced a new hire is excellent who notices every
success and explains away every missed deadline, so the early verdict
hardens into certainty. The bias bites whenever a conclusion is reached
early and the later evidence is gathered to defend it rather than to
test it.

**How it is calculated**

κ_D = w_δδ_disconfirm + w_φ\|Δφ\| + w_hH_0 − w_νν_story

The control surface is a steelman of the opposing case paired with a
required quota of disconfirming evidence, so the search is forced to
look where it would rather not. The reading is that the record scores
well when genuine counter-evidence is present and the pull toward a tidy
story is held down.

κ_D is the structural and narrative control strength.

δ_disconfirm is the coverage of disconfirming evidence.

Δφ is the difference between equivalent frames.

H_0 is the null-model or baseline hypothesis.

ν_story is the pressure toward narrative closure.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, confirmation bias matters when evaluating a favored program, a trusted partner, or a strategy the leadership already loves. Requiring the team to bring the strongest evidence against its own preference turns a comfortable review into an honest one. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, confirmation bias distorts diligence on a deal the CEO wants and forecasts for a product the team is proud of. A standing demand for disconfirming evidence keeps the analysis from becoming a brief for a conclusion already chosen. |

**D2 - Framing Effect**

**What it measures**

The framing effect is the mind's habit of judging the very same facts
differently depending on how they are worded, so a result called a 90%
success rate feels better than the identical 10% failure rate. It
belongs to the structural and narrative family, where presentation
quietly overrides substance. Imagine a board that approves a reserve
policy described as protecting 9 of every 10 programs yet hesitates at
the same policy described as risking 1 in 10. The bias bites whenever a
single framing is allowed to stand unchallenged and no one restates the
choice in its opposite terms.

**How it is calculated**

κ_D = w_δδ_disconfirm + w_φ\|Δφ\| + w_hH_0 − w_νν_story

The control surface is a restatement of the decision in opposing frames
before any choice is made, which exposes how much of the preference came
from wording alone. The reading is that the record scores well when the
gap between equivalent frames is measured rather than ignored.

κ_D is the structural and narrative control strength.

δ_disconfirm is the coverage of disconfirming evidence.

Δφ is the difference between equivalent frames.

H_0 is the null-model or baseline hypothesis.

ν_story is the pressure toward narrative closure.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the framing effect matters when a budget cut is described as a saving or a fee increase as a sustainability measure. Asking for the same proposal stated in its plainest opposite terms lets the board react to the facts rather than to the wording. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, framing shapes how pricing changes and layoffs are presented for approval. Requiring each material decision to be read once in gain terms and once in loss terms keeps the vote anchored to substance. |

**D3 - Hindsight Bias**

**What it measures**

Hindsight bias is the mind's trick of believing, once an outcome is
known, that it was obvious all along, quietly rewriting how uncertain
things actually felt beforehand. It belongs to the structural and
narrative family, where memory bends to fit the ending. Picture a board
reviewing a grant that fell through and insisting the warning signs were
clear, when six months earlier every member had voted for it with
confidence. The bias bites in every post-mortem, where it punishes
reasonable past judgment and teaches false lessons about what could have
been foreseen.

**How it is calculated**

κ_D = w_δδ_disconfirm + w_φ\|Δφ\| + w_hH_0 − w_νν_story

The control surface is the capture of pre-registered forecasts written
down before outcomes land, so the record holds what was really believed
at the time. The reading is that the record scores well when a stated
baseline exists to check memory against and the urge to tidy the story
is restrained.

κ_D is the structural and narrative control strength.

δ_disconfirm is the coverage of disconfirming evidence.

Δφ is the difference between equivalent frames.

H_0 is the null-model or baseline hypothesis.

ν_story is the pressure toward narrative closure.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, hindsight bias matters most when reviewing a program that failed or a partnership that soured. Comparing the outcome against forecasts written before the result was known lets the board learn what was truly knowable, not what only looks obvious now. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, hindsight bias warps post-mortems on missed targets and failed launches, breeding unfair blame and false confidence. Recording forecasts in advance gives the review an honest yardstick for what the team could reasonably have seen. |

**D4 - Law of Triviality**

**What it measures**

The law of triviality is the mind's drift toward spending the most
attention on the matters easiest to grasp, so a small, familiar question
crowds out a large, hard one. It belongs to the structural and narrative
family, where comfort of understanding, not size of stake, sets the
agenda. The classic scene is a board that debates the color of the new
bike shed for 45 minutes and waves through a 4 million dollar reactor
budget in 5. The bias bites whenever the items everyone feels equipped
to discuss soak up the time that the consequential decisions deserve.

**How it is calculated**

κ_D = w_δδ_disconfirm + w_φ\|Δφ\| + w_hH_0 − w_νν_story

The control surface is a time budget set per option in proportion to its
value or strategic weight, so attention follows stakes rather than ease.
The reading is that the record scores well when discussion time is
allocated deliberately instead of by whatever feels approachable.

κ_D is the structural and narrative control strength.

δ_disconfirm is the coverage of disconfirming evidence.

Δφ is the difference between equivalent frames.

H_0 is the null-model or baseline hypothesis.

ν_story is the pressure toward narrative closure.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the law of triviality matters when an agenda lets office logistics or event details consume the hour reserved for the annual budget or a major program decision. Assigning meeting time in proportion to what is at stake keeps the big choices from being rushed. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias appears when a committee polishes wording in a policy while skimming the capital plan behind it. A time budget tied to the value of each item keeps the room's attention on the decisions that move the business. |

**D5 - Narrative Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The narrative fallacy is the mind's compulsion to wrap a clean
cause-and-effect story around events that may be little more than
chance. It belongs to the structural and narrative family, where a
satisfying tale feels truer than scattered data. Picture a board
crediting a charismatic new director for a 20% jump in donations, when
the rise tracked a one-time bequest and a strong market that had nothing
to do with leadership. The bias bites whenever a vivid explanation
arrives before anyone has checked whether plain randomness could account
for the same pattern.

**How it is calculated**

κ_D = w_δδ_disconfirm + w_φ\|Δφ\| + w_hH_0 − w_νν_story

The control surface is a null-model audit run before any story is told,
asking whether noise alone could have produced the pattern. The reading
is that the record scores well when a baseline hypothesis is tested and
the pressure toward a neat narrative is held down.

κ_D is the structural and narrative control strength.

δ_disconfirm is the coverage of disconfirming evidence.

Δφ is the difference between equivalent frames.

H_0 is the null-model or baseline hypothesis.

ν_story is the pressure toward narrative closure.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the narrative fallacy matters when a single good or bad quarter is explained by a tidy story about a person or a program. Asking first whether ordinary variation could account for the swing keeps strategy from being rebuilt around a coincidence. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias turns one strong quarter into a sweeping theory of why the strategy works. Testing the result against a null model first keeps capital from following a story that the data does not actually support. |

**D6 - Pro-Innovation Bias**

**What it measures**

Pro-innovation bias is the mind's habit of overvaluing whatever is new
and underweighting the evidence about how often new things disappoint.
It belongs to the structural and narrative family, where the glow of
novelty stands in for proof. Imagine a board that commits 500,000
dollars to an untested platform because it promises to transform service
delivery, while three similar promises in the sector quietly failed in
the past two years. The bias bites whenever the excitement of being
early outruns a sober look at the base rate of such projects.

**How it is calculated**

κ_D = w_δδ_disconfirm + w_φ\|Δφ\| + w_hH_0 − w_νν_story

The control surface is reference-class forecasting against the actual
record of past innovative projects, which replaces hope with history.
The reading is that the record scores well when an outside baseline
grounds the estimate and the seductive story of novelty is held in
check.

κ_D is the structural and narrative control strength.

δ_disconfirm is the coverage of disconfirming evidence.

Δφ is the difference between equivalent frames.

H_0 is the null-model or baseline hypothesis.

ν_story is the pressure toward narrative closure.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, pro-innovation bias matters when a new technology or program model promises to leap past old constraints. Checking the plan against how comparable innovations actually fared keeps scarce funds from chasing novelty for its own sake. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias drives over-investment in unproven products and platforms sold on transformation. Forecasting against the real track record of similar ventures keeps the capital decision honest about the odds. |

**E1 - Affect Heuristic**

**What it measures**

The affect heuristic is the mind's shortcut of letting a quick feeling
about something stand in for a careful judgment of it, so a warm glow or
a flash of dread decides the answer before analysis begins. It belongs
to the cognitive-noise and processing family, where emotion arrives
first and reasons are recruited after. Picture a director who approves a
partnership within minutes because the founder is likable, then
assembles the business case to match that first impression. The bias
bites most when a choice stirs strong feeling and the decision is made
in the heat of that moment.

**How it is calculated**

κ_E = w_RR_rubric + w_ττ_delay + w_ρρ_ref − w_αα_affect − w_μμ_memory

The control surface is a cool-down delay paired with a structured
rubric, so the strong first feeling has time to settle and the judgment
runs against fixed criteria. The reading is that the record scores well
when a rubric and a pause carry the decision and raw affect carries less
of it.

κ_E is the cognitive-processing control strength.

R_rubric is the completeness of the structured rubric.

τ_delay is the cooling-off interval.

ρ_ref is reference-class retrieval.

α_affect is the affective intensity.

μ_memory is recency or recall salience.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the affect heuristic matters when a compelling story or a charismatic applicant produces an instant yes or no. A short delay and a written rubric let the board test that gut reaction against the actual merits before committing. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias shapes snap reactions to a polished pitch or a frightening headline. A mandatory pause and a scoring rubric keep capital and hiring choices anchored to evidence rather than to the feeling of the moment. |

**E2 - Curse of Knowledge**

**What it measures**

The curse of knowledge is the mind's difficulty in imagining what it is
like not to know something it already knows, so an expert assumes
context that others simply lack. It belongs to the cognitive-noise and
processing family, where fluency in a subject hides the gaps in everyone
else's picture. Picture a finance director presenting a budget so
steeped in internal shorthand that the board approves it without
grasping where the real risk sits. The bias bites whenever a
knowledgeable person judges or explains a matter without checking what
the audience actually understands.

**How it is calculated**

κ_E = w_RR_rubric + w_ττ_delay + w_ρρ_ref − w_αα_affect − w_μμ_memory

The control surface is an explain-to-novice test that draws out the
context an expert was silently assuming. The reading is that the record
scores well when the explanation has been checked against a non-expert
and the unstated assumptions are made plain.

κ_E is the cognitive-processing control strength.

R_rubric is the completeness of the structured rubric.

τ_delay is the cooling-off interval.

ρ_ref is reference-class retrieval.

α_affect is the affective intensity.

μ_memory is recency or recall salience.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the curse of knowledge matters when staff present technical or financial material to a volunteer board that does not share their fluency. Asking the presenter to explain the decision as if to a newcomer reveals the assumptions the board needs to see before it votes. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias hides risk inside expert briefings that skip the context outsiders need. An explain-to-novice check forces the unspoken assumptions into the open before a major decision is made. |

**E3 - Empathy Gap**

**What it measures**

The empathy gap is the mind's poor reach across its own emotional
states, so a calm person cannot truly picture how they will feel under
stress, and a hungry one cannot picture being full. It belongs to the
cognitive-noise and processing family, where the present mood blinds us
to a future one. Picture a board that approves an austere cost-cutting
plan in a comfortable quarter, then watches staff morale collapse in the
lean months it failed to feel in advance. The bias bites whenever a
decision made in one emotional state will be lived out in a very
different one.

**How it is calculated**

κ_E = w_RR_rubric + w_ττ_delay + w_ρρ_ref − w_αα_affect − w_μμ_memory

The control surface is an affective-forecast pre-mortem joined to
reference-class memory, which asks how people will actually feel when
the plan meets reality. The reading is that the record scores well when
a remembered comparison grounds the forecast and present mood is kept
from doing the predicting.

κ_E is the cognitive-processing control strength.

R_rubric is the completeness of the structured rubric.

τ_delay is the cooling-off interval.

ρ_ref is reference-class retrieval.

α_affect is the affective intensity.

μ_memory is recency or recall salience.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the empathy gap matters when a plan made in good times will be carried out by staff and clients in hard ones. Forecasting how people will really feel under the new conditions, drawing on past cycles, keeps the decision humane as well as sound. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias distorts decisions about layoffs, on-call burdens, and customer changes that feel manageable on paper. A pre-mortem on the lived experience, anchored to how similar moves landed before, keeps the plan from breaking under its own strain. |

**E4 - Information Bias**

**What it measures**

Information bias is the mind's appetite for more data even when the
extra data cannot change what will be done, mistaking the comfort of
knowing for the value of knowing. It belongs to the cognitive-noise and
processing family, where gathering feels like progress while the
decision waits. Picture a board that delays a clear hire for three weeks
to commission a reference check that, whatever it returns, will not
alter the offer. The bias bites whenever a search for information is
launched without first asking whether any answer would shift the action.

**How it is calculated**

κ_E = w_RR_rubric + w_ττ_delay + w_ρρ_ref − w_αα_affect − w_μμ_memory

The control surface is a pre-action test that asks, before collecting a
datum, whether its answer could change the decision. The reading is that
the record scores well when each information request must justify itself
against the choice it is meant to inform.

κ_E is the cognitive-processing control strength.

R_rubric is the completeness of the structured rubric.

τ_delay is the cooling-off interval.

ρ_ref is reference-class retrieval.

α_affect is the affective intensity.

μ_memory is recency or recall salience.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, information bias matters when a decision stalls for studies or reports that will not change the choice. Asking in advance whether any finding could move the action keeps the organization from spending time and money to feel more certain about a settled call. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias breeds analysis paralysis, where another study delays a decision the data already supports. A simple test of whether the result could change the action keeps diligence from sliding into delay. |

**E5 - Planning Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The planning fallacy is the mind's habit of forecasting a project's time
and cost from its best imagined path, ignoring how long similar efforts
actually took. It belongs to the cognitive-noise and processing family,
where the inside view of a plan crowds out the outside record. Picture a
team that promises a new database in 6 months when every comparable
build in the sector ran closer to 14, because this time feels different.
The bias bites whenever an estimate is drawn from the specifics of the
plan rather than from the base rate of projects like it.

**How it is calculated**

κ_E = w_RR_rubric + w_ττ_delay + w_ρρ_ref − w_αα_affect − w_μμ_memory

The control surface is reference-class forecasting with uplift bands,
which sets the estimate against how comparable projects truly ran and
adds room for the usual overrun. The reading is that the record scores
well when an outside baseline shapes the forecast rather than the
optimism of the plan.

κ_E is the cognitive-processing control strength.

R_rubric is the completeness of the structured rubric.

τ_delay is the cooling-off interval.

ρ_ref is reference-class retrieval.

α_affect is the affective intensity.

μ_memory is recency or recall salience.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/89c01c71907e77bf491f8dd5648364ea24c5129e.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:2.82292in"
alt="Planned against actual duration." />

> **Figure 5.** *Planned against actual duration.* People estimate how
> long a task will take by imagining it going smoothly, so plans cluster
> below reality almost every time. The pattern holds even for those who
> have overrun on the very same kind of task before.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the planning fallacy matters when a grant timeline or a building project is promised faster and cheaper than experience warrants. Estimating from how similar projects actually unfolded, with a margin added, protects the organization from commitments it cannot keep. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias drives missed launch dates and blown budgets that erode credibility with investors. Building forecasts from the real record of comparable work, with realistic uplift, keeps commitments grounded. |

**E6 - Recency Bias**

**What it measures**

Recency bias is the mind's habit of giving the latest events far more
weight than older ones, as though what happened last week reveals more
than the years before it. It belongs to the cognitive-noise and
processing family, where the freshest memory shouts loudest. Picture a
board that overhauls its investment policy after one bad quarter,
forgetting the decade of steady returns that preceded it. The bias bites
whenever a recent stretch of good or bad results is treated as the whole
pattern rather than as one slice of a longer record.

**How it is calculated**

κ_E = w_RR_rubric + w_ττ_delay + w_ρρ_ref − w_αα_affect − w_μμ_memory

The control surface is time-balanced sampling that deliberately pulls in
older comparables alongside the recent ones. The reading is that the
record scores well when the evidence spans the full history and the
loudness of recent memory is held down.

κ_E is the cognitive-processing control strength.

R_rubric is the completeness of the structured rubric.

τ_delay is the cooling-off interval.

ρ_ref is reference-class retrieval.

α_affect is the affective intensity.

μ_memory is recency or recall salience.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, recency bias matters when one strong or weak season reshapes fundraising or program decisions. Deliberately setting recent results beside several earlier years keeps the board reacting to the trend rather than the last data point. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias overweights the latest quarter in forecasts and strategy resets. Sampling across a longer span of comparable periods keeps a single run of results from rewriting the plan. |

**F1 - Bias Blind Spot**

**What it measures**

The bias blind spot is the mind's curious blindness to its own
distortions while seeing them clearly in everyone else, so a person who
can name a colleague's bias believes their own judgment is clean. It
belongs to the meta-cognitive family, where the very faculty meant to
catch error exempts itself. Picture a director who confidently spots
favoritism in another department's hiring yet cannot see it in their
own, certain that their reasoning is purely objective. The bias bites
hardest in self-review, where confidence in one's own neutrality blocks
the correction that is most needed.

**How it is calculated**

κ_F = w_ss_self + w_dd_challenge + w_qq_evidence − w_bb_blindspot

The control surface is a third-party calibration audit that judges
decisions against externally visible scores rather than self-assessment.
The reading is that the record scores well when an outside check carries
weight and the untested belief that bias lives only in others is held
down.

κ_F is the self-audit control strength.

s_self is the explicit record of self-scrutiny.

d_challenge is the completeness of challenge and dissent.

q_evidence is the support from evidence quality.

b_blindspot is the untested assumption that bias is only external.

w\_\* are the pre-set governance weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the bias blind spot matters whenever the leadership reviews its own decisions and judges them sound. Inviting an outside party to score those choices against visible measures gives the board a mirror its own confidence cannot provide. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this bias lets a team certify its own diligence as objective while missing the slant inside it. An external calibration audit against measurable outcomes supplies the check that self-review alone never will. |

**Quant Entries (28)**

*The 28 entries here are the measuring instruments of forecasting. Where
the bias entries describe how judgment fails, these convert a judgment
into a number that another person can check. They run from the scoring
rules that grade a single forecast, through the calibration tools that
test a whole record, to the decision rules that turn a probability into
a choice.*

**Q1 - Brier Score**

**What it measures**

The Brier score measures how far a stated probability sat from the event
that actually happened, averaged across every forecast on the record. It
belongs to the family of proper scoring rules, which reward a forecaster
only for saying what the evidence truly supports. A director who tells
the board that a grant renewal is 70% likely collects a penalty of 0.09
when the grant arrives and 0.49 when it does not. Across a thousand such
judgments the average settles into one number between 0 and 1, where 0
marks a forecaster who was certain and right every time.

**How it is calculated**

BS = (1/N)∑\_{i=1}^{N}(p_i − o_i)^2

Reading: the Brier score is the mean of the squared gap between each
forecast probability and what actually happened, BS = mean((p - o)^2).

BS is the Brier score.

N is the number of forecasts.

p_i is the forecast probability for case i.

o_i ∈ {0,1} is the realized binary outcome.

∑ is the summation over all scored cases.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/b6d134d08926bcdcc1272aaa765a6e0133861df5.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.20833in"
alt="The penalty attached to one forecast." />

> **Figure 6.** *The penalty attached to one forecast.* The blue curve
> applies when the event occurs and the orange curve when it does not. A
> forecaster who says 95% and is right pays almost nothing, while one
> who says 95% and is wrong pays nearly a full unit. The curves meet at
> one half, the point of pure indecision.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/6aa949065b2b356105154ef825e2763c4febf050.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:4.16667in"
alt="A reliability diagram for 1,000 forecasts." />

> **Figure 7.** *A reliability diagram for 1,000 forecasts.* Each point
> gathers the forecasts in one band of stated probability and shows how
> often the event then occurred. The dashed line is perfect calibration.
> The bars beneath count how many forecasts fell in each band.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/9f80d2477399f1630d58069ad149449f56ccd165.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:2.71875in"
alt="The Brier score divided into its sources." />

> **Figure 8.** *The Brier score divided into its sources.* Reliability
> rewards calibration. Resolution credits a forecaster for telling
> likely from unlikely cases and so subtracts from the score.
> Uncertainty belongs to the question rather than the forecaster. The
> parts sum to 0.178.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the Brier score gives a fair record of how well the organization reads its own future across its revenue and fundraising calls. A development office that scores each major gift forecast against what donors actually did learns within one cycle whether its confidence has been earned. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the Brier score turns a year of capital and pricing forecasts into a single honest tally of how often stated odds matched reality. When every binary call is scored at the unit level, the leaders who quietly guessed well become visible next to those whose confidence ran ahead of their results. |

**Q2 - Brier Skill Score**

**What it measures**

The Brier skill score asks a sharper question than raw accuracy. It
measures how much better a forecaster did than a dull baseline that
simply predicts the long-run base rate every time. A score of 0 means
the forecaster matched that baseline and added nothing. A score
approaching 1 means the forecaster crushed it, while a negative score is
the quiet embarrassment of having done worse than a clock that is right
twice a day.

**How it is calculated**

BSS = 1 − BS_f/BS_ref

Reading: the skill score is 1 minus the ratio of the forecaster's Brier
score to the baseline's Brier score, BSS = 1 - BS_forecast /
BS_reference.

BSS is the Brier skill score.

BS_f is the Brier score for the focal forecast.

BS_ref is the Brier score for the reference or climatology baseline.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/0c4edd93f9fc11ec6a8c01c2bcbe3354dd75feb7.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:2.73958in"
alt="A skill score against the naive baseline." />

> **Figure 9.** *A skill score against the naive baseline.* The skill
> score rescales the Brier score so that 0 means no better than always
> predicting the long-run base rate and 1 means perfection. This
> forecaster reaches 0.29, a real but modest edge.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the Brier skill score lets you compare two program officers who forecast on very different terrain, because each is measured against the natural base rate of their own work. A grants team that beats the baseline by a wide margin is reading donors better than chance, not merely riding an easy year. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this score separates real forecasting skill from a market that was simply easy to call. When you compare analysts across product lines with different base rates, the skill score puts them on common ground and exposes anyone whose record was no better than guessing the average. |

**Q3 - Logarithmic Scoring Rule**

**What it measures**

The logarithmic scoring rule grades a forecast by the probability it
placed on the outcome that actually occurred, then punishes confident
wrongness without mercy. It is strictly proper, so a forecaster
minimizes the expected penalty only by reporting honest beliefs. The
cruelty is in the tail. A manager who declared an outcome 1% likely,
only to watch it happen, earns a penalty of roughly 4.6, while a humble
50% guess on the same event costs only 0.69.

**How it is calculated**

NLL = −(1/N)∑\_{i=1}^{N}ln p_i(y_i)

Reading: the score is the average negative natural log of the
probability each forecast assigned to the outcome that truly occurred,
NLL = -log(p_observed).

NLL is the negative log likelihood, also called the logarithmic score.

p_i(y_i) is the probability assigned to the realized outcome y_i.

ln is the natural logarithm.

N is the number of observations.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/339fee001b18b4c5d79f007987208ce6d503f09a.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.19792in"
alt="The logarithmic penalty." />

> **Figure 10.** *The logarithmic penalty.* The penalty is the negative
> logarithm of the probability the forecaster placed on what actually
> happened. A forecaster who assigns near zero to an event that occurs
> is punished without limit, which is why this rule is the harshest
> judge of false certainty.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the logarithmic score catches the dangerous habit of ruling something out entirely. A program leader who keeps insisting a funding source is dead certain, then is blindsided when it shifts, will see that overconfidence show up here as a heavy penalty that the Brier score would have softened. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this score is the right tool when a single overconfident call can sink real capital. It singles out the executive who assigns near-zero probability to a competitor move or a demand collapse that then arrives, the exact failure that a gentler average would forgive. |

**Q4 - Ignorance Score**

**What it measures**

The ignorance score is the logarithmic score read in bits, the language
of information itself. It measures how surprised the forecast should
have been by the outcome, where each bit is one doubling of that
surprise. If a forecaster put 25% on the event that occurred, the
ignorance score is 2 bits, the same surprise as guessing the result of
two fair coin flips. A perfectly confident and correct forecast scores 0
bits, the calm of having expected exactly what came.

**How it is calculated**

I = −log₂(p_y)

Reading: the score is the negative base-2 logarithm of the probability
placed on the observed outcome, IS = -log2(p_observed).

I is the ignorance score in bits.

p_y is the probability assigned to the observed outcome y.

log₂ is the base-2 logarithm.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/339fee001b18b4c5d79f007987208ce6d503f09a.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.19792in"
alt="The ignorance penalty in bits." />

> **Figure 11.** *The ignorance penalty in bits.* The ignorance score is
> the logarithmic rule read in base 2, so the penalty is measured in
> bits of surprise. The shape is identical. A confident wrong call
> carries an unbounded cost.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the ignorance score offers a plain reading of how much each forecast was caught off guard, counted in bits of surprise. A board that hears a program scored 3 bits on a key outcome understands at once that reality was eight times less likely than the team believed. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the bits-of-surprise reading travels cleanly across different kinds of forecasts, from churn to supply risk, without arguing over units.
