When a quarterly outcome lands at 4 bits of surprise, leadership has a unit-free signal that the team's model of the market was badly out of step with what happened. |

**Q5 - Continuous Ranked Probability Score**

**What it measures**

The continuous ranked probability score grades a forecast that names not
a single number but a whole distribution of possible values, such as
next year's revenue. It rewards a forecast for being both centered near
the truth and appropriately narrow, penalizing a vague wide guess and a
confident wrong one alike. Picture a finance lead who forecasts revenue
as a bell curve. The score measures the squared distance between that
predicted curve and the hard step of what the books finally reported, so
a tight forecast that lands on target scores near 0.

**How it is calculated**

CRPS(F,y) = ∫\_{−∞}^{∞}(F(x) − 𝟙{x ≥ y})² dx

Reading: the score integrates the squared gap between the forecast's
cumulative distribution and the step function that jumps at the realized
value, CRPS(F, y) = integral((F(x) - 1{x \>= y})^2 dx).

CRPS is the continuous ranked probability score.

F(x) is the forecast cumulative distribution.

y is the realized value.

𝟙{·} is the indicator function.

x is the integration variable.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/d086890caf664e3377b80ec2cb71867687e18266.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.16667in"
alt="CRPS as the area between two curves." />

> **Figure 12.** *CRPS as the area between two curves.* The smooth curve
> is the forecast distribution stated as a cumulative probability. The
> step rises at the value that actually occurred. The score is the
> shaded area between them, so a sharp forecast centered on the truth
> earns a small number.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this score is the honest way to grade a revenue or timeline forecast that was given as a range rather than a point. A finance office that scores its annual revenue distribution against the closed books learns whether its ranges were both well-aimed and the right width, not merely wide enough to be safe. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the continuous ranked probability score fits exactly the forecasts that drive capital plans, where a delivery date or a revenue number arrives as a spread of outcomes. It rewards the planning team that drew a tight, well-placed distribution over the one that hedged with a range so wide it could never be wrong. |

**Q6 - Bregman Divergence**

**What it measures**

The Bregman divergence is the parent shape from which many familiar
scoring rules descend, including the Brier score and log-loss. Choose a
convex generator function and you fix the exact way a forecast is
penalized for drifting from the truth. Its value to a careful analyst is
that it lets you design a proper score whose penalties bend the way a
real decision does. When the cost of a false alarm differs sharply from
the cost of a miss, the divergence can be tuned to honor that asymmetry
while still rewarding honest belief.

**How it is calculated**

D_φ(p,q) = φ(p) − φ(q) − ∇φ(q)^T(p − q)

Reading: the divergence is the gap between the generator at p and its
tangent-line estimate taken from q, D_phi(p, q) = phi(p) - phi(q) -
grad(phi(q)) dot (p - q).

D_φ is the Bregman divergence under convex generator φ.

p is the assessed distribution.

q is the reference distribution.

∇φ(q) is the gradient at q.

T is the transpose.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the Bregman divergence matters when the two ways of being wrong are not equally costly, such as missing a child at real risk versus a false flag. It gives a way to build a fair scoring rule that weights those errors the way the mission demands, while still rewarding forecasters for stating what they truly believe. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this divergence is the toolkit for designing a custom scoring rule when one error dwarfs the other, as in a fraud screen or a stock-out decision. Leadership can shape the penalty to match the real economics of being wrong without giving up the guarantee that honesty remains the best forecasting strategy. |

**Q7 - Quadratic Loss / MSE of Probability**

**What it measures**

The quadratic loss extends the Brier idea from a single yes-or-no event
to a forecast spread across several mutually exclusive classes. It
averages the squared error in the probability assigned to each class
against what actually occurred. Suppose a hiring panel forecasts that a
candidate is 60% likely to accept, 30% to decline, and 10% to vanish.
When the candidate accepts, the loss accumulates the small misses across
all three buckets into one number, where 0 marks a forecast that placed
all its weight on the truth.

**How it is calculated**

MSE = (1/NK)∑\_{i=1}^{N}∑\_{k=1}^{K}(p\_{ik} − o\_{ik})²

Reading: the loss is the mean squared gap between forecast probability
and observed indicator, taken over every class in every case, MSE =
mean((p_class - o_class)^2).

MSE is the mean squared probability error.

N is the number of cases.

K is the number of classes.

p\_{ik} is the probability for class k in case i.

o\_{ik} is the one-hot observed class indicator.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the quadratic loss gives one clean summary when an outcome has more than two doors, such as a client moving into housing, staying in shelter, or leaving the program. A case team that scores its multiclass forecasts this way gets a single honest number for how well it read paths that were never a simple yes or no. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this loss collapses a multiclass forecast into one probability-error figure, useful for segmentation calls or multi-way market outcomes. It tells leadership at a glance whether a team's spread of probabilities across several outcomes was well placed or merely scattered. |

**Q8 - Local Proper Scoring Rules**

**What it measures**

A local proper scoring rule grades a forecast using only the probability
it placed on the outcome that actually happened, ignoring how it spread
the remaining belief. This locality is a deliberate ethic. It says a
forecaster should be judged on the truth alone, not on bookkeeping over
events that never occurred. The logarithmic score is the famous member
of this family, and any rule chosen here must be fixed before the
outcome is known so that no one can tune the grading to flatter a result
already in hand.

**How it is calculated**

S(y,p) = g(p_y)

Reading: the score is a fixed function of the probability the forecast
assigned to the realized outcome, score = f(p_observed).

S is the local proper score.

y is the realized outcome.

p_y is the probability assigned to y.

g(·) is the locally proper scoring function selected before review.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, a local proper scoring rule keeps the grading honest by judging only the weight a forecaster put on what actually came to pass. Fixing the rule before outcomes are known protects the review from the human temptation to bend the standard once the result is already in view. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, a local proper rule suits settings where outcome-conditional honesty is the whole point, such as analyst forecasts that feed directly into compensation. By committing to the scoring function in advance, leadership removes any room to reshape the standard after the market has spoken. |

**Q9 - Expected Calibration Error**

**What it measures**

Expected calibration error asks whether stated confidence means what it
says. It sorts forecasts into bins by their stated probability, then
measures the gap between how often the team said something would happen
and how often it actually did, weighted by how many forecasts fall in
each bin. If everything a manager called 80% likely came true only 60%
of the time, that 20-point gap is the heart of the error. A
well-calibrated record drives the figure toward 0.

**How it is calculated**

ECE = ∑\_{b=1}^{B}(n_b/N)\|acc_b − conf_b\|

Reading: the error is the size-weighted average gap between empirical
accuracy and stated confidence across all bins, ECE = sum((n_b / N) \*
abs(acc_b - conf_b)).

ECE is the expected calibration error.

B is the number of populated bins.

n_b is the count of cases in bin b.

N is the total number of cases.

acc_b is the empirical accuracy in bin b.

conf_b is the mean stated confidence in bin b.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/fb71585096d3b94791b4d8c366c13e19dbd79109.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.42708in"
alt="Calibration error as a weighted average of gaps." />

> **Figure 13.** *Calibration error as a weighted average of gaps.* Each
> orange bar is the distance between a band's stated probability and how
> often the event then occurred. The expected calibration error averages
> those gaps, weighting each band by how many forecasts it holds.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, expected calibration error reveals whether the words confident and likely carry real weight in the organization's forecasts. Once a batch reaches roughly 20 entries, a board can see at a glance whether a team's 90% truly behaves like 90% or has quietly become wishful language. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this measure checks whether the confidence attached to revenue or risk forecasts is trustworthy enough to act on. A persistent gap between stated and realized rates warns leadership that the numbers feeding capital decisions are systematically too bold or too timid. |

**Q10 - Maximum Calibration Error**

**What it measures**

Maximum calibration error reports not the average miscalibration but the
single worst bin, the confidence level where the team is most badly
fooling itself. Where the expected version forgives a bad bin if most
others are fine, this one refuses to look away from the worst case.
Imagine a medical triage tool that is well calibrated everywhere except
at the 95% confidence band, where it is right only 70% of the time. That
25-point gap is exactly the failure this measure puts in front of you.

**How it is calculated**

MCE = max\_{b∈B}\|acc_b − conf_b\|

Reading: the error is the largest absolute gap between accuracy and
confidence found in any populated bin, MCE = max(abs(acc_b - conf_b)).

MCE is the maximum calibration error.

b is a populated calibration bin.

acc_b is the empirical accuracy in bin b.

conf_b is the mean confidence in bin b.

max is the largest absolute bin gap.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/fb71585096d3b94791b4d8c366c13e19dbd79109.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.42708in"
alt="The same gaps read at their worst." />

> **Figure 14.** *The same gaps read at their worst.* The maximum
> calibration error reports the single widest orange bar rather than the
> average. It guards against a forecaster who is calibrated on the whole
> but badly wrong in one band that may carry the costliest decisions.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, maximum calibration error is the right lens when one band of overconfidence could cause real harm, such as a risk screen that fails precisely where it claims near-certainty. It tells a board where the forecasts break worst, not merely how they do on average. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this measure belongs in safety-critical reviews where the worst-calibrated band, not the typical one, is what threatens people or capital. It points leadership straight to the confidence level where the model or the team is most dangerously wrong. |

**Q11 - Hosmer-Lemeshow + Extensions**

**What it measures**

The Hosmer-Lemeshow statistic tests whether a set of probability
forecasts fits reality across the whole range of confidence, not just on
average. It sorts forecasts into groups by predicted risk, then compares
the outcomes that were expected in each group against those observed,
summing the squared gaps into one statistic. A large value is the signal
that the forecasts and the world have parted ways somewhere. Extensions
carry the same idea into multinomial outcomes and into samples too small
for the classic decile version.

**How it is calculated**

χ²_HL = ∑\_{b=1}^{B}(O_b − E_b)²/V_b

Reading: the statistic sums the squared gap between observed and
expected outcomes in each group, scaled by that group's variance, HL =
sum((observed_b - expected_b)^2 / variance_b).

χ²_HL is the Hosmer-Lemeshow goodness-of-fit statistic.

O_b is the observed outcomes in bin b.

E_b is the expected outcomes in bin b.

V_b is the bin variance term.

B is the number of bins or groups.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/31b5d23a87787d066913cb8f9c0643f7aabc92a9.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.10417in"
alt="Observed against expected by decile." />

> **Figure 15.** *Observed against expected by decile.* Forecasts are
> sorted into ten groups. For each group the chart compares how many
> events the forecasts expected with how many actually occurred. A close
> match across all groups is the sign of a well-calibrated model.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, the Hosmer-Lemeshow test is a formal way to ask whether a risk model holds up across every level of predicted risk, given a large enough sample. A board reviewing a screening tool can use it to confirm the model fits not just typical cases but the high-risk and low-risk groups it must get right. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this test checks whether a scoring model, such as one for credit or churn, stays faithful across the full span of predicted risk. With a large sample, a high statistic warns leadership that the model misfits somewhere in the risk ladder before that drift turns into losses. |

**Q12 - Reliability-Resolution-Uncertainty Decomposition**

**What it measures**

This decomposition splits the Brier score into the parts that explain
why a forecaster did well or poorly. Reliability captures
miscalibration, the penalty for stated odds that did not match outcomes.
Resolution captures discrimination, the credit for separating events
that happened from those that did not. The third part is the irreducible
uncertainty of the outcome itself, which no forecaster can erase. Seeing
these apart tells you whether a bad score comes from confidence that
needs fixing or from a forecaster who cannot tell the cases apart.

**How it is calculated**

BS = REL − RES + UNC

Reading: the Brier score equals the reliability penalty minus the
resolution credit plus the base uncertainty, BS = REL - RES + UNC.

REL is the reliability or miscalibration penalty.

RES is the resolution or discrimination benefit.

UNC is the outcome uncertainty or base-rate variance.

BS is the Brier score.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this decomposition answers a practical question about a poor forecasting record. It shows whether the team needs calibration training, because its confidence is off, or sharper judgment, because it cannot yet tell likely outcomes from unlikely ones. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, breaking the Brier score into its parts turns a vague complaint about bad forecasts into a clear repair plan. Leadership learns whether to invest in calibration discipline or in the deeper analytical skill of telling winning bets from losing ones. |

**Q13 - Murphy Empirical-Var(y) Decomposition**

**What it measures**

This is the Murphy decomposition fitted to outcomes that are not simple
yes-or-no events but real-valued quantities. It replaces the base
uncertainty term with the empirical variance of the observed values
themselves, the natural spread that the data carried before any forecast
was made. For a team forecasting continuous figures like donation
totals, this matters because the binomial uncertainty of a coin-flip
world is far too narrow to describe how much those totals naturally
swing.

**How it is calculated**

UNC = Var(y) = (1/N)∑\_{i=1}^{N}(y_i − ȳ)²

Reading: the uncertainty term is the empirical variance of the observed
outcomes around their mean, UNC = empirical Var(y).

UNC is the empirical uncertainty.

Var(y) is the variance of outcomes.

y_i is the observed outcome or value.

ȳ is the sample mean.

N is the number of observations.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this version of the decomposition is the honest choice when outcomes are continuous, such as dollars raised or clients served, rather than plain yes-or-no events. It measures the natural spread of those real numbers so the board does not mistake ordinary variation for a forecasting failure. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the empirical variance term fits continuous business outcomes like revenue or volume, where binomial uncertainty would badly understate the real swing. It gives leadership a faithful baseline for how much the numbers move on their own before crediting or blaming the forecasters. |

**Q14 - Yates Diagnostics**

**What it measures**

Yates diagnostics open up a forecasting record into a few plain readings
of where it goes wrong. The first is calibration drift, the gap between
the average forecast and the actual base rate, which reveals a team that
runs habitually high or low. The others read the spread of the forecasts
and how that spread tracks real outcomes. Together they expose the
over-sharp forecaster who issues bold 90% and 10% calls when the
evidence only justified something far closer to the middle.

**How it is calculated**

Δ_cal = f̄ − ȳ; Var(f); Cov(f,y)

Reading: the procedure computes calibration drift as mean forecast minus
base rate, then the forecast variance and the forecast-outcome
covariance, Procedure: mean_forecast - base_rate, Var(f), Cov(f, y).

Δ_cal is the calibration-in-the-large drift.

f̄ is the mean forecast.

ȳ is the mean outcome or base rate.

Var(f) is the forecast variance.

Cov(f,y) is the forecast-outcome covariance.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, Yates diagnostics show whether a team's forecasts run high or low as a whole and whether their confidence outpaces what the outcomes support. A board can spot a development office that is consistently too optimistic, or one that issues sharp odds it has not earned. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, these diagnostics catch two common faults at once: a forecasting function that drifts above or below the true base rate, and one that is too sharp for its own evidence. Leadership gets an early reading on whether bold quarterly calls reflect real insight or just overconfident habit. |

**Q15 - Platt Scaling**

**What it measures**

Platt scaling takes the raw score a model spits out and bends it through
a single logistic curve so that the number it reports can be read as an
honest probability. It belongs to the family of calibration repair, the
small set of methods that fix a forecaster who ranks cases well but
states confidence poorly. Picture a loan model that loves to say 0.9 for
borrowers who actually default a third of the time. Platt scaling learns
two parameters from past cases and pulls that inflated 0.9 down toward
0.66, so a stated probability finally means what it says.

**How it is calculated**

p̂ = σ(As + B) = 1/(1 + e^{−(As+B)})

Reading: the calibrated probability is the sigmoid of A times the raw
score plus B.

p̂ is the calibrated probability.

σ is the logistic sigmoid.

s is the raw model score.

A and B are the two fitted calibration parameters.

e is the base of the natural logarithm, roughly 2.71828.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/015b2b51c3bc3532e9749af18a5e89fa7696dfa9.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:2.98958in"
alt="Platt scaling as a logistic map." />

> **Figure 16.** *Platt scaling as a logistic map.* Platt scaling fits a
> single logistic curve that turns a raw, uncalibrated score into a
> probability. It assumes the distortion is smooth, so it repairs gentle
> overconfidence with very few parameters.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use Platt scaling when a scoring model already ranks grant prospects or program risks sensibly but reports confidence that history does not support. It works best on a moderate set of past cases where the scores fall in a sensible order. The repaired number lets the board treat a stated 0.8 as a real 4-in-5 chance rather than wishful arithmetic. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership weighing capital allocation or pricing bets, Platt scaling turns a well-ordered but overconfident model into one whose probabilities can carry money. On a moderate calibration set the two fitted parameters quietly correct the inflation. A board can then read a 0.7 as a genuine 7-in-10 prospect instead of a salesman's round number. |

**Q16 - Isotonic Regression**

**What it measures**

Isotonic regression repairs a model's probabilities without assuming any
fixed shape for the correction, demanding only that higher raw scores
keep mapping to higher probabilities. It sits in the family of
calibration repair, and where Platt scaling forces a single smooth
S-curve, this method follows whatever staircase the data describe.
Imagine a hospital readmission model whose miscalibration is lumpy, fine
in the middle and badly off at the extremes. Isotonic regression carves
the scores into ordered steps and assigns each step the rate that
actually occurred, so the staircase it builds matches reality rather
than a tidy formula.

**How it is calculated**

p̂ = g(s), with g nondecreasing

Reading: the calibrated probability is a monotone fit applied to the raw
score.

p̂ is the calibrated probability.

s is the raw score.

g is the isotonic monotone calibration function fitted on validation
data.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/9aba161ba6cd293a4c615b71e489af8b0e78d2f9.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:2.97917in"
alt="Isotonic regression as a monotone staircase." />

> **Figure 17.** *Isotonic regression as a monotone staircase.* Isotonic
> regression fits the best non-decreasing step function to the raw
> scores. It makes no assumption about shape beyond order, so it can
> correct stranger distortions than a logistic curve, at the cost of
> needing more data.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director should reach for isotonic regression when there is a large pool of past cases and the gentle S-curve of simpler methods is too stiff to capture how a model really errs. The fitted staircase needs enough data to stand on, which is why volume matters. The payoff is a probability the board can trust even where the model used to drift worst. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership with a deep history of outcomes, isotonic regression corrects a forecast model flexibly when its errors do not follow any neat curve. It earns its keep on large calibration sets where a rigid sigmoid would leave money on the table. The board gains probabilities that hold up across the whole range, not just the comfortable middle. |

**Q17 - Beta Calibration**

**What it measures**

Beta calibration fixes a model's probabilities with three fitted
parameters, enough flexibility to bend an S-curve that leans harder on
one tail than the other. It belongs to the family of calibration repair,
and it answers a failure that simpler logistic correction cannot reach.
Picture a fraud model that is roughly honest about likely-clean
transactions but wildly overconfident about the rare suspicious ones.
Platt scaling, with its symmetric curve, leaves that one tail
uncorrected. Beta calibration adds the extra term that pulls the
stubborn end back into line.

**How it is calculated**

p̂ = σ(a ln r + b ln(1 − r) + c)

Reading: the calibrated probability is the sigmoid of a weighted blend
of the log of the raw score and the log of its complement, plus a
constant.

p̂ is the beta-calibrated probability.

r is the raw probability score.

a, b, c are the three fitted parameters.

ln is the natural logarithm.

σ is the logistic sigmoid.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/6d8ba9b04aa468d6ea8ae3da47403c0816501065.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:2.9375in"
alt="The beta family of calibration curves." />

> **Figure 18.** *The beta family of calibration curves.* Beta
> calibration extends the logistic map with a second shape parameter, so
> a single family can pull overconfident probabilities down or push
> underconfident ones up. The grey diagonal is the curve that leaves a
> forecast unchanged.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director should use beta calibration when a simpler logistic repair leaves one end of the probability range still misbehaving, often the high-stakes tail of rare outcomes. The third parameter buys the asymmetry that a plain sigmoid cannot express. The board then gets honest numbers even on the unusual cases that matter most. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, beta calibration is the right tool when Platt scaling corrects most of a forecast but misses one probability tail, the very region where a pricing or risk decision can go badly wrong. Its three parameters let the curve lean where the data lean. The board reads confidence that holds at the extremes as well as the middle. |

**Q18 - Spline / Bayesian Calibration**

**What it measures**

Spline and Bayesian calibration repair a model's probabilities with a
smooth curve, either flexed freely by spline knots or steadied by a
prior when data are thin. Both live in the family of calibration repair,
and both shine when a hard staircase would overfit. Imagine a small
charity with only 80 past grant outcomes trying to calibrate a risk
score. A raw isotonic fit would chase noise. A spline draws a gentle
line through the points, while a Bayesian version leans on a prior
calibration curve from a sister program to keep the estimate sane.

**How it is calculated**

p̂ = g_spline(s) or p(θ\|D) ∝ p(D\|θ)p(θ)

Reading: the calibrated probability is either a smooth spline of the raw
score or a posterior over calibration parameters given the data.

p̂ is the calibrated probability.

g_spline is the smooth calibration curve.

θ is the calibration parameters.

D is the calibration data.

∝ means proportional to.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director should choose spline or Bayesian calibration for small-sample repair, especially when a related program offers a usable prior curve to borrow from. The smoothness keeps the fit from chasing a handful of lucky or unlucky cases. The board gets a stable probability even when the record is short. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership facing a new product or market with few past outcomes, spline or Bayesian calibration repairs the forecast without overreacting to scarce data. A prior drawn from an adjacent line of business can anchor the curve where the evidence runs out. The board reads probabilities that bend with the data yet refuse to lurch on noise. |

**Q19 - Cost-Loss Rule**

**What it measures**

The cost-loss rule sets the probability at which acting to prevent a
harm becomes worth its price, by weighing the cost of action against the
loss suffered if the harm strikes unmitigated. It belongs to the family
of decision utility, where a forecast is finally turned into a choice.
Suppose protective maintenance costs 2,000 dollars and a failure left
unaddressed costs 50,000. The threshold falls to 0.04, meaning a failure
probability above 4% already justifies the spend. Below that line the
cheaper gamble is to wait.

**How it is calculated**

p\* = C/L

Reading: the action threshold equals the cost of acting divided by the
loss if the event occurs.

p\* is the action threshold.

C is the cost of mitigation or action.

L is the loss if the adverse event occurs unmitigated.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/8387949be10da2bdbd35dfeeb3544e4ea911e4be.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.125in"
alt="The economic value of a forecast across decisions." />

> **Figure 19.** *The economic value of a forecast across decisions.* A
> forecast is worth more to some decisions than others. The curve traces
> its value as the ratio of the cost of acting to the loss of being
> caught unprepared. Value is highest when that ratio sits near the
> event's base rate.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use the cost-loss rule whenever a preventive action carries a known price and the avoided harm carries a known cost, such as repairing a roof before a feared leak. The ratio names the exact probability above which acting beats waiting. The board sees the spending decision reduced to one honest threshold. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, the cost-loss rule turns a risk forecast into a clear spending line whenever the cost of acting and the loss from inaction are both visible. A recall, a hedge, or a maintenance program each has its own threshold. The board can then judge whether the stated failure probability clears the bar that justifies the outlay. |

**Q20 - Reference-Class Blending / Outside-View**

**What it measures**

Reference-class blending tempers a case-specific estimate by mixing it
with the base rate from comparable situations that came before. It
belongs to the family of decision utility, and it exists to curb the
optimism that grips us when we stare only at the project in front of us.
A team swears its new clinic will open in nine months because their plan
looks airtight. The outside view notes that the last dozen comparable
clinics averaged 15 months. Blending the two pulls the forecast toward
the sober record instead of the hopeful plan.

**How it is calculated**

p_adj = αp_inside + (1 − α)p_ref

Reading: the adjusted probability is the inside-view estimate weighted
by alpha plus the reference-class estimate weighted by the remainder.

p_adj is the adjusted probability.

α ∈ \[0,1\] is the weight placed on the inside view.

p_inside is the case-specific estimate.

p_ref is the reference-class or base-rate probability.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director should blend in the outside view on any planning forecast backed by at least five comparable past cases, where the temptation to trust this time is different runs strong. The base rate of similar efforts anchors the estimate to lived history. The board gets a timeline or success rate that has already met reality once. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, reference-class blending guards expansion and launch forecasts against inside-view optimism by folding in how comparable bets actually played out. Five or more analogous cases give the base rate enough weight to matter. The board reads a number disciplined by the company's own track record rather than the latest pitch. |

**Q21 - Posterior Predictive p-values**

**What it measures**

A posterior predictive p-value asks how often a model that you believe
would generate data as extreme as the data you actually saw. It belongs
to the family of decision utility as a check on whether a fitted model
deserves trust. Imagine a donation model that predicts smooth monthly
giving, yet the real year held two wild spikes. You simulate many years
from the model and count how many show spikes that large. If almost none
do, the small p-value warns that the model is missing something real.

**How it is calculated**

p_ppc = Pr\[T(y_rep) ≥ T(y_obs) \| y_obs\]

Reading: the value is the probability that a replicated statistic is at
least as extreme as the observed statistic, given the observed data.

p_ppc is the posterior predictive p-value.

T(·) is the discrepancy statistic.

y_rep is the replicated outcome drawn under the posterior model.

y_obs is the observed outcome.

Pr is probability.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use a posterior predictive p-value to test whether a calibration repair or a fitted model truly matches the program's history before leaning on it. A value near 0 or 1 signals that the model fails to reproduce what actually happened. The board learns whether the tool is sound before betting decisions on it. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, the posterior predictive p-value validates whether a forecasting model can recreate the patterns already seen in the business. An extreme value flags a model that quietly omits a real driver of revenue or risk. The board gains a warning light before a flawed model shapes capital choices. |

**Q22 - Zone Classification**

**What it measures**

Zone classification reads a system's recent trajectory and sorts its
current state into a named regime, from stable through transition to
critical. It belongs to the family of dynamics and anomaly detection,
and it exists to catch the moment when a small nudge could trigger an
outsized swing. Think of a reservoir whose water quality has drifted for
months. A classifier watching the trend can label it as still stable or
as entering a transition where one hot week tips it over. That label
tells operators when caution must replace routine.

**How it is calculated**

z_t = f(θ_t,x_t) ∈ {stable, transition, critical}

Reading: the zone at time t is a pre-specified classifier applied to the
parameter trajectory and the current state.

z_t is the zone label at time t.

θ_t is the parameter trajectory.

x_t is the observed system state.

f is the pre-specified classifier.

∈ means membership in the set of possible states.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use zone classification to watch a program or system whose small shifts sometimes cause large swings, such as a service whose demand can suddenly overwhelm capacity. The label marks when the system has left its calm regime. The board gets early notice to act before a transition becomes a crisis. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, zone classification flags when an operation or market sits near a state where minor changes can produce major output shifts. A move from stable to transition is a signal to tighten oversight. The board reads a clear regime label rather than a wall of raw telemetry. |

**Q23 - Tipping Points + AR(1) Early-Warning**

**What it measures**

This early-warning method watches a time series for two quiet symptoms
that often precede a regime shift, rising correlation from one moment to
the next and a swelling variance. It belongs to the family of dynamics
and anomaly detection. As a system loses its resilience it recovers more
slowly from each small jolt, so today's value clings closer to
yesterday's and the swings grow wider. Picture a lake easing toward
sudden algal collapse. Months ahead, its weekly readings start tracking
each other more tightly and bouncing more violently, a warning visible
before the water turns.

**How it is calculated**

φ₁ = corr(x_t,x\_{t−1}); flag if Δφ₁ \> 0 and ΔVar(x) \> 0

Reading: estimate the lag-one autocorrelation as the correlation between
each value and the one before it, then raise a flag when both that
autocorrelation and the variance are climbing.

φ₁ is the lag-one autocorrelation.

x_t is the value at time t.

Δ is the change over the monitoring window.

Var(x) is the variance.

corr is the correlation.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/619bcfa8165f5fc2c553cb8220b0abe81a1f57a5.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.35417in"
alt="An early-warning signal before a tipping point." />

> **Figure 20.** *An early-warning signal before a tipping point.* As a
> system loses resilience it recovers from small shocks more slowly, so
> each value resembles the one before it more closely. The lower panel
> tracks that lag-1 autocorrelation. Its climb begins before the visible
> shift at the dashed line.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use this early-warning signal on any system with a long run of historical readings, watching for the twin rise in autocorrelation and variance that hints at an approaching break. Both must climb together for the flag to mean much. The board gains lead time before a slow drift becomes an abrupt shift. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, the AR(1) early-warning watches a monitored time series for the loss of resilience that precedes a sharp turn in a market or operation. A joint rise in lag-one correlation and variance is the tell. The board reads a forecast of fragility while there is still room to respond. |

**Q24 - Influence Gini**

**What it measures**

The influence Gini measures how unevenly weight is spread across the
people in a group review, running from 0 when everyone counts equally to
near 1 when one voice dominates. It belongs to the family of dynamics
and anomaly detection, here applied to the politics of a panel rather
than a physical system. Picture a grant committee of eight where one
senior figure quietly drives every verdict. A Gini of 0.6 exposes that
lopsided authority, where a value near 0.1 would show a panel that truly
shares the floor. The number turns a vague sense of capture into
something the board can see.

**How it is calculated**

G = \[∑\_{i=1}^{n}∑\_{j=1}^{n}\|w_i − w_j\|\] / \[2n∑\_{i=1}^{n}w_i\]

Reading: the coefficient is the average absolute difference between
every pair of influence weights, scaled by twice the number of reviewers
times the total weight.

G is the influence Gini coefficient.

w_i and w_j are the influence weights of two reviewers.

n is the number of reviewers.

\|·\| is the absolute value.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/603832f5ca0344953349e469d2d833288431ca60.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.125in"
alt="How concentrated influence is in a review body." />

> **Figure 21.** *How concentrated influence is in a review body.*
> Reviewers are ranked from least to most influential along the
> horizontal axis. The curve shows the share of total influence they
> hold. A straight diagonal would mean every reviewer counts equally.
> The bowed curve here means a few voices carry most of the weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use the influence Gini to audit whether a review committee genuinely deliberates or quietly bends to one authority or a bandwagon. A high value reveals that a few voices carry most of the weight. The board learns whether its panels need rebalancing before their verdicts harden into policy. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, the influence Gini checks whether an investment or hiring committee shares judgment or defers to a dominant figure. A coefficient drifting toward 1 warns of authority capture that can steer capital badly. The board gets a concrete reading of how evenly decision power is actually distributed. |

**Q25 - Bias Cascade vs Snowball**

**What it measures**

This pair of measures separates two ways a bias spreads, a cascade that
jumps from one actor to many and a snowball that compounds inside a
single actor over time. It belongs to the family of dynamics and anomaly
detection. The distinction matters because the cures differ. Suppose an
early reviewer's harsh framing infects six colleagues who each repeat
it. That is a cascade across people. Now suppose one analyst's first
error nudges every later judgment of his own in the same direction. That
is a snowball within one mind, and rotating reviewers will not touch it.

**How it is calculated**

κ_cascade = \|E_adoption\|; ℓ_snowball = max path length

Reading: count the cascade as the number of cross-actor adoption edges,
and measure the snowball as the longest within-actor amplification
chain.

κ_cascade is the cross-actor adoption count.

E_adoption is the set of directed adoption edges.

ℓ_snowball is the longest within-actor amplification chain.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use this distinction when deciding whether to rotate reviewers or to recalibrate an individual. A wide cascade across people argues for fresh panels. A long snowball inside one reviewer argues for coaching that one person. The board chooses the remedy that fits the actual spread. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, separating cascade from snowball clarifies whether a bias problem lives between people or within one decision-maker. A high cross-actor count points to rotation, while a long within-actor chain points to individual recalibration. The board directs the fix where the contagion actually runs. |

**Q26 - Coordination Tax**

**What it measures**

The coordination tax measures how much individual judgment must be
flattened to reach a single agreed number, taken as the average distance
between each person's estimate and the consensus. It belongs to the
family of dynamics and anomaly detection, and it treats disagreement as
information rather than noise. Imagine five analysts forecasting demand
at values ranging from 60 to 140, settling on a consensus of 100. The
average gap is 24 units. That tax is the silent cost of agreement, the
spread of honest views that the single headline number quietly erased.

**How it is calculated**

τ_coord = (1/N)∑\_{i=1}^{N}\|x_i − c\|

Reading: the tax is the average absolute distance between each
individual estimate and the consensus estimate.

τ_coord is the coordination tax.

x_i is an individual estimate.

c is the consensus estimate.

N is the number of reviewers or estimates.

\|·\| is the absolute compression required.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use the coordination tax in group decisions where wide disagreement may itself be the signal worth heeding. A large tax means the consensus hides real division rather than reflecting genuine accord. The board learns when a tidy single number deserves a second look. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, the coordination tax reveals how much dissent was compressed to manufacture a unified forecast for a market or product call. A high value warns that the headline figure masks a divided room. The board can then probe the disagreement before committing capital on a false calm. |

**Q27 - Copula-Based Dependence**

**What it measures**

Copula-based dependence captures how variables move together beyond the
straight-line link that Pearson correlation can see, including the way
some pairs cling hardest in their extreme tails. It belongs to the
family of dependence and uncertainty. Two assets may look mildly
correlated in calm markets yet crash in lockstep during a panic. A
copula isolates that tail dependence, the probability they fail together
precisely when it hurts most. Picture two funding streams that seem
independent until a recession dries up both at once, a joint collapse a
single correlation number would have missed.

**How it is calculated**

ρ_s, τ_K, λ_L, λ_U, C(u,v)

Reading: summarize dependence through rank correlations and tail
measures, or through a fitted copula or vine that joins the marginal
distributions.

ρ_s is the Spearman rank correlation.

τ_K is the Kendall rank correlation.

λ_L is the lower tail dependence and λ_U is the upper tail dependence.

C(u,v) is the copula joining the marginal distributions.

> **Figure 22.** *Dependence shown on the rank scale.* A copula strips
> away each forecast's own distribution and shows only how the two move
> together. When points crowd the diagonal, the forecasts tend to fail
> at the same time, which is exactly the hidden link that turns separate
> risks into one large one.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use copula-based dependence to model the risk that several funding sources or programs fail together, where a plain correlation would understate the danger. Tail dependence shows whether they collapse in unison just when reserves run thin. The board sees joint-failure risk that Gaussian assumptions tend to hide. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, copula methods expose how products and counterparties move together in the tails, where a normal-distribution model dangerously assumes calm. The tail-dependence figures reveal who sinks alongside whom in a crisis. The board gains a clearer picture of joint failure before it concentrates exposure. |

**Q28 - Conformal Prediction**

**What it measures**

Conformal prediction builds a forecast interval that comes with a
coverage guarantee, promising to contain the true outcome at least a
stated share of the time without trusting any particular distribution.
It belongs to the family of dependence and uncertainty. The method
studies how large the model's past errors were on held-out cases and
widens its intervals accordingly. A demand model might report not just
1,000 units but a band of 850 to 1,150 that will hold the real figure in
90% of cases. That honest band is worth more to a planner than a single
confident point.

**How it is calculated**

Pr{Y ∈ C\_{1−α}(X)} ≥ 1 − α

Reading: the probability that the true outcome falls inside the
conformal set is at least 1 minus the allowed error rate.

Y is the future outcome.

X is the predictors or context.

C\_{1−α}(X) is the conformal prediction set.

α is the allowed error rate.

Pr is the marginal coverage probability.

<img
src="/Users/gordonlaabs/Developer/Clarvoy/stable-dev/reports/_ingested/clarvoy_bias_control_reference_catalog_pcc_forprofit/media/media/5b806d2c074a77032ce66d38241575c88dd491ab.png"
title="Figure" style="width:5.625in;height:3.10417in"
alt="A prediction band with a guaranteed coverage rate." />

> **Figure 23.** *A prediction band with a guaranteed coverage rate.*
> Conformal prediction turns any model into one that outputs an interval
> rather than a point. The band is built so that, over many cases, the
> true outcome lands inside it at the promised rate, here about 95%,
> without assuming the data follow any particular distribution.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | A nonprofit director can use conformal prediction when a forecast needs an honest uncertainty band and the data refuse to fit a clean parametric model. The guaranteed coverage means the interval will hold the truth about as often as it claims. The board plans against a realistic range rather than a single fragile estimate. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership, conformal prediction supplies distribution-free intervals for revenue or demand forecasts that arrive with a coverage promise. The band is built from real past errors rather than convenient assumptions. The board can size inventory or capital against a range it can actually trust. |

**Combo Records (15)**

*Biases rarely arrive alone. These 15 records describe the combinations
that do the most damage, where one distortion feeds another until a
group talks itself into a decision no member would have made alone. Each
record names the pattern and the control that interrupts it.*

**C1 - Planning Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The planning fallacy is the mind's habit of estimating a project from
the inside, by imagining the smooth path from here to done, while
forgetting how every similar project actually unfolded. A program
director maps out a new shelter expansion and sees 8 months of clean
work, because she is picturing her own plan rather than the dozen
comparable builds that each ran past a year. The correction is to weigh
that hopeful inside estimate against the reference-class rate of how
such efforts really went.

**How it is calculated**

p_adj = αp_inside + (1 − α)p_ref

The adjusted forecast blends the focal plan with the historical base
rate, so a low weight on the inside view pulls the number toward what
actually happened to similar efforts.

p_adj is the reference-adjusted forecast.

α is the inside-view weight.

p_inside is the focal plan estimate.

p_ref is the reference-class rate.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board weighing a capital build or a fundraising commitment before they commit, this pairing replaces a single optimistic timeline with a number anchored in how past projects of the same kind actually ran. It tells the board whether the plan in front of them has been checked against history or only against hope. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board deciding on a product launch or a major investment before commit, the reference-class adjustment exposes the gap between the team's inside estimate and the record of comparable bets. It gives the board one disciplined number to challenge instead of an internal forecast that no outside experience has tested. |

**C2 - Overconfidence / Dunning-Kruger**

**What it measures**

Overconfidence is the mind stating more certainty than its track record
can support, and the Dunning-Kruger pattern is the version where the
least skilled are the surest. An analyst who says he is 90% sure of nine
straight calls, then gets only six right, is living inside this gap
between felt confidence and measured accuracy. Read after outcomes are
known, the bias shows up as a persistent distance between how confident
the forecaster sounded and how often events actually broke the predicted
way.

**How it is calculated**

BS = (1/N)∑(p_i − o_i)²; ECE = ∑(n_b/N)\|acc_b − conf_b\|

The Brier score is the average squared miss across all forecasts, and
the expected calibration error groups forecasts into confidence bins and
measures how far stated confidence drifts from observed accuracy in
each.

BS is the Brier score.

ECE is the expected calibration error.

p_i is the forecast probability.

o_i is the outcome.

n_b is the count in a bin.

acc_b is the accuracy within a bin, and conf_b is the stated confidence
within that bin.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board reviewing decisions after the outcome is in, these two numbers turn a vague sense of how well the team forecasts into a measured record. They tell the board, within a single cycle, whether the confidence voiced in the room was earned or merely felt. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board looking back on strategy or investment calls, the Brier score and calibration error reveal whether the executives who sounded most certain were also most right. They give the board a way to price the reliability of its own forecasters rather than trusting the loudest voice. |

**C3 - Noisy expert override**

**What it measures**

A noisy expert override is the moment a person sets aside a sound rule
or model and substitutes a hunch that leaves no durable reason behind
it. A senior underwriter overrules the pricing model on a gut feeling
about one client, and a year later no one can reconstruct why the
exception was made or whether it paid off. The cost-loss rule offers a
fixed line for when action is worth taking, so an override can be
checked against a threshold rather than a mood.

**How it is calculated**

p\* = C/L

The action threshold is the cost of acting divided by the loss avoided,
so any forecast probability above that line justifies the mitigation and
any below it does not.

p\* is the action threshold.

C is the cost of action or mitigation.

L is the loss if the event is not mitigated.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board facing a partnership risk or a capacity commitment before they commit, the cost-loss threshold sets a clear bar for when caution is worth its price. When a staff expert wants to override the rule, the board can ask what changed in the cost or the loss rather than deferring to seniority. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board weighing an operational risk before commit, the threshold makes the trade between the price of acting and the price of inaction explicit. An executive who overrides it must then justify the move against a stated number, which keeps a single confident voice from quietly steering capital. |

**C4 - Authority / Bandwagon / Groupthink**

**What it measures**

This cluster names the way a room's judgment collapses toward one
source, whether a senior voice, a growing majority, or the simple wish
to agree. A grant panel hears the executive director speak first, and
the four reviewers who follow shade their scores toward hers without
quite noticing they have stopped thinking independently. The influence
Gini measures how unequally weight was spread across reviewers, so a
number near 1 reveals that one person effectively decided what looked
like a panel.

**How it is calculated**

G = \[∑∑\|w_i − w_j\|\]/\[2n∑w_i\]

The influence Gini sums the differences between every pair of reviewer
weights and scales them, returning 0 when influence is perfectly even
and approaching 1 when it concentrates in one person.

G is the influence Gini.

w_i and w_j are reviewer influence weights.

n is the number of reviewers.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board designing a blind review panel, the influence Gini shows whether a decision that looked collective was actually carried by one dominant voice. It tells the board when to restructure a panel so that authority and quiet conformity stop standing in for evidence. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board setting up a review of an investment or a strategy, this measure reveals how evenly judgment was distributed before the vote. A concentrated score warns the board that consensus in the room may be the shadow of one person rather than independent agreement. |

**C5 - False Consensus / Halo**

**What it measures**

False consensus is the assumption that others see a question as you do,
and the halo effect is letting one strong impression color every other
judgment about a person or plan. A board reads warm nods around the
table as agreement on a risky merger, never measuring how widely the
private estimates actually scatter. Auditing the spread of individual
judgments before any discussion reveals whether the apparent consensus
was real or a story the room told itself.

**How it is calculated**

σ_j = stdev(x_i); ECE = ∑(n_b/N)\|acc_b − conf_b\|

The judgment spread is the standard deviation of independent estimates,
and the calibration error checks whether the confidence attached to
those estimates matched how often they proved right.

σ_j is the judgment spread.

x_i is an individual judgment.

ECE is the calibration gap measured over bins.

n_b/N is the weight of a bin.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board designing a blind panel, measuring the spread of estimates before anyone speaks shows whether real agreement exists or whether one confident impression has been mistaken for many. It protects the board from approving a plan that only seemed to have broad support. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board structuring a blind review, the spread of independent judgments exposes false consensus before it hardens into a decision. It tells the board whether the team genuinely converged or whether a single strong impression carried the day. |

**C6 - Commission Bias / Optimism**

**What it measures**

Commission bias is the pull toward doing something rather than waiting,
and optimism is the quiet faith that the something will work, even when
no one has counted the ways it could fail. A leadership team rushes to
launch a new service line and never writes down what might break,
because acting feels braver than pausing. A premortem with a risk
register forces each failure mode onto paper, where likelihood and
severity can be multiplied into an honest exposure.

**How it is calculated**

R_i = ℓ_i × s_i

The exposure for each failure mode is its likelihood multiplied by its
severity, so a rare but ruinous event and a common but minor one can be
compared on the same scale.

R_i is the exposure for failure mode i.

ℓ_i is the likelihood.

s_i is the severity.

× is the multiplicative composition of likelihood and severity.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board running a premortem before a new program or a capital spend, the risk register turns the urge to act into a list of named failures with sizes attached. It gives the board a sober count of what could go wrong rather than a confident plan with the dangers left unsaid. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board weighing a product or market move, scoring each failure mode by likelihood times severity converts optimism into a ranked map of real exposure. It lets the board see which risks deserve mitigation before capital is committed to action. |

**C7 - Anchoring**

**What it measures**

Anchoring is the way a first number quietly holds the mind in place, so
that later evidence nudges the estimate only slightly when it should
move it a great deal. A foundation officer hears an opening ask of \$2
million and ends up settling near \$1.8 million, even after learning
facts that should have pushed the figure far lower. Tracking the
revision delta, the distance between the first guess and the final one,
shows whether new evidence actually moved the judgment or merely
decorated the anchor.

**How it is calculated**

RD = \|p_final − p_initial\|

The revision delta is the absolute distance between the final forecast
and the first estimate made before the evidence arrived, so a number
near 0 signals that fresh facts barely moved the view.

RD is the revision delta.

p_final is the final forecast.

p_initial is the initial pre-evidence forecast.

\|·\| marks the absolute change.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board during evidence review, the revision delta shows whether the team's estimate genuinely responded to what was learned or stayed tethered to its first number. A delta near 0 after strong new evidence is a signal that an anchor, not the facts, is driving the decision. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board reviewing evidence on a deal or a forecast, the revision delta measures how far judgment actually traveled as facts came in. It warns the board when a starting price or an early projection has quietly fixed the answer that should have changed. |

**C8 - Base Rate Neglect / Sample-size insensitivity**

**What it measures**

Base rate neglect is treating a vivid specific case as if the broad
background frequency did not exist, and sample-size insensitivity is
trusting a percentage drawn from far too few cases. A board sees that
four of five pilot sites succeeded and reads 80% as a stable rate,
forgetting that five sites can swing wildly by chance alone. A posterior
predictive check asks how often data this extreme would appear under the
assumed model, which exposes a small sample posing as solid ground.

**How it is calculated**

p_ppc = (1/N_rep)∑𝟙{T_rep ≥ T_obs}

The posterior predictive p-value counts how often a replicated test
statistic matches or exceeds the observed one across many simulated
draws, so a value near 0 or 1 flags data the model struggles to
reproduce.

p_ppc is the posterior predictive p-value.

N_rep is the number of replicated draws.

T_rep is the replicated test statistic, and T_obs is the observed test
statistic.

𝟙{·} is the indicator that counts each qualifying draw.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board before they commit to scaling a pilot, this check tests whether an encouraging early percentage rests on enough cases to trust. It keeps the board from betting living-support capacity on a number that a handful of lucky sites produced. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board before commit on a product or market call, the posterior predictive check reveals whether a promising early result reflects a real signal or a small sample's noise. It protects capital from being deployed against a percentage that cannot bear the weight placed on it. |

**C9 - Dunning-Kruger**

**What it measures**

Here the Dunning-Kruger pattern hides inside a single tidy Brier number,
where a forecaster's poor judgment is masked because one summary score
can look acceptable for the wrong reasons. A team reports a middling
Brier score and calls its forecasting adequate, never noticing that the
score is propped up by easy questions rather than real skill. The Murphy
decomposition splits that one number into its parts, separating how well
calibrated the forecasts were from how much they actually distinguished
events.

**How it is calculated**

BS = REL − RES + UNC

The Murphy decomposition breaks the Brier score into a reliability
penalty, a resolution benefit, and the base uncertainty of the events,
so a decent total can be traced to genuine skill or to easy questions.

REL is the reliability penalty.

RES is the resolution benefit.

UNC is the base uncertainty.

BS is the Brier score.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board reviewing forecasts after the outcome, the decomposition shows whether a respectable score came from real discernment or from questions that were never hard. It keeps the board from crediting skill where only easy calls were made. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board assessing forecasting after the fact, splitting the Brier score into calibration and resolution reveals whether the team genuinely distinguished outcomes. It stops a comfortable headline number from disguising weak judgment about the decisions that mattered. |

**C10 - Generalized overconfidence**

**What it measures**

Generalized overconfidence is the habit of reporting a single point
probability as if it settled the matter, while the weak assumptions
about noise behind it stay out of view. An executive states a 0.30
chance of failure with no range around it, and the board treats that
lone figure as firm ground. A conformal prediction interval wraps the
point estimate in an honest band of uncertainty, and the cost-loss rule
then asks whether action is warranted across that whole band.

**How it is calculated**

C\_{1−α}(x) = \[ŷ − q̂\_{1−α}, ŷ + q̂\_{1−α}\]; p\* = C/L

The conformal interval surrounds the point prediction with a
residual-based band that holds at the chosen error rate, and the
cost-loss threshold then marks the probability above which acting is
worthwhile.

C\_{1−α}(x) is the conformal interval.

ŷ is the point prediction.

q̂ is the residual quantile.

α is the error rate.

C/L is the cost-loss threshold.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board before they commit, pairing an honest uncertainty band with a cost-loss line shows whether a decision holds up across the plausible range, not just at one convenient point. It keeps the board from acting on a single confident number that hides how wide the real uncertainty runs. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board before commit on capital or product, the interval plus threshold tests whether the case for acting survives across the full span of likely outcomes. It guards against an overconfident point estimate steering a major decision past risks it never priced. |

**C11 - Confirmation Bias / Framing Effect**

**What it measures**

Confirmation bias is the search for evidence that supports what one
already believes, and the framing effect is giving different answers to
the same question dressed in different words. A program team gathers
only the studies that back its favored approach, then rates the plan
more highly when it is framed as a 70% success rather than a 30%
failure. Setting a quota for disconfirming evidence and measuring the
gap between answers under two frames drags both habits into the light.

**How it is calculated**

β = d/(s+d+n); Δφ = \|p_frameA − p_frameB\|

The disconfirming balance is the share of gathered evidence that cuts
against the favored view, and the frame delta is the distance between
the same judgment under two wordings.

β is the disconfirming-evidence balance.

d is the disconfirming count or weight.

s is the supporting count or weight.

n is the neutral count or weight.

Δφ is the frame sensitivity.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board during evidence review, the disconfirming quota forces the team to seek out what argues against its plan, and the frame delta checks whether the same proposal would survive a different wording. Together they tell the board whether a decision rests on a fair reading of the evidence. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board reviewing evidence on a strategy, requiring disconfirming evidence and testing two framings reveals whether a recommendation reflects the facts or only the team's preference. It protects capital from a case built to confirm what someone already wanted. |

**C12 - Planning Fallacy / Groupthink**

**What it measures**

When the planning fallacy meets groupthink, hopeful inside-view
estimates are first formed in isolation and then quietly squeezed toward
a shared number the group can comfortably endorse. Each engineer
privately fears a 14-month timeline, yet the meeting converges on the 9
months leadership clearly prefers, and the private doubts vanish into
consensus. The coordination tax measures how far individual estimates
were bent to reach agreement, exposing the cost of that compression.

**How it is calculated**

τ_coord = (1/N)∑\|x_i − c\|

The coordination tax is the average distance between each person's
private estimate and the consensus figure, so a large value shows how
much individual judgment was bent to reach agreement.

τ_coord is the coordination tax.

x_i is an individual estimate.

c is the consensus estimate.

N is the number of estimates.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board designing a blind panel, the coordination tax reveals how far private estimates were pulled to manufacture agreement on a plan. A high value tells the board that consensus came at the cost of honest individual judgment. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board structuring a blind review, this measure shows whether a unified timeline or forecast reflects real agreement or compressed dissent. It warns the board when comfortable consensus has buried the private doubts that should inform the decision. |

**C13 - Narrative Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The narrative fallacy is the mind's rush to wrap events in a clean
story, especially a story of why a regime shifted that only becomes
obvious once the shift has already happened. After a donor base
collapses, the board constructs a tidy account of the warning signs,
though no one read them as a pattern at the time. Watching lag-one
autocorrelation rise gives an early, numerical warning of a system
losing its stability, before the story is available to tell.

**How it is calculated**

φ₁ = corr(x_t,x\_{t−1})

The lag-one autocorrelation measures how strongly each value tracks the
one just before it, and a steady climb toward 1 is a recognized early
sign that a system is slowing and may be near a tipping point.

φ₁ is the lag-one autocorrelation.

x_t is the current value.

x\_{t−1} is the prior value.

corr is the correlation.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board monitoring an ongoing program or revenue stream, rising autocorrelation offers a quiet early warning that conditions are drifting toward a break. It lets the board act on a measured signal rather than waiting for the after-the-fact story. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board monitoring a market or operational metric, a climbing autocorrelation flags a system losing resilience before any narrative explains it. It gives the board time to respond to instability while it is still a number rather than a crisis. |

**C14 - Authority / Bandwagon**

**What it measures**

Authority and bandwagon biases describe how a single skewed judgment
spreads, either jumping from one actor to many as a cascade or looping
back to amplify itself within one decision chain. One respected director
endorses a vendor, and within weeks every department has adopted the
same choice, each pointing to the others as proof. Tracking the size of
the adoption cascade alongside the longest self-reinforcing chain shows
how far one initial nudge actually traveled.

**How it is calculated**

κ_cascade = \|E_adoption\|; ℓ_snowball = max path length

The cascade size counts how many adoption links a single judgment set
off, and the snowball length measures the longest chain in which the
bias kept feeding itself.

κ_cascade is the cascade size.

E_adoption is the set of adoption edges.

ℓ_snowball is the longest self-amplifying chain.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board monitoring how decisions spread across teams, these measures show whether a choice was independently reached or simply copied down a chain from one authority. They tell the board when apparent agreement is really a single judgment echoing through the organization. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board monitoring how a decision propagates, the cascade size and chain length reveal whether a market or vendor consensus grew from independent reasoning or from one influential move repeating itself. They warn the board when a single early nudge has quietly shaped a company-wide commitment. |

**C15 - Calibration model overfit**

**What it measures**

Calibration model overfit is the trap of a repair that flatters its own
training data while failing on cases it has not seen. A team applies an
isotonic adjustment, watches the in-sample calibration look perfect, and
ships it, only to find it falls apart on next quarter's forecasts. A
posterior predictive check run after the repair tests whether the fix
actually reproduces held-out data or merely memorized the sample it was
built on.

**How it is calculated**

p_ppc = (1/N_rep)∑𝟙{T_rep ≥ T_obs}

After a Platt or isotonic repair, the posterior predictive p-value
counts how often replicated statistics match or exceed the observed one,
so a value near 0 or 1 signals a calibration fix that does not
generalize.

p_ppc is the posterior predictive p-value after calibration repair.

N_rep is the number of replicated draws.

T is the test statistic.

𝟙{·} is the indicator that counts each qualifying draw.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board reviewing a calibration repair after the outcome, this check tests whether the fix holds on data it never saw or only looked good on its own training set. It keeps the board from trusting a correction that will quietly fail in the next cycle. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board assessing a calibration adjustment after the fact, the posterior predictive check distinguishes a genuine improvement from one that merely memorized past data. It protects future decisions from a repair that flatters the sample but breaks on real forecasts. |

**Quant-Field Entries (9)**

*These 9 measures look not at a forecast but at the conditions under
which a review was made. They count the things that decide whether a
judgment can be trusted at all, such as how many independent voices
weighed in and how widely their scores spread.*

**UQ1 - Blind sample size**

**What it measures**

Blind sample size counts how many people wrote down a judgment on their
own before anyone heard the room lean one way. Imagine a hiring panel
where each of six reviewers submits a score before the meeting opens,
rather than nodding along after the senior partner speaks first. A
higher count means the final view rests on minds that thought alone, and
a count of 1 or 2 means the record holds little more than a single
opinion wearing the clothes of a group.

**How it is calculated**

n_blind = \|{j_i : t_i \< t_reveal ∧ I_i = 1}\|

The measure counts only the judgments that were both filed before the
reveal and made without leaning on anyone else. n_blind is the count of
blind independent judgments. j_i is judgment i. t_i is the moment that
judgment was submitted. t_reveal is the moment discussion or visible
consensus began. I_i marks whether the reviewer worked independently.
The bars \|·\| mean the size of the set.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, blind sample size shows whether a program or grant decision rested on real independent reading or on one early voice the rest deferred to. When the count is low, the board learns that what looked like agreement may never have been tested. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this count reveals how many people actually formed a view on a capital or market commitment before the room aligned. A confident recommendation backed by only one or two blind judgments deserves a harder look before money moves. |

**UQ2 - Score spread**

**What it measures**

Score spread measures how far reviewers drifted apart in their ratings
once the scores are put on a common scale. Picture five evaluators
rating a proposal from 1 to 10, where one says 3 and another says 9.
That gap is information, because honest disagreement usually means the
evidence genuinely points in more than one direction. A healthy spread
sits in a normal band for the kind of decision at hand, while a spread
far below normal can mean the reviewers were copying each other rather
than judging.

**How it is calculated**

z_spread = (s_score − μ_s)/σ_s

The measure takes the observed disagreement and states it as a distance
from what disagreement usually looks like for similar reviews. z_spread
is the standardized score spread. s_score is the observed standard
deviation of the review scores. μ_s is the average spread expected for
comparable reviews. σ_s is how much that expected spread normally
varies.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, score spread tells whether reviewers of a program or funding choice truly wrestled with it or quietly settled on the same number. A spread far tighter than comparable reviews is a quiet warning that real scrutiny may have been skipped. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the spread shows how much genuine debate sat behind a product or investment score. Unusually tight scores on a hard, uncertain decision often signal pressure to agree rather than real consensus. |

**UQ3 - Uniformity pressure**

**What it measures**

Uniformity pressure measures how much tighter the agreement turned out
than the situation should have allowed. When evidence is thin and
reviewers come from different roles, opinions ought to scatter. If
instead everyone lands on nearly the same score, the gap between the
spread you expected and the spread you got is the pressure to conform. A
value near 0 means the agreement was earned, while a large value means
the room may have closed ranks before the question was settled.

**How it is calculated**

υ = max(0, s_expected − s_observed)

The measure subtracts the disagreement actually seen from the
disagreement that comparable reviews would predict, and reports only the
shortfall. υ is the uniformity-pressure signal. s_expected is the score
spread expected for comparable reviews. s_observed is the spread that
actually occurred. The max(0,·) holds the value at zero whenever
observed disagreement meets or beats what was expected.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, uniformity pressure flags moments when a program or staffing vote looked unanimous but the underlying evidence never justified such calm agreement. It points the board toward decisions that may need a second, more candid hearing. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this signal catches the kind of false consensus that precedes costly market or talent bets. A high reading invites leadership to reopen a question the team appeared to have closed too neatly. |

**UQ4 - Reviewer independence**

**What it measures**

Reviewer independence measures how far each reviewer stayed clear of the
same starting numbers, the same authority, and the same social cues that
pull judgments together. Think of three analysts who each see the deal
team's preferred valuation before scoring, against three who never do.
