The first group will tend to echo the anchor, the second will think for
themselves. The score runs from 0 to 1, where values near 1 mean
reviewers reached their views on separate paths and values near 0 mean
they were all reading from the same script.

**How it is calculated**

ι = 1 − norm(a_shared + v_prior + h_authority)

The measure adds up the shared influences on the reviewers, scales that
sum into a 0-to-1 range, and subtracts it so that less shared influence
yields more independence. ι is the reviewer independence score. a_shared
is how much reviewers were exposed to the same anchor. v_prior is how
visible earlier scores were to later reviewers. h_authority is how
visible a senior or authoritative view was. The norm(·) bounds the
combined exposure to the interval from 0 to 1.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, reviewer independence shows whether the people assessing a grant or program reached their views separately or simply absorbed one dominant opinion. A low score warns that an apparent consensus may be a single judgment repeated several times. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this score reveals how much of a recommendation came from independent thinking rather than a shared anchor planted early. On large capital or risk decisions, independence near the low end means the supporting votes carry less weight than their number suggests. |

**UQ5 - Evidence balance**

**What it measures**

Evidence balance measures how lopsided the case was between evidence
that supported the decision and evidence that cut against it. A board
paper that cites 20 reasons to proceed and almost nothing that might
give pause is telling you about its authors as much as about the world.
The measure runs from 0 to 1, where a value near 0 means supporting and
disconfirming evidence carried roughly equal weight and a value near 1
means the file leaned almost entirely one way.

**How it is calculated**

β = \|W_support − W_disconfirm\| / max(ε,W_total)

The measure takes the gap between supporting and opposing evidence and
divides it by all the evidence on the table, so a one-sided file scores
high. β is the evidence-balance gap. W_support is the weighted
supporting evidence. W_disconfirm is the weighted disconfirming
evidence. W_total is the total weight of all evidence. ε is a small
guard that keeps the denominator from reaching zero.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, evidence balance shows whether a proposal was argued fairly or built only from reasons to say yes. A high gap tells the board that the disconfirming side of the case was never seriously gathered. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this gap reveals when a product or market case has been assembled to persuade rather than to test. A reading near 1 on a major commitment is a sign to ask what evidence against the plan was left out of the room. |

**UQ6 - Processing completeness**

**What it measures**

Processing completeness measures the share of the evidence that was
actually dated, sorted, checked for freshness, and tied to the claim it
was meant to support. Raw material piled into a decision file is not the
same as evidence that has been worked through. If 40 sources were
submitted and only 10 were handled this way, the file is one-quarter
processed. The measure runs from 0 to 1, and a value near 1 means little
was left sitting unexamined.

**How it is calculated**

χ = n_processed / max(1,N_evidence)

The measure divides the evidence that was properly worked through by all
the evidence gathered. χ is the processing completeness. n_processed is
the number of evidence rows that were properly worked through and
connected to the claim they support. N_evidence is the total number of
evidence rows. The max(1,·) keeps the calculation safe when no evidence
was logged.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, processing completeness shows whether the evidence behind a decision was genuinely examined or merely collected and filed. A low share warns that much of the supporting material was never checked for age or relevance. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this share separates a decision built on worked-through evidence from one resting on an impressive but untouched stack. On a costly bet, a low value means the file deserves real processing before approval. |

**UQ7 - Phrase-family hits**

**What it measures**

Phrase-family hits measures how thickly a written rationale is salted
with language that signals false certainty, such as words of
inevitability, sweeping cause, or absolute confidence. A memo that calls
an outcome certain and then inevitable is leaning on tone where it
should be leaning on evidence. The measure is reported per 1,000 words
so that long and short documents can be compared, and a lower rate means
the writing claimed only what it could show.

**How it is calculated**

φ = \[∑\_k w_kh_k\] / max(1,W_words/1000)

The measure weighs each cluster of loaded phrases, adds them up, and
divides by the length of the rationale in thousands of words. φ is the
weighted phrase-family hit rate per 1,000 words. h_k is the number of
hits in phrase family k. w_k is the weight given to phrase family k.
W_words is the word count of the rationale. The max(1,·) keeps very
short rationales from distorting the rate.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this rate flags proposals that persuade through confident language rather than through what the evidence can bear. A high count invites the board to ask whether the certainty in the writing is matched by certainty in the facts. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the phrase rate exposes pitches that lean on inevitability and absolute language to carry a weak case. On a large commitment, a high reading is a prompt to strip the tone away and read what remains. |

**UQ8 - Challenge record completeness**

**What it measures**

Challenge record completeness measures how much of the dissent and
pre-commitment was actually written down: the alternatives weighed, the
owner named, the deadline set, and the condition that would call the
whole thing off. A decision that records no kill criterion and no named
skeptic has left no trace of having been challenged. The measure runs
from 0 to 1, where a value near 1 means the hard questions were captured
rather than waved through.

**How it is calculated**

γ = n_completed / N_required

The measure divides the challenge fields that were filled in by the
fields the process required. γ is the challenge-record completeness.
n_completed is the number of challenge fields actually completed.
N_required is the number of challenge fields the process called for.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this measure shows whether dissent and exit conditions were recorded before a program or funding commitment, not invented afterward. A low value tells the board that the decision was never seriously challenged on the record. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, completeness here reveals whether a major bet carried a written kill criterion and a named challenger from the start. When the record is thin, the organization has no honest way to know later whether it should stop. |

**UQ9 - Calibration error**

**What it measures**

Calibration error measures the distance between how confident forecasts
were and how often those forecasts came true. If a team calls a string
of events 80% likely and only 50% of them happen, its confidence has run
ahead of reality by 30 points. The measure compares average stated
probability against the observed rate across comparable decisions, and a
value near 0 means the team's confidence has been earned by results.

**How it is calculated**

ε_cal = \|p̄ − ō\|

The measure takes the absolute gap between the average probability the
team forecast and how often the event actually occurred. ε_cal is the
calibration error. p̄ is the mean forecast probability. ō is the observed
frequency, meaning the rate at which the predicted outcomes happened.
The bars \|·\| make the gap a plain distance regardless of direction.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, calibration error gives a fair record of whether the organization's confidence in its forecasts has matched what actually unfolded. A persistent gap tells the board to discount future predictions until the confidence is brought back in line with results. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this gap shows whether the numbers attached to forecasts can be trusted when capital is at stake. A large and steady error means the team's stated probabilities should be treated as opinion, not measurement, until calibration improves. |

**Composite Combo Records (5)**

*The 5 composite records gather the larger failure patterns that take
hold across a whole governing body over time. Each one is a slow drift
rather than a single error, and each is named so that a board can
recognize it while it is still reversible.*

**UC1 - Deferential Consensus Combo**

**What it measures**

Deferential Consensus is the pattern that appears when a respected voice
speaks first and the room quietly arranges itself behind that voice. The
authority bias and the bandwagon effect feed each other, reviewers stop
scoring on their own, and the spread of their ratings collapses toward a
single agreed number. Picture a foundation board where the chair says
the new program is excellent, and within minutes every reviewer has
marked it a 9 out of 10 without naming a single risk. The danger is not
that the chair is wrong. It is that the record now holds one borrowed
opinion wearing the costume of five independent judgments.

**How it is calculated**

κ_UC1 = γ_auth + γ_crowd + (1 − ι) + υ

Reading: the composite rises with the authority signal, the crowd or
bandwagon signal, the lack of reviewer independence, and the pressure
toward uniform scores.

κ_UC1 is the deferential-consensus risk.

γ_auth is the authority signal.

γ_crowd is the crowd or bandwagon signal.

ι is the independence score, so (1 − ι) grows as independence falls.

υ is the uniformity pressure in the scores.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, reach for UC1 when a major decision drew unusual agreement very fast and no one on the record dissented. A grant or hiring choice that every reviewer scored alike, right after a senior figure stated a view, is a sign to ask each reviewer for an independent judgment before the board commits. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, UC1 flags the investment or acquisition vote that closed with suspicious harmony in the room. When the deal memo shows uniform enthusiasm following a founder or chief executive endorsement, require at least one named owner to argue the opposing case in writing before capital moves. |

**UC2 - Evidence Echo Combo**

**What it measures**

Evidence Echo is what happens when a group keeps hearing the same claim
and slowly mistakes its familiarity for proof. Confirmation bias filters
out the contradicting facts, repetition makes the surviving claim feel
true, and a thin set of sources begins to look like a chorus. Imagine a
leadership team that reads four memos all citing the same consultant,
notices the consultant's name everywhere that week, and concludes the
market has spoken. In truth one voice has simply been played back to
them four times, and the group has counted the echo as new evidence.

**How it is calculated**

κ_UC2 = β + r_repeat + a_attention + (1 − d_source)

Reading: the composite rises with the imbalance in the evidence, the
rate at which the same claim repeats, the recurrence of attention to it,
and the lack of variety in the sources.

κ_UC2 is the evidence-echo risk.

β is the evidence-balance gap.

r_repeat is the repetition rate.

a_attention is the attention recurrence.

d_source is the source diversity, so (1 − d_source) grows as the sources
narrow.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use UC2 when the case for a decision rests on the same handful of voices repeated across many documents. If a program looks proven mainly because the claim keeps recurring, ask the staff to bring an independent source that could have shown the opposite before the board relies on it. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, UC2 catches the market thesis that feels obvious only because the same analyst note has circulated under many covers. Before funding a product or entering a market on that thesis, require evidence from a source with no tie to the original claim. |

**UC3 - Narrative Lock-In Combo**

**What it measures**

Narrative Lock-In is the grip of a story so tidy that a group can no
longer imagine abandoning it. An early figure anchors the discussion, a
clean narrative closes around it, and the fear of admitting loss binds
everyone to the money and effort already spent. Think of an executive
team that set a 5 million dollar product bet two years ago, still
describes it as the company's signature move, and treats every overrun
as one more reason to continue rather than to stop. The story has become
the reason, and the longer it runs the more expensive it feels to
question.

**How it is calculated**

κ_UC3 = α_anchor + ν_story + λ_loss + σ_sunk

Reading: the composite rises with the strength of the anchor, the
pressure toward narrative closure, the intensity of the loss frame, and
the amount of sunk-cost language in the record.

κ_UC3 is the narrative-lock-in risk.

α_anchor is the anchor strength.

ν_story is the narrative-closure pressure.

λ_loss is the loss-frame intensity.

σ_sunk is the sunk-cost language intensity.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, UC3 is the warning that a beloved program is being defended by its history rather than its results. When the discussion leans on how much has already been invested, ask what the board would choose if it were starting fresh today, with no prior commitment. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, UC3 flags the project that survives on its founding story and the dread of writing off past spending. Before approving the next round of capital, force a decision framed only on future returns, with the money already spent treated as gone. |

**UC4 - Governance Drift Combo**

**What it measures**

Governance Drift is the slow erosion of real oversight, where a board
spends its energy on small comforts and waves through the things that
matter. The members do not see their own blind spot, they linger on
trivial items, they accept the first adequate answer, and no one is left
to object. Picture a board that debates the wording of a press release
for 40 minutes, then approves a 2 million dollar contract in 4 with no
dissent recorded. The meeting felt productive, yet the one decision that
carried weight received the least scrutiny.

**How it is calculated**

κ_UC4 = m_self + τ_low + s_first + m_dissent

Reading: the composite rises when self-checking is missing, when
low-stakes items consume the time, when the group stops at the first
adequate source, and when dissent is absent.

κ_UC4 is the governance-drift risk.

m_self is the missing self-check.

τ_low is the share of time spent on low-stakes items.

s_first is the first-adequate-source indicator.

m_dissent is the missing dissent.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use UC4 when the minutes show long debate on minor matters and quick assent on major ones. If a significant approval passed with no recorded objection, assign someone to prepare the opposing view before the next vote of that size. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, UC4 catches the governance meeting that polishes the trivial and rubber-stamps the consequential. When a large commitment clears without a single challenge on the record, require a named dissent owner and a documented reason the alternative was rejected. |

**UC5 - Learning Washout Combo**

**What it measures**

Learning Washout is the failure to learn from a decision because
hindsight has rewritten how it was made. Once the result is known, the
group judges the choice by that result, claims it saw the outcome
coming, and recalls its own past confidence as firmer than it was.
Imagine a team reviewing a campaign that happened to succeed, now
certain the win was obvious and that everyone backed it from the start,
though the original notes recorded real doubt. The record of how the
team actually reasoned dissolves, and the next decision inherits no
genuine lesson.

**How it is calculated**

κ_UC5 = ω_outcome + η_hindsight + η_choice + r_cert

Reading: the composite rises with the weight placed on the outcome, the
amount of hindsight language, the amount of choice-supportive language,
and the retrospective certainty in the account.

κ_UC5 is the learning-washout risk.

ω_outcome is the outcome-weighting pressure.

η_hindsight is the hindsight language.

η_choice is the choice-supportive language.

r_cert is the retrospective certainty.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, UC5 is the sign that a review is grading the result instead of the reasoning. When a post-mortem says the outcome was always clear, return to what the staff actually wrote beforehand and judge the decision against the uncertainty that existed at the time. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, UC5 flags the retrospective that launders a lucky win into proof of skill. Before carrying that lesson into the next bet, compare the confident hindsight account with the contemporaneous record of what the team really expected. |

**Review Dynamics**

*Review Dynamics covers the noise and social pressure that move a panel
away from the evidence in front of it. The entries describe how the same
case can draw different scores depending on who speaks first and how the
room is feeling that day.*

**Level Noise**

**What it measures**

Level noise is the steady gap in severity between reviewers who are
looking at the very same kind of proposal. One panelist routinely scores
a 7 where another routinely scores a 4, and the difference says more
about the two people than about the work in front of them. Imagine two
staff members reading the same housing grant. One has spent a decade
saying no to protect the budget. The other came up believing most
applicants deserve a chance, and the proposal lands at 8.1 or 5.3
depending only on whose queue it falls into. When that gap drives the
outcome, the panel is measuring its own reviewers more than the
proposal.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Level Noise when the score exceeds the local control
limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit the
pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each other
more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this matters most when blind reviewers score an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid. If level noise shows up, hold approval until you have collected scores gathered independently, so a habitually harsh or generous reviewer cannot decide the result alone. That protects the commitment to presume competence from being quietly overruled by one person's calibration. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, level noise bites when the same reviewers keep rating market entries and executive hires. A deal that clears at one reviewer's table and dies at another's is being judged by temperament rather than merit, and real capital follows that accident. Require independent scoring and an anonymized read of the rationale before the number is trusted. |

**Stable Pattern Noise**

**What it measures**

Stable pattern noise is a reviewer's durable personal habit of weighting
one thing too much and another too little, round after round. It is not
random, which is what makes it hard to see. Picture a panelist who
always rewards a polished budget and always discounts a strong community
letter. Over 20 reviews this reviewer hands the well-formatted proposal
an extra point and quietly costs the grassroots applicant one, and
because the tilt is so consistent everyone reads it as judgment rather
than a fixed lean. When a single reviewer's signature pattern decides
close calls, the panel inherits that person's blind spot.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Stable Pattern Noise when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, watch for this when the same reviewers score an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid across many cycles. If stable pattern noise appears, gather independent scores before approval so one reviewer's fixed lean does not silently set the bar. That keeps the presume-competence commitment from being undercut by a habit nobody named. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this shows up when a recurring reviewer always favors the same kind of plan and always dings another, whether the question is a pricing change or a hire. The pattern feels like wisdom because it never wavers, yet it can steer capital toward one shape of bet for years. Use independent scoring and an anonymized rationale review to test whether the preference is earned or merely habitual. |

**Occasion Noise**

**What it measures**

Occasion noise is the swing in a single reviewer's judgment caused by
the moment rather than the merits, by passing fatigue or by the order in
which files arrive. The same person can score the same proposal
differently on a Monday morning and a Friday afternoon. Picture a
reviewer reading the 12th application of the day, right before lunch,
after three weak submissions in a row. The strong proposal that follows
gets a 6 now and would have earned an 8 at 9 a.m. with a clear desk.
Nothing about the work changed, only the hour and the appetite of the
person reading it.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Occasion Noise when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this matters when a single reviewer scores an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid in long sittings. If occasion noise appears, slow down and collect independent scores taken at different times, so an applicant's fate does not hinge on what slot they drew. A genuinely good plan deserves a fair reading whether it arrives first or 15th. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, occasion noise is the reason a market entry or hire can clear after a good morning and stall after a bad one. The judgment moved while the proposal did not, and millions can ride on that drift. Spread the reads across sittings and require independent scoring so the timing of the meeting does not stand in for analysis. |

**Bandwagon Effect**

**What it measures**

The bandwagon effect is the pull toward a view simply because it looks
like it is winning the room. As soon as a position seems to be gaining
support, agreeing with it feels safer and smarter than questioning it.
Picture a review meeting where the first two speakers praise a proposal
warmly. The third reviewer, who arrived with real doubts, hears the
momentum and trims an intended 4 up to a 7, telling herself the others
must see something she missed. The proposal did not improve in those
four minutes. The count of visible supporters did, and that count
quietly did the persuading.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Bandwagon Effect when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this is the risk when reviewers score an an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid together in one room. If the bandwagon effect appears, collect each reviewer's score privately before any discussion, so an early surge of agreement cannot manufacture the rest. The presume-competence commitment is safest when each judgment is its own. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the bandwagon effect turns an early show of enthusiasm into a consensus that was never really tested, whether the call is an acquisition or a launch. By the time the room feels unanimous, the dissent that might have saved capital has gone quiet. Gather independent scores before debate and keep the rationale anonymized so the tally cannot do the arguing. |

**Authority Bias**

**What it measures**

Authority bias is the extra weight a judgment gathers simply because it
came from someone powerful, a senior leader or a major funder. The
status of the speaker stands in for the strength of the argument.
Picture a panel that has read a proposal carefully and is leaning toward
no, until the executive director says she likes it. Several reviewers
revise upward within the hour, not because new evidence arrived but
because disagreeing with the boss feels costly. The proposal carries the
same facts it carried before she spoke. What changed is who is now
attached to it.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Authority Bias when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this is the danger when a senior voice weighs in while reviewers score an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid. If authority bias appears, separate the senior voice from the evidence and collect scores before leadership states a preference. That keeps the presume-competence commitment from quietly bending to rank. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, authority bias lets a founder's or a large investor's preference substitute for analysis on a market entry or a major capital allocation. The deal that the most powerful person favors can sail through review that a quieter sponsor's identical proposal would never survive. Require a pre-read that keeps the rationale separate from who is backing it, and score independently before anyone with rank speaks. |

**Social Desirability Bias**

**What it measures**

Social desirability bias is the bending of a stated judgment so it looks
compassionate or loyal to the people in the room. The reviewer reports
the answer that will play well rather than the one the evidence
supports. Picture a panelist who privately thinks a beloved legacy
program should be wound down, then writes a warm endorsement because
voting against it would look cold to colleagues who founded it. The
honest assessment never reaches the record. What reaches it is the
version that protects the reviewer's standing, and the program survives
on a verdict nobody actually believed.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Social Desirability Bias when the score exceeds the
local control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round
order fit the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean
on each other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence
carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this bias hides in reviews of an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid where a candid no would feel unkind. If social desirability bias appears, collect scores privately so a reviewer's honest read is not edited for the room. The presume-competence commitment depends on reviewers saying what they actually think. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, social desirability bias produces approvals that everyone can live with and nobody quite endorses, on launches and capital calls alike. The pet project of a respected colleague gets a kind score that costs the company later. Take scores independently and review the rationale anonymized, so the safe answer cannot crowd out the true one. |

**False Consensus Effect**

**What it measures**

The false consensus effect is the quiet assumption that most people see
a question the way you do, when the actual support is far thinner. The
reviewer mistakes a personal conviction for common ground. Picture a
program officer certain that donors will love a bold new direction, who
states in review that the base is clearly behind it. In truth perhaps
35% of donors share that enthusiasm, and the officer never tested the
figure because it felt self-evident. The proposal advances on an
imagined majority. When the real numbers arrive, the consensus that
justified the decision turns out to have lived mostly in one head.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag False Consensus Effect when the score exceeds the
local control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round
order fit the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean
on each other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence
carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this appears when a reviewer scores an an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid on the belief that staff or donors plainly agree. If the false consensus effect shows, ask for the evidence behind the claimed agreement before approval, rather than treating one person's certainty as the room's. That keeps the presume-competence commitment grounded in what people actually said. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the false consensus effect lets a leader's read of the market pass as the market's own view on a pricing change or entry. The assumption that customers obviously want this can commit capital before anyone checks. Require independent scoring and a pre-read that asks for the measured support behind any claim of broad agreement. |

**Third-Person Effect**

**What it measures**

The third-person effect is the belief that persuasion and pressure work
on other people but not on oneself. The reviewer grants that the crowd
can be swayed while quietly exempting his own judgment. Picture a
panelist who dismisses a slick funder presentation as marketing that
might fool the junior staff, then approves the proposal anyway, sure
that he saw through it. The same polish that he assumed only moved
others moved him too. The danger of this bias is precisely that it
disarms the guard, because the person most certain of his immunity is
the one taking no precautions.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Third-Person Effect when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this matters when reviewers face a persuasive pitch for an an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid. If the third-person effect appears, build in independent scoring rather than trusting a reviewer's sense that he alone is unmoved. The presume-competence commitment is safer when no one is assumed immune to the room. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the third-person effect is why a confident executive waves off a vendor's polish as something that only sways the less experienced, then signs the deal himself. The certainty of immunity is what removes the check. Require independent scoring and an anonymized rationale so the persuasion that supposedly works only on others has somewhere to be caught. |

**Overconfidence Effect**

**What it measures**

The overconfidence effect is the gap between how sure a reviewer feels
and how accurate that reviewer has actually been. Confidence outruns the
track record that should anchor it. Picture a panelist who says he is
90% certain a venture will succeed, while reviewers with his past calls
have been right closer to 60% of the time. He has not earned the last 30
points of that certainty, yet the panel treats his conviction as if it
were calibrated. When felt confidence stands in for demonstrated
accuracy, the strongest voice in the room can also be the least
warranted.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Overconfidence Effect when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this appears when a reviewer voices strong certainty about an an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid. If the overconfidence effect shows, weigh the confidence against the reviewer's past accuracy before approval, not against how firmly it is stated. The presume-competence commitment is better served by calibrated judgment than by loud conviction. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, overconfidence is why a forecast stated with 95% certainty can sink a market entry that honest odds put closer to a coin flip. The conviction sells the decision and the calibration would have caught it. Score independently and check stated confidence against the forecaster's real hit rate before committing capital. |

**Dunning-Kruger Effect**

**What it measures**

The Dunning-Kruger effect is the trap in which limited skill comes
paired with inflated self-assessment, because the very knowledge needed
to see one's gaps is the knowledge one lacks. The less someone knows,
the harder it is to know how little that is. Picture a reviewer new to
public health who rates a clinical proposal with breezy certainty,
missing the design flaws an experienced epidemiologist would catch in
minutes. He scores it a confident 9, unaware that his confidence and his
competence point in opposite directions. The result is not arrogance so
much as a blind spot that hides its own edges.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Dunning-Kruger Effect when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this matters when a reviewer outside their field scores an an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid with easy certainty. If the Dunning-Kruger effect appears, route the proposal to someone with the relevant depth before approval rather than trusting a confident outsider. The presume-competence commitment needs reviewers who can see what they do not know. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this effect is why a confident generalist can wave through a technical acquisition whose real risks only a specialist would spot. The certainty is loudest exactly where the competence is thinnest. Match reviewers to the domain and require independent scoring so unearned confidence does not stand in for expertise on capital decisions. |

**Hard-Easy Effect**

**What it measures**

The hard-easy effect is a tilt in calibration set by task difficulty,
with confidence running too high on genuinely hard judgments and too low
on easy ones. The mind misjudges its own footing depending on the
terrain. Picture a panel forecasting a complex multi-year program, where
reviewers feel 80% sure of an outcome that history would put nearer 55%.
On a simple, familiar renewal the same panel hedges at 70% when the
honest figure is closer to 95%. The errors point in opposite directions,
so a panel can look well calibrated on average while being wrong in a
patterned way on both the hardest and the easiest calls.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Hard-Easy Effect when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this matters when reviewers rate an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid that range from routine to genuinely novel. If the hard-easy effect appears, treat confidence on the hardest proposals with extra caution and revisit the easy ones that drew oddly low marks. The presume-competence commitment is steadier when difficulty is named rather than left to skew the scores. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this effect inflates certainty on the hardest bets, a first-of-its-kind market entry, while deflating it on familiar ones a team should own with ease. Capital then chases overrated long shots and underrates safe ground. Calibrate stated confidence against the difficulty of the call and score independently before committing. |

**Illusory Superiority**

**What it measures**

Illusory superiority is a person's or group's habit of rating its own
ability or diligence as above average without the evidence to back it.
Most people place themselves in the better half, which is a statistical
impossibility once enough of them do it. Picture a board convinced it
screens proposals more rigorously than peer organizations, when its
actual approval errors run about the same as everyone else's. That
belief makes the board slower to add outside review, because it feels
unnecessary. The conviction of being better than average is itself the
thing that lets the average go unexamined.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Illusory Superiority when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this matters when the team assumes its own scoring of an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid is sharper than it has shown itself to be. If illusory superiority appears, bring in independent review precisely where the team feels it is least needed. The presume-competence commitment extends to honesty about the panel's own limits, not only the applicant's strengths. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, illusory superiority is why a team sure of its edge skips the outside check on an acquisition or launch that its track record does not actually justify. The felt advantage discourages the very scrutiny that would test it. Require independent scoring and an anonymized rationale review so confidence in the team's judgment is earned against results, not assumed. |

**Egocentric Bias**

**What it measures**

Egocentric bias is the tendency to weight one's own role and effort more
heavily than everyone else's when judging a shared question. The view
from inside one's own contribution looks larger than the others'.
Picture a department head reviewing a joint proposal who, asked what
share of the work each unit drove, puts her own team at 60% while every
other lead privately claims a similar slice. The shares cannot all be
right, since they add past 100%. Her score for the proposal quietly
favors the part she sees most clearly, which is the part she had a hand
in.

**How it is calculated**

r_RD = w_n𝟙(n_blind \< n\*) + w_sz_spread + w_υυ + w_γγ_social − w_ιι

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.25\*z(score spread) +
0.20\*z(uniformity pressure) + 0.20\*z(confidence minus support) +
0.20\*z(1 - reviewer independence) + 0.15\*z(1 - blind sample size
normalized). Flag Egocentric Bias when the score exceeds the local
control limit and review metadata, confidence fields, or round order fit
the pattern. The score rises as reviewers disagree more, lean on each
other more, or claim more confidence than their evidence carries.

r_RD is the review-dynamics risk score. n_blind is the count of blind
judgments. n\* is the minimum acceptable blind sample. z_spread is the
standardized spread across reviewer scores. υ is the uniformity pressure
pushing reviewers toward one answer. γ_social is the pull of authority
and the wish to look agreeable. ι is the independence score. w\_\* are
the pre-set weights.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a PCC director and board, this matters when reviewers who helped shape an an inclusive enterprise plan or a fundraising bid also score it. If egocentric bias appears, separate authorship from review so a reviewer's own contribution does not inflate the verdict. The presume-competence commitment is cleanest when the people judging a plan are not silently scoring their own work. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, egocentric bias is why a sponsor who built a market-entry case rates it higher than a neutral reviewer would, overvaluing the part they own. Several such sponsors at one table can push a weak plan through. Keep authorship separate from scoring and require independent review before capital follows the loudest claim of credit. |

**Evidence Quality**

*Evidence Quality gathers the biases that corrupt the record a decision
rests on, long before anyone reasons about it. Each entry shows how a
particular habit of attention quietly filters what counts as evidence at
all.*

**Confirmation Bias**

**What it measures**

Confirmation bias is the mind's habit of hunting for evidence that fits
what it already believes while quietly setting aside whatever does not.
Imagine a program director who is sure a new tutoring model works. She
reads the three glowing parent notes closely and skims past the
attendance log that shows half the children stopped coming. The belief
did not lie to her. It told her where to look, and she looked only
there. The bias bites hardest exactly when the stakes feel settled,
because that is when the search for contrary facts goes quiet.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, run this check before you rest a grant case or a program-outcome claim on evidence that was gathered by the people who already believed it. When the flag appears, ask for the cases that cut the other way, the current numbers rather than last year's, and the view of an outside reader. The point is to learn whether the favorable picture survived an honest look for its opposite. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, treat this flag as a pause before capital follows a market-size or retention claim built only from the wins. Ask for the base rate, fresh figures, and at least one adverse case that the original author had reason to ignore. A claim that holds up after a real search for its weaknesses is worth far more than one that was only ever defended. |

**Ostrich Effect**

**What it measures**

The ostrich effect is the choice to look away from information that is
unwelcome but plainly relevant. The mind treats the bad news as if not
reading it keeps it from being true. Picture a finance officer who lets
the variance report sit unopened for three weeks because she suspects
the program is running over budget. The number does not change while the
envelope stays closed. It only grows. The bias bites when a person most
needs to know something and least wants to.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this before a capacity forecast or expansion plan that conveniently omits the data nobody wanted to gather. When it fires, the missing piece is usually the threatening one, so ask for the current figures and the disconfirming examples by name. A plan that has faced its own bad news is far steadier than one that has avoided it. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag often marks a deal where an inconvenient risk went unmeasured rather than unseen. Before approving capital, require the adverse cases and the fresh quantitative support that the original case skipped. The cost of opening the bad report now is almost always smaller than the cost of meeting it later. |

**Selective Perception**

**What it measures**

Selective perception is the way expectations and preferences quietly
sort ambiguous evidence, making some facts vivid and others nearly
invisible. Two people read the same client survey and come away certain
of opposite things, each having truly noticed only the lines that
matched what they hoped to find. Think of a board that wants a program
to succeed reading a mixed evaluation and remembering the warm quotes
far better than the flat outcome data. Nobody lied. The filter sat
between the evidence and the eye.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this before you trust a single reading of an outcome claim, because the same record can support more than one story. Bring in an outside reader and the views of the adults served, whose account may differ from staff impressions. Two honest interpretations placed side by side reveal which facts were really there and which were supplied by hope. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that the read on a market signal may have been shaped by what the team wanted it to mean. Ask for the same evidence interpreted by someone with no stake in the outcome, alongside the base rate. The discipline is to separate what the data says from what the reader hoped it would say. |

**Conservatism Bias**

**What it measures**

Conservatism bias is the failure to move a belief far enough when solid
new evidence says it should change. The mind treats its prior view as an
anchor and tugs only slightly toward the fresh fact. Consider a director
who has long believed a flagship program serves 400 families well, then
receives clean data showing real engagement has fallen to 240. She
nudges her estimate to 380 and calls it caution. The evidence asked for
a large step and received a small one. The bias bites when the world
moves faster than the mind allows.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when current data clashes with a long-held picture of a program and the update on offer feels too small to match the gap. Ask explicitly how much the new evidence should move the estimate, then check whether it actually did. A belief that barely shifts in the face of strong data is a belief that has stopped listening. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag marks the moment when fresh numbers contradict an established view and the revision is suspiciously gentle. Require the outside-view benchmark and the quantitative support, then ask whether the conclusion truly absorbed them. Capital committed to yesterday's belief, lightly adjusted, is capital exposed to a world that already changed. |

**Base Rate Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The base rate fallacy is the mind's habit of fixing on the vivid details
of a single case while ignoring how often such things actually happen.
The story in front of you crowds out the history behind it. A funder
hears a stirring account of one youth who turned his life around and
funds the program as if that outcome were typical, when the base rate of
similar programs shows perhaps 15% reach it. The case was real. It was
simply not representative. The bias bites whenever a particular story is
more available than the general rate.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this before a single success story stands in for an entire program's likely results. Ask for the historical rate across comparable efforts and place the one case beside it. The story can stay, but it should illustrate the rate rather than replace it. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a compelling individual account is being mistaken for the typical case. Before capital follows it, require the base rate and an outside-view benchmark drawn from how such ventures usually fare. The vivid example tells you what is possible, not what is probable. |

**Attentional Bias**

**What it measures**

Attentional bias is the pull that vivid or emotionally charged evidence
exerts on the mind, drawing notice away from quieter facts that matter
just as much. What grips us is not always what informs us. A leadership
team that watched one angry client confrontation last week may weigh it
above six months of steady satisfaction scores when judging a service.
The dramatic event did not become more common. It became more present.
The bias bites when the loudest piece of evidence is mistaken for the
most important one.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when one striking incident threatens to outweigh the full record behind a program or service. Set the memorable event against the current data and ask whether its weight matches its frequency. A single vivid moment deserves attention, not the whole vote. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag marks a decision tilting toward the most recent or dramatic signal rather than the steady evidence. Before capital moves, require the quantitative support and a fresh look at the wider record. What seized the room's attention is rarely the same as what should decide the matter. |

**Context Effect**

**What it measures**

The context effect is the way a judgment shifts because of its
surroundings rather than its substance. The same fact reads differently
depending on what sits beside it, how it is worded, or where it falls in
a sequence. A budget request for 50,000 dollars feels modest right after
a 2 million dollar item and extravagant right after a 5,000 dollar one,
though the request never changed. Picture a board approving a program
because it was presented just after a far weaker one. The bias bites
whenever the frame around the evidence does work that the evidence
itself has not earned.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when the order or framing of a presentation may be carrying a proposal that its own merits would not. Ask to see the case on its own, with a neutral benchmark, free of the comparison that made it shine. A program worth funding should still look worth funding once the surrounding scenery is removed. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that wording or sequence may be flattering a deal beyond what its numbers support. Require the figures judged against an outside benchmark rather than against whatever was shown just before. Capital should follow the substance of an option, not the staging of it. |

**Bizarreness Effect**

**What it measures**

The bizarreness effect is the mind's tendency to remember the odd and
unusual far better than the ordinary, and then to treat that vividness
as if it were importance. Strangeness sticks, and we mistake what sticks
for what matters. A reviewer recalls the one truly unusual failure in a
program's history and weighs it heavily, while a hundred routine
successes blur together and fade. The peculiar case was memorable
precisely because it was rare. The bias bites when ease of recall is
confused with weight of evidence.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when an unusual case looms large in a discussion about a program's worth. Ask how often it actually occurs and set it against the routine record that is easy to forget. A rare event can teach a real lesson without deserving to govern the decision. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a memorably strange outcome may be steering judgment past the ordinary data. Before capital responds, require the base rate and the quantitative support that show how typical the case really is. What is unforgettable is not always what is representative. |

**Experimenter's Bias**

**What it measures**

Experimenter's bias is the way an observer's expectations seep into how
evidence is gathered and recorded, so the result tilts toward what was
anticipated. The hand that measures is not neutral. A program evaluator
who expects good outcomes may, without any dishonesty, record borderline
cases as successes and phrase questions in ways that invite the answer
she awaits. The data look clean. The thumb on the scale was invisible
even to the one pressing it. The bias bites whenever the people
producing the evidence already know what they hope to find.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when the people who collected an outcome claim had a stake in how it turned out. Ask for an independent reviewer and for the raw method behind the numbers, not only the summary. Evidence gains trust when the hand that gathered it had no reason to wish it one way or the other. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a favorable result may carry the expectations of whoever produced it. Before capital relies on it, require independent verification and a look at how the data were coded. The cleaner a self-collected number looks, the more it is worth checking against a disinterested source. |

**Semmelweis Reflex**

**What it measures**

The Semmelweis reflex is the reflexive rejection of new evidence simply
because it collides with established practice or authority. The mind
defends the familiar before it weighs the fact. The name recalls a
physician whose data on handwashing were dismissed for years because
they offended accepted medical opinion, while patients kept dying.
Picture a long-serving team waving away an evaluation that questions a
beloved method, not because the evidence is weak but because the method
is theirs. The bias bites when the strength of a finding is judged by
how comfortably it fits what everyone already does.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a finding that challenges a settled practice is being dismissed faster than it is being examined. Ask the team to argue the evidence on its merits and to invite an outside reviewer who carries no loyalty to the old way. A practice worth keeping can survive an honest test of the data against it. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag marks evidence rejected for clashing with how things have always been done. Before that reflex shapes capital, require the disconfirming case to be heard and an outside-view benchmark applied. The most expensive mistakes often begin with a true fact that was waved away for being inconvenient. |

**Illusory Truth Effect**

**What it measures**

The illusory truth effect is the strange comfort that repetition lends
to a claim, making it feel more true each time it is heard even though
hearing it again adds nothing to the evidence. Familiarity masquerades
as proof. A figure cited in every board deck for three years comes to
feel established simply because it keeps appearing, though no one has
checked it since it was first written down. The repetition built
confidence without building support. The bias bites when how often a
claim is repeated is mistaken for how well it is grounded.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a long-repeated claim about a program has come to feel certain by habit alone. Trace it back to its original source and check whether the current data still bear it out. A statement repeated for years is not the same as a statement verified this year. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a much-repeated figure may owe its credibility to familiarity rather than fresh support. Before capital rests on it, require current quantitative backing and a return to the primary source. A number does not become true by being said often. |

**Frequency Illusion (Baader-Meinhof)**

**What it measures**

The frequency illusion is the sense that something starts happening
everywhere the moment you begin noticing it, when in truth only your
attention has changed. The world held steady. Your filter sharpened. A
director who just learned a new term for a funding model suddenly sees
it in every proposal and concludes a trend is sweeping the field, though
the rate never moved. What rose was her sensitivity to it. The bias
bites when a freshly noticed pattern is mistaken for a genuinely growing
one.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a pattern you have only just begun to notice starts to feel like a wave worth chasing. Ask for the actual counts over time before and after you started looking. A real trend shows up in the numbers, not only in your heightened awareness of it. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a newly salient pattern may feel like a market shift that the data do not confirm. Before capital follows it, require the measured rate over a fixed period rather than the impression of sudden prevalence. Noticing something more often is not the same as it happening more often. |

**Survivorship Bias**

**What it measures**

Survivorship bias is the error of studying only the cases that made it
through while the failures and closures sit silently outside the data.
The record is written by what survived, and the absent cases never get a
vote. A foundation reviews its thriving grantees to learn what works and
misses that the ones who closed shared the same traits, because they are
simply gone from the file. The lesson drawn from survivors alone can be
exactly backward. The bias bites whenever the missing cases would have
