told a different story.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when conclusions about what works rest only on the programs still standing. Ask deliberately about the ones that closed or dropped out and what they shared. The cases that did not survive often hold the very warning the survivors cannot give. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a strategy drawn from winners alone may ignore the identical bets that failed and vanished. Before capital follows the pattern, require the full set including the losses and an outside-view benchmark. A picture built only from survivors hides the true odds of joining them. |

**Insensitivity to Sample Size**

**What it measures**

Insensitivity to sample size is the mind's habit of trusting a small
batch of cases as if it were as steady as a large one. A handful of
results feels like a verdict when it is barely a hint. A board sees that
4 of 5 pilot participants improved, reads 80%, and treats it as proof,
though five people can swing wildly by chance. The same 80% across 500
people would mean something entirely firmer. The bias bites when the
size of the evidence is ignored and only its direction is read.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this before a small pilot's results are read as a settled answer about a program. Ask how many cases stand behind the percentage and whether that number could turn on luck. A promising rate from a handful of people earns a larger test, not yet a commitment. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a striking rate may rest on too few cases to trust. Before capital follows it, require the sample size and the quantitative support that show whether the result would hold at scale. A small sample can point a direction, but it cannot yet carry the decision. |

**Anecdotal Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The anecdotal fallacy is the habit of letting one vivid story outweigh
the systematic evidence that should govern a judgment. A single tale,
well told, feels more real than a table of numbers. A board hears one
moving account of a client failed by a new intake process and moves to
scrap it, even though the data across hundreds of cases show the process
working well. The story was true and the harm was real, yet it was one
case among many. The bias bites when a single example is asked to do the
work of a whole dataset.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when one powerful story is about to overturn what the wider record shows. Honor the account, then set it beside the current data on how the program performs overall. A story can reveal a real problem worth fixing without proving the whole effort is broken. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a single memorable case is being weighed above the systematic evidence. Before capital responds, require the quantitative support and an outside-view benchmark to show how the one story fits the larger pattern. Vivid does not mean representative. |

**Clustering Illusion**

**What it measures**

The clustering illusion is the mind's insistence on seeing meaningful
patterns in what is merely random scatter. Chance naturally produces
streaks and clumps, and the eye reads design into them. A director
notices that three large gifts arrived in the same week and builds a
theory about donor timing, though random arrivals routinely bunch like
that. The streak felt like a signal. It was the ordinary lumpiness of
luck. The bias bites when a run of events is treated as a pattern before
any test confirms it is more than coincidence.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this before a short run of events becomes the basis for a new theory about a program or its donors. Ask whether the cluster exceeds what chance alone would produce over the same period. A streak deserves a statistical check before it earns a strategy. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a run of results may be random noise dressed as a trend. Before capital follows it, require the quantitative support that distinguishes a real pattern from ordinary scatter. Chance produces clusters on its own, and acting on them as signals is a costly habit. |

**Illusory Correlation**

**What it measures**

Illusory correlation is the perception of a relationship between two
things when the data hold no such link. The mind pairs them, often
because both stand out, and then remembers every match while forgetting
the mismatches. A team comes to believe that grants written in spring
succeed more often, recalling the spring wins vividly and overlooking
the spring losses and the autumn successes. The connection felt obvious.
The full table shows no tie at all. The bias bites when a felt
association is trusted before the counts are checked.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when two factors feel linked in a program's story without a number behind the link. Ask for the full count of cases where they appear together and apart. A relationship that holds up in the complete data is worth acting on, but a felt one is not. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a believed connection between two variables may not exist in the data. Before capital rests on it, require the quantitative support that tests the link across all cases, not only the memorable matches. A pattern that lives in impression and not in the numbers will not pay out. |

**Pareidolia**

**What it measures**

Pareidolia is the mind's reflex to find meaning in noise, reading a
clear shape into data or images that hold none. The brain is built to
detect pattern, and it would rather see one too many than one too few.
An analyst studying a jagged month of donation data sees a definite
cycle and plans around it, though the line is closer to static than to
signal. The shape was compelling and entirely imagined. The bias bites
when ambiguous or random material is read as a clear message.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this before a shape seen in noisy data becomes the reason for a plan. Ask whether the pattern survives a proper test or dissolves into randomness on closer look. Reading a clear message into static leads to plans built on nothing. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a vivid pattern in ambiguous data may be invented by the eye rather than present in the numbers. Before capital follows it, require the quantitative support that separates real structure from noise. The brain offers patterns freely, and acting on the false ones is expensive. |

**Anthropomorphism**

**What it measures**

Anthropomorphism, as an evidence problem, is the habit of assigning
human motives and feelings to systems or markets that have none. The
story of intention is satisfying, and it quietly replaces the harder
search for real causes. A board says the market punished us or the
funder lost faith in us, as if these were persons with grudges, when the
truth lay in interest rates or a shifted strategy. The human framing
felt explanatory and explained nothing. The bias bites when imagined
intent stands in for evidence about how a system actually behaves.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when an explanation leans on what a funder or a system wanted rather than on what actually drove the result. Ask for the mechanism behind the outcome and the data that support it. Naming an intention can feel like understanding while leaving the real cause untouched. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that talk of what the market or a competitor intended may be standing in for analysis of how it works. Before capital responds, require the quantitative support and the actual mechanism rather than a tale of motive. Systems do not hold grudges, and treating them as if they do hides the levers that matter. |

**Picture Superiority Effect**

**What it measures**

The picture superiority effect is the way images lodge in memory more
firmly than words and then sway judgment beyond what their evidence
deserves. A photograph outlasts a paragraph, and that staying power can
be mistaken for weight. A board remembers one striking image from a site
visit long after the evaluation report has faded, and gives the picture
the deciding vote. The image carried feeling, not data. The bias bites
when something is believed more strongly because it was seen rather than
because it was shown to be true.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memorable image is carrying more weight in a decision than the written evidence behind it. Set the picture beside the current data and ask which one actually measures the outcome. A photograph can move the heart without proving the case. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a vivid visual may be steering judgment past the figures. Before capital follows it, require the quantitative support and weigh the image as illustration rather than proof. What was seen lingers longest, but it is not always what should decide. |

**Humor Effect**

**What it measures**

The humor effect is the extra grip that amusing material holds on memory
and favor, so a funny point is recalled and trusted more than a dull one
of equal substance. Laughter makes a claim stick, and stickiness gets
mistaken for merit. A board remembers the consultant's witty line about
a program far better than the sober analysis beside it, and leans toward
the position that made them smile. The joke earned attention, not
accuracy. The bias bites when how entertaining a claim is shapes how
persuasive it feels.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memorable, amusing pitch is winning a decision that the quieter evidence does not support. Ask for the same case stated plainly and judged on its data. A point that only persuades when it is funny has not yet earned the vote. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a charming presentation may be carrying a claim past its actual support. Before capital follows it, require the quantitative backing stripped of the performance. Wit makes a message stick, but it does not make it correct. |

**Von Restorff Effect**

**What it measures**

The Von Restorff effect is the way a distinctive item stands out from
its neighbors and then claims more weight in later judgment than its
content warrants. What differs is what we remember, and memory lends it
undue authority. Among twenty even line items in a budget review, the
one printed in bold or phrased oddly is the one the board recalls and
debates, while the larger ordinary figures pass unexamined. Its
prominence was visual, not substantive. The bias bites when standing out
is confused with mattering most.

**How it is calculated**

r_EQ = w_ββ + w_m(1 − d_disconfirm) + w_ω(1 − ω_outside) + w_q(1 −
q_quant) + w_ττ_stale + w_s(1 − d_source) + w_χ(1 − χ)

Operational reading: Score BQC = 0.20\*z(evidence imbalance) +
0.18\*z(1 - disconfirming evidence) + 0.16\*z(1 - outside-view
coverage) + 0.16\*z(1 - quantitative support) + 0.12\*z(evidence
staleness) + 0.10\*z(1 - source diversity) + 0.08\*z(1 - processing
completeness). The bias is flagged when the evidence ledger shows the
named imbalance.

The equation adds up the ways a record can lean toward what someone
already believed, with each weight set in advance. r_EQ is the
evidence-quality risk score. β is the evidence-balance gap. d_disconfirm
is the disconfirming-evidence indicator. ω_outside is the outside-view
coverage. q_quant is the quantitative support. τ_stale is the freshness
penalty for stale evidence. d_source is the source diversity. χ is the
processing completeness. Each w\_ is a pre-set weight.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when one item stands out in a review for how it looks rather than for how much it matters. Ask whether the attention it draws matches its real weight in the program or budget. The figures that decide an organization's health are often the plain ones that never caught the eye. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this flag warns that a distinctive item may be commanding scrutiny out of proportion to its importance. Before capital follows that focus, weigh each element by its actual magnitude rather than its prominence. The most consequential numbers are frequently the unremarkable ones that no one paused on. |

**Rationale-Language Heuristics**

*These entries read the words of a written rationale for the
fingerprints of bias. Each one names a pattern of language, such as flat
certainty or borrowed authority, that tends to mark a judgment built on
something other than evidence. The shared score counts those marks
against the credit a document earns for naming its own uncertainty.*

**Anchoring Bias**

**What it measures**

Anchoring bias is the pull of the first number or label a writer meets,
which quietly drags every later estimate toward itself. You see it in
the words of a rationale when an early figure becomes the gravitational
center of the argument and the prose keeps circling back to it. A
program officer who opens a memo by stating that last year's renewal
came in at 92% will tend to write the rest of the case as if 92% were
the natural truth, even when the new evidence points lower. The bias
bites hardest when that opening anchor is stated as settled fact and
never reopened.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag anchoring bias when a rationale leans on the named
pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, reach for this when a board packet or staff memo describes risk or the people served in flat, certain language built around one early number. When the anchoring pattern shows up, ask the writer to set the opening figure aside and rebuild the claim from current measured evidence and plainer wording. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a strategy memo or investor narrative carries strong conviction yet little that can be tested, especially when the whole case orbits a single founding number. When the pattern appears, ask for the underlying assumptions, a forecast range, and a named owner who will check the claim against what actually happens. |

**Availability Heuristic**

**What it measures**

The availability heuristic is the habit of judging how likely or how
important something is by how easily an example springs to mind. In a
written rationale it shows up as vivid recent stories standing in for
the wider record. After one donor publicly walks away, a development
memo may declare that major gifts are collapsing, drawing the whole
conclusion from the single case that is freshest in memory. The bias
bites when the easy-to-recall instance is treated as representative of a
pattern it does not actually measure.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the availability heuristic when a rationale leans
on the named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a packet or memo argues from one memorable episode rather than the full run of cases. When the availability pattern appears, ask for the base rate across all comparable events and for plainer, measured wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a memo or investor update reasons from a single recent win or loss that happens to be top of mind. When the pattern shows, ask for the full distribution behind the story, a forecast range, and a named owner who will test the claim against the record. |

**Mere Exposure Effect**

**What it measures**

The mere exposure effect is the way repetition alone breeds liking, so a
claim feels more valid simply because we have read it many times. In a
rationale it surfaces as a phrase that has hardened into received wisdom
through sheer repetition rather than fresh proof. A board that has heard
for five straight years that a flagship program is the heart of the
mission may keep writing it that way long after the evidence stopped
supporting it. The bias bites when familiarity is mistaken for
confirmation and no new evidence is asked for.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the mere exposure effect when a rationale leans on
the named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a long-repeated belief about a program or population is stated as fact without recent supporting evidence. When the pattern appears, ask for current measured results and for plainer wording that respects the people described. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a familiar internal slogan stands in for analysis in a strategy memo or investor narrative. When the pattern shows, ask for fresh assumptions, a forecast range, and a named owner who will retest the worn claim against new data. |

**Negativity Bias**

**What it measures**

Negativity bias is the mind's tendency to give bad news more weight and
memory than good or neutral news of equal size. In a rationale it
appears as prose where a single setback colors the entire assessment
while comparable successes barely register. A quarterly review that
spends four paragraphs on one failed pilot and one sentence on three
programs that met their targets is reading the record through this tilt.
The bias bites when the alarming detail crowds out an honest accounting
of what went right.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag negativity bias when a rationale leans on the named
pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo dwells on one failure while passing over results of equal importance. When the pattern appears, ask for a balanced ledger of outcomes and for plainer, measured language about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a strategy memo or investor update lets a single bad quarter overwhelm the wider trend. When the pattern shows, ask for the full set of results weighted fairly, a forecast range, and a named owner to keep score over time. |

**Framing Effect**

**What it measures**

The framing effect is the shift in judgment that comes from how the same
facts are dressed, as a gain or a loss, a risk or an opportunity. In a
rationale it betrays itself when one presentation of a number is chosen
and the equivalent one is quietly left out. A grant memo that calls a
program a 90% success reads very differently from one that calls it a
10% failure, though both describe the identical result. The bias bites
when the chosen frame does the persuading that the evidence alone could
not.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the framing effect when a rationale leans on the
named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a packet states a result in only its flattering form. When the pattern appears, ask to see the same number framed both ways and to settle on plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when an investor narrative or strategy memo leans on a favorable framing to carry a weak claim. When the pattern shows, ask for the neutral statement of the same facts, a forecast range, and a named owner who will test it. |

**Contrast Effect**

**What it measures**

The contrast effect is the way an option looks better or worse depending
on whatever sat beside it, rather than on its own merits. In a rationale
it shows up when an argument leans on comparison to a nearby alternative
instead of a fixed standard. A vendor proposal can be praised as the
strong choice only because it followed a weaker one in the review, with
the prose never measuring it against an absolute bar. The bias bites
when the neighbor, not the criteria, sets the verdict.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the contrast effect when a rationale leans on the
named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a choice is justified mainly by how it compares to a weaker option placed next to it. When the pattern appears, ask for the fixed criteria the option must meet on its own and for plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a memo sells a deal or product by contrast with a poor alternative rather than against a real bar. When the pattern shows, ask for the absolute standard, a forecast range, and a named owner who will judge it on its own. |

**Distinction Bias**

**What it measures**

Distinction bias is the trap of side-by-side comparison, where small
differences loom large in the moment of choosing yet fade once the
choice is lived with. In a rationale it appears as prose that magnifies
a gap between two options that will barely matter in daily use. A memo
comparing two case-management systems may dwell on a minor feature
difference that no caseworker will notice after the first week. The bias
bites when the comparison itself inflates a distinction the real world
will flatten.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag distinction bias when a rationale leans on the
named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a comparison memo treats a small feature gap as decisive. When the pattern appears, ask whether the difference will still matter in everyday operation and request plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a product or vendor comparison inflates a minor distinction into a strategic one. When the pattern shows, ask how the gap behaves in real use, request a forecast range, and name an owner to verify it. |

**Narrative Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The narrative fallacy is our hunger to read a clean story into events
and then mistake that story for proof. A rationale falls into it when a
smooth chain of cause and effect is offered as if the very smoothness
were evidence. A memo may explain a successful campaign as the
inevitable result of one bold decision, when chance and a dozen
unmentioned factors did much of the work. The bias bites when the
coherence of the telling stands in for any test of whether the story is
true.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the narrative fallacy when a rationale leans on the
named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo offers a tidy cause-and-effect story as if the neatness proved the case. When the pattern appears, ask what evidence would break the story and request plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a strategy memo or investor narrative explains success through one clean storyline. When the pattern shows, ask for the alternative explanations, a forecast range, and a named owner who will test the account against data. |

**Fundamental Attribution Error**

**What it measures**

The fundamental attribution error is the habit of blaming a person's
character for an outcome while ignoring the situation that shaped it. In
a rationale it shows up when failure is pinned on someone's disposition
and the surrounding constraints go unmentioned. A review that says a
program manager missed the target because she lacks discipline, while
saying nothing about the budget cut she absorbed, is reading character
into circumstance. The bias bites when the words reach for a personal
flaw before they reach for the conditions of the work.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the fundamental attribution error when a rationale
leans on the named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support
it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo explains a shortfall by naming a person's character rather than the conditions they faced. When the pattern appears, ask what situational pressures were present and request plainer, fairer wording about the people involved. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a review blames an individual's traits for a result that the market or budget largely set. When the pattern shows, ask for the situational factors, a forecast range, and a named owner who will weigh both fairly. |

**Halo Effect**

**What it measures**

The halo effect is the glow that one good quality casts over judgments
it has nothing to do with, so a strength in one area makes everything
else look strong too. In a rationale it appears when a person or
organization is trusted on a new question simply because they impressed
us elsewhere. A board may approve a partner's budget plan with little
scrutiny because that partner ran a brilliant gala last year. The bias
bites when a reputation earned in one place is spent as evidence in
another.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the halo effect when a rationale leans on the named
pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a strong reputation in one area is used to wave through a judgment in another. When the pattern appears, ask for evidence specific to the question at hand and request plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a leader's past success buys an untested plan an easy pass. When the pattern shows, ask for evidence tied to this decision, a forecast range, and a named owner who will judge it on its own merits. |

**Gambler's Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The gambler's fallacy is the belief that chance keeps a ledger and owes
us a correction, so a run of one result makes the opposite feel overdue.
In a rationale it shows up when a writer treats independent events as if
they were balancing each other out. A development memo might argue that
after three lean years a strong fundraising year is due, as though the
calendar were settling a debt. The bias bites when the words assume that
randomness has a memory it does not have.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the gambler's fallacy when a rationale leans on the
named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo argues that a good year is owed after a string of poor ones. When the pattern appears, ask what real driver would change the odds and request plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a forecast assumes that chance must even out after a streak. When the pattern shows, ask for the actual mechanism behind any expected change, a forecast range, and a named owner to track it. |

**Stereotyping**

**What it measures**

Stereotyping is judging a person by the category they belong to instead
of the evidence in front of you. In a rationale it surfaces when a
sweeping group generalization stands in for individual facts. A memo
that assumes a younger applicant will not stay long, or that a large
funder will always be slow, is letting the label decide before the
record is read. The bias bites when the category does the reasoning that
only the individual case can honestly support.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag stereotyping when a rationale leans on the named
pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo judges a person or group by a category rather than their own record. When the pattern appears, ask for the individual evidence and insist on plainer, more respectful wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a hiring or customer judgment rests on a group generalization. When the pattern shows, ask for the case-specific evidence, a forecast range where a number is claimed, and a named owner to verify it. |

**Curse of Knowledge**

**What it measures**

The curse of knowledge is the expert's blind spot, the inability to
imagine not knowing what you already know. In a rationale it shows up as
prose that assumes the reader shares context or terms that were never
spelled out. A finance lead may write that the reserve ratio is
obviously fine, forgetting that most board members have never been told
what ratio counts as fine or why. The bias bites when the writer's
fluency leaves the reader unable to check the claim.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the curse of knowledge when a rationale leans on
the named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo assumes knowledge the board has not been given. When the pattern appears, ask the writer to state the missing context plainly so the people served and the numbers can be understood by everyone at the table. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a technical memo skips the context a board needs to test it. When the pattern shows, ask for the unstated assumptions written out, a forecast range, and a named owner who can answer questions in plain terms. |

**Placebo Effect**

**What it measures**

The placebo effect is improvement that flows from the expectation of
help rather than from the help itself. In a rationale it appears when
gains are credited to an intervention without separating what the
intervention actually did from what hope and attention did. A memo may
report that a new coaching program lifted morale, when the simple fact
of being noticed could have produced the same lift. The bias bites when
the words claim a cause that no comparison has yet isolated.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the placebo effect when a rationale leans on the
named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo credits a program for a gain that attention alone might explain. When the pattern appears, ask for a comparison group or baseline and request plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a memo attributes improvement to a new initiative without isolating its real effect. When the pattern shows, ask for a control or baseline, a forecast range, and a named owner who will separate signal from expectation. |

**Mood-Congruent Memory Bias**

**What it measures**

Mood-congruent memory bias is the way our present feelings tint the
past, making memories that match the mood easier to recall and heavier
in the weighing. In a rationale it shows up when the emotional
temperature of the moment shapes which history the writer reaches for. A
leader writing after a hard week may recall only the failures of a
program and write its whole history as a struggle. The bias bites when
today's mood, not the full record, selects the evidence on the page.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag mood-congruent memory bias when a rationale leans
on the named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo's tone seems to be choosing its memories, recalling only what matches the writer's current mood. When the pattern appears, ask for the full record across good and hard periods and request plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a memo written in a low or high moment recalls a one-sided history. When the pattern shows, ask for the complete record, a forecast range, and a named owner who will assemble the evidence apart from the day's mood. |

**Empathy Gap**

**What it measures**

The empathy gap is our poor reach across emotional states, the
difficulty of feeling now how we or others will want things in a
different state. In a rationale it shows up when a calm planner sets
policy for people who will be in crisis, or a comfortable board decides
for those under strain. A memo written in a quiet office may set a
help-line wait time that feels reasonable on paper and unbearable to a
caller in distress. The bias bites when the writer's present state
stands in for the very different state of the people the decision will
touch.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the empathy gap when a rationale leans on the named
pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a calm-state decision sets terms for people who will meet them under stress. When the pattern appears, ask how the choice feels from the other state and request plainer, more humane wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a policy set in comfort governs customers or staff under pressure. When the pattern shows, ask for evidence from the affected state, a forecast range, and a named owner who will check the decision against real experience. |

**Normalcy Bias**

**What it measures**

Normalcy bias is the quiet assumption that tomorrow will look like
yesterday, which dulls our reading of warning signs. In a rationale it
appears when prose waves off early signals of trouble because the recent
past has been steady. A memo may note a sudden drop in a major grant and
then conclude that funding remains stable, because stability is all the
writer has lately known. The bias bites when the comfort of routine is
mistaken for a forecast.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag normalcy bias when a rationale leans on the named
pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo dismisses a warning sign because conditions have been calm. When the pattern appears, ask what would have to be true for the danger to be real and request plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a memo assumes market or funding stability in the face of an early shock. When the pattern shows, ask for a stress case, a forecast range, and a named owner who will watch the signal rather than the routine. |

**Declinism**

**What it measures**

Declinism is the warm glow we cast on the past paired with a harsh light
on the present, so that today's conditions read as a falling-off from a
better age. In a rationale it shows up when current results are framed
as decay and an idealized earlier era is treated as the true baseline. A
memo may lament that the organization has lost the spirit it had a
decade ago, measuring now against a golden version of then that may
never have existed. The bias bites when nostalgia, not the record, sets
the standard the present is judged against.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag declinism when a rationale leans on the named
pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a memo frames the present as decline measured against an idealized past. When the pattern appears, ask for the actual figures from both periods and request plainer wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a narrative treats current performance as decay against a rosy memory of earlier years. When the pattern shows, ask for the comparable numbers across both periods, a forecast range, and a named owner who will check the trend against the record. |

**Planning Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The planning fallacy is the steady optimism that makes us underestimate
how long a task will take and how much it will cost, even when our own
history says otherwise. In a rationale it shows up when a timeline or
budget is stated with confidence and no nod to the last project that ran
over. A memo may promise a new database in three months, though the last
two such efforts took nine. The bias bites when the plan describes the
best case and quietly calls it the expected one.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score counts weighted phrase-family hits for every 1,000 words of
rationale, adds deterministic modal language, affective intensity, and
frame repetition, then subtracts credit for documented uncertainty and
cited evidence. Flag the planning fallacy when a rationale leans on the
named pattern without enough qualifying evidence to support it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk.

h_k is the count of phrase-family hits.

w_k is the weight on each phrase family.

W_words is the rationale word count.

μ_modal is the intensity of deterministic modal language.

a_affect is the affective intensity.

Δφ_frame is the frame repetition and sensitivity.

u_uncertain is the documented uncertainty.

c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.

e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this when a plan states a confident schedule or budget with no reference to past overruns. When the pattern appears, ask for the record of similar projects and request plainer wording about what the people served can actually expect. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this when a memo presents a best-case timeline or cost as the expected one. When the pattern shows, ask for the history of comparable efforts, a forecast range, and a named owner who will hold the estimate to that record. |

**Illusion of Validity**

**What it measures**

The illusion of validity is the confidence a writer feels in a judgment
simply because the story behind it hangs together, even when that story
predicts almost nothing. The clue lives in the words. A rationale that
says a program "will clearly succeed" because every detail fits a
familiar pattern, yet offers no record of how similar bets actually
turned out, is leaning on coherence in place of evidence. The mind
mistakes a tidy narrative for a tested one, and the prose reads as
certain long before the facts have earned it.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags the
illusion of validity when a rationale uses confident phrasing without
the qualifying evidence to back it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a board packet or staff rationale describes a program's odds in the language of certainty while the file holds little measurable evidence. When the illusion of validity shows up, ask the author to restate the claim in plainer words and to point to the past cases that actually support it. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this in a strategy memo or investor narrative that sounds sure of itself yet offers nothing a skeptic could test. When the illusion of validity shows up, turn the confident sentence into a stated assumption with a forecast range and a clear test that could prove it wrong. |

**Confabulation**

**What it measures**

Confabulation is the mind quietly building a reasonable-sounding
explanation after the fact to cover a gap in memory or evidence, then
presenting it as the real reason. In a written rationale it appears as a
smooth, complete account that no one could have actually held at the
moment of decision. A memo that explains a hire by reciting a clean
chain of reasons, none of which were written down before the choice was
made, is filling a hole with story. The danger is that the account feels
honest to its author, because the mind does not flag the seam where
memory ended and invention began.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags
confabulation when a rationale reads as a smooth after-the-fact account
with no contemporaneous record behind it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale tells a complete and confident story about why a decision was made but no notes from the time of the decision exist to match it. When confabulation shows up, ask for the record that was written before the choice rather than the explanation assembled afterward. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a post-mortem or board narrative reconstructs a clean reason for a market move that no document foresaw. When confabulation shows up, separate what was genuinely known at the time from the tidy account written later, and weigh the decision on the first. |

**Masked Man Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The masked man fallacy is the slip of treating two names for the same
thing as if they must be two different things. In a rationale it appears
when one description carries weight that another, equally true, would
not. A board note that approves "a strategic regional partner" while a
separate paragraph warns against "the unproven vendor in that region"
may be describing one company twice, with the wording alone deciding the
verdict. The mind responds to the label rather than the object, and the
prose hides the fact that the two are one.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags the
masked man fallacy when a rationale lets two labels for one thing pull
in opposite directions.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a packet praises one description of a partner or program while a different paragraph distrusts what is quietly the same thing under another name. When the masked man fallacy shows up, ask the author to use one consistent name and judge the object itself. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo treats "our incumbent supplier" and "the high-risk single source" as two separate matters that are in fact identical. When the masked man fallacy shows up, collapse the descriptions into one and reassess the claim against the actual entity. |

**Hot-Hand Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The hot-hand fallacy is the belief that a recent run of success makes
more success likely, as if luck or momentum had a memory. In a rationale
it reads as a streak doing the work of evidence. A memo that recommends
doubling a campaign because "the last three quarters all beat target"
treats a short, recent run as proof of a durable rate. The mind feels
the warmth of the streak and forgets to ask whether the underlying odds
ever changed.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags the
hot-hand fallacy when a rationale leans on a recent streak in place of a
stable base rate.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale predicts continued success from a recent string of good results without checking the longer record. When the hot-hand fallacy shows up, ask for the base rate across many cycles rather than the warmth of the last few. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo scales a bet because the most recent quarters ran hot, with no test of whether the underlying odds have shifted. When the hot-hand fallacy shows up, ask whether anything real changed beneath the streak before committing more capital. |

**Group Attribution Error**

**What it measures**

The group attribution error is the move that treats a few members of a
group as the whole, or treats a group's decision as the choice of every
member inside it. In a rationale it appears whenever a single trait
stands in for a population. A note that says "that department always
resists change" because two people pushed back is letting a pair speak
for many. The mind reaches for a clean group label, and the prose erases
the variety that actually sits inside the group.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags the
group attribution error when a rationale generalizes from a few members
to an entire group.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale assigns one attitude to a whole team or population of people served based on a handful of cases. When the group attribution error shows up, ask for the spread within the group and a count of how many cases actually support the claim. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo treats a market segment or a division as a single mind, judging all of it by a few loud examples. When the group attribution error shows up, ask the author to show the variation inside the group before acting on the generalization. |

**Essentialism**

**What it measures**

Essentialism is the habit of treating a category as if it carried a
fixed inner nature, so that members are judged by what they supposedly
are rather than how they behave in context. In a rationale it shows up
as language of permanence around a label. A memo that dismisses a
proposal because "that kind of program never works here" treats a
category as having a built-in destiny. The mind prefers a stable essence
to a shifting set of conditions, and the words quietly close off the
chance that context could change the result.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags
essentialism when a rationale fixes a category's fate in permanent
language without weighing context.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale treats a population or a program type as having a fixed character that decides the outcome in advance. When essentialism shows up, ask what conditions would have to change the result, and require respectful wording about the people involved. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo writes off a market, a technology, or a customer type as simply being a certain way. When essentialism shows up, ask the author to name the specific conditions behind the claim rather than resting on a fixed label. |

**Just-World Hypothesis**

**What it measures**

The just-world hypothesis is the comforting belief that outcomes are
deserved, which leads a writer to credit success to virtue and failure
to fault while the role of luck and circumstance fades from view. In a
rationale it reads as moral language attached to results. A note that
explains a grantee's struggle by saying they "simply didn't want it
enough" skips past the funding gaps and access barriers that shaped the
outcome. The mind would rather live in a fair world than a partly random
one, and the prose rewards the winners and blames the losers without
checking what they were given to start with.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags the
just-world hypothesis when a rationale reads outcomes as deserved and
underweights luck and structure.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale treats a person's or program's result as plainly earned and skips the access and funding behind it. When the just-world hypothesis shows up, ask for the conditions each party started from and require fair wording about the people served. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo credits a win purely to skill or blames a loss purely on weak execution, ignoring market timing and starting position. When the just-world hypothesis shows up, ask the author to account for luck and circumstance alongside merit. |

**Loss Aversion**

**What it measures**

Loss aversion is the way a possible loss weighs heavier on the mind than
an equal possible gain, so the fear of giving something up can freeze a
decision that the numbers would otherwise favor. In a rationale it shows
up as language that dwells on what might be lost while the matching
upside goes nearly unmentioned. A memo that warns at length about "the
risk of losing our current donors" but barely names the larger base a
change could win is feeling a 100-dollar loss as if it hurt twice as
much as a 100-dollar gain. The mind guards what it holds, and the prose
tilts toward staying put.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags loss
aversion when a rationale magnifies a possible loss while leaving the
