matching gain almost unspoken.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale dwells on what a change might cost while saying little about what it could win. When loss aversion shows up, ask for the gains and the losses on the same scale so the choice rests on the full picture. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo resists a pivot mainly out of fear of losing current revenue, with the larger possible upside left vague. When loss aversion shows up, ask the author to size the gain and the loss in the same units before deciding. |

**Sunk Cost Fallacy**

**What it measures**

The sunk cost fallacy is the pull to keep going on a failing course
because so much money and effort has already gone into it, as if past
spending could be recovered by spending more. In a rationale it reads as
the past justifying the future. A memo that argues to continue a project
because "we have already put two years and 400,000 dollars into it" is
letting money that is already gone decide what comes next. The mind
hates to admit a loss, and the prose treats the past investment as a
reason rather than the irrelevance it has become.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags the sunk
cost fallacy when a rationale uses past investment to justify continuing
rather than judging the choice forward.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale defends staying the course mainly by pointing to the time and money already spent. When the sunk cost fallacy shows up, ask the author to judge the decision only on the future costs and benefits from today onward. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo argues to keep funding a program because of how much capital is already in it. When the sunk cost fallacy shows up, ask whether the same investment would be made fresh today, knowing what is now known. |

**Endowment Effect**

**What it measures**

The endowment effect is the extra value a thing takes on simply because
it already belongs to the organization, so a held asset feels worth more
than the same asset would if someone were offered it new. In a rationale
it shows up as ownership doing the arguing. A memo that defends keeping
an aging program because "it has been part of who we are for 15 years"
is pricing identity into a decision that should turn on present worth.
The mind clings to what it owns, and the prose dresses attachment up as
judgment.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags the
endowment effect when a rationale values something more highly only
because the organization already owns it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale defends a program or asset mainly because it is already part of the organization's holdings or identity. When the endowment effect shows up, ask what the same thing would be worth if it were being acquired today. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo holds onto a product line or business unit because it has always been ours rather than for its present value. When the endowment effect shows up, ask the author to value it as an outside buyer would. |

**Zero-Risk Bias**

**What it measures**

Zero-risk bias is the strong pull to wipe out one small risk completely,
even when the same effort spent elsewhere would cut far more total
danger. In a rationale it reads as the comfort of a clean zero. A memo
that spends heavily to remove the last 2% of risk from one minor
process, while a larger hazard nearby goes unaddressed, is buying the
feeling of certainty rather than the most safety. The mind loves the
tidy zero, and the prose celebrates eliminating a risk without asking
whether it was the risk most worth eliminating.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags
zero-risk bias when a rationale prizes eliminating one small risk over
reducing the larger total.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale prizes driving one minor risk to zero while a bigger hazard goes unaddressed. When zero-risk bias shows up, ask which choice cuts the most total risk for the people served, not which one feels cleanest. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo spends to perfect the safety of a small exposure while a larger one sits open. When zero-risk bias shows up, ask the author to rank spending by how much total risk each dollar removes. |

**Status Quo Bias**

**What it measures**

Status quo bias is the quiet preference for things as they are, because
the current arrangement is familiar, already approved, and easier to
keep than to change. In a rationale it shows up as the present being
treated as the safe default that needs no defense. A memo that keeps a
vendor because "this is how we have always done it" puts the burden of
proof entirely on change while the existing choice escapes the same
scrutiny. The mind treats the known as safer than the unknown, and the
prose lets inertia pass for a decision.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags status
quo bias when a rationale defends the current state mainly because it is
familiar or already authorized.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale keeps a current arrangement chiefly because it is familiar and already in place. When status quo bias shows up, ask the author to hold the existing choice to the same test as any proposed change. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo defends an incumbent supplier or process simply because it is the way things run now. When status quo bias shows up, ask for the same evidence from the status quo that a new option would have to provide. |

**Pro-Innovation Bias**

**What it measures**

Pro-innovation bias is the glow that surrounds a new idea or program
because it is new, with the costs and harms of adopting it quietly
discounted. In a rationale it reads as novelty doing the persuading. A
memo that urges buying a fresh platform because it is "cutting-edge"
while skipping the training time, switching costs, and chance of failure
is letting newness stand in for worth. The mind is drawn to the next
thing, and the prose sells the promise while passing over the bill that
comes with it.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags
pro-innovation bias when a rationale favors something for its novelty
while underweighting its costs.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale champions a new program or tool largely because it is new, with adoption costs barely named. When pro-innovation bias shows up, ask for the full cost of switching and the risk of failure beside the promised benefit. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo pushes a novel technology or product on the strength of its novelty while the harms and switching costs stay vague. When pro-innovation bias shows up, ask the author to price the downside as carefully as the upside. |

**Commission Bias**

**What it measures**

Commission bias is the urge to act rather than wait, because doing
something looks more responsible and decisive than holding still, even
when holding still is wiser. In a rationale it shows up as language that
treats action itself as the proof of leadership. A memo that urges "we
must do something now" about a slow decline, without weighing whether
the move helps, is mistaking motion for progress. The mind would rather
be seen acting than seen waiting, and the prose rewards the visible step
over the patient one.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags
commission bias when a rationale treats acting as inherently better than
waiting without testing whether the action helps.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale presses for immediate action mainly because doing something feels responsible. When commission bias shows up, ask whether the proposed move actually improves the outcome compared with waiting and watching. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo demands a decisive move under pressure without showing that action beats patience. When commission bias shows up, ask the author to weigh the cost of acting against the cost of holding before committing. |

**Omission Bias**

**What it measures**

Omission bias is the mirror of the urge to act. It is the preference for
doing nothing, because harm that follows from a deliberate move feels
more blameworthy than the same harm that follows from standing aside. In
a rationale it reads as inaction framed as the safe and innocent path. A
memo that avoids a needed change because "if we act and it goes wrong,
that is on us" treats the damage of inaction as if it carried no blame
at all. The mind fears being the visible cause of harm, and the prose
lets a costly silence pass as caution.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags omission
bias when a rationale treats inaction as safer or less blameworthy than
action without weighing the harm of doing nothing.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale favors doing nothing because action would feel more blameworthy if it went wrong. When omission bias shows up, ask the author to weigh the harm of inaction on the people served as seriously as the harm of acting. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo defends standing still because a failed move would draw blame, while the cost of doing nothing goes uncounted. When omission bias shows up, ask the author to put the price of inaction beside the price of action. |

**Optimism Bias**

**What it measures**

Optimism bias is the habit of expecting the future to turn out better
than base rates and past experience would warrant. It shows up in the
words of a rationale as flat certainty, a memo that says a launch will
succeed rather than that it is likely to. A development plan that
pencils in best-case revenue for 3 years running, while every prior year
fell short, is optimism bias written down.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

Read plainly, the score counts weighted bias-language hits per 1,000
words and adds deterministic and emotional language, then subtracts the
credit a rationale earns for naming its own uncertainty and citing
evidence.

w_k is the weight of phrase family k. h_k is the count of hits in that
family. W_words is the document length in words. μ_modal is the level of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the amount of frame repetition. u_uncertain and c_counter
and e_cited are the offsetting credits for stated uncertainty and cited
evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, watch for this when a board packet describes a risky plan in language that leaves no room for failure. The flag asks the author to state plainly what would have to go right and how likely that really is. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the flag rises when a strategy memo carries strong language but weak testability. It turns a confident narrative back into a checkable claim before capital follows it. |

**Decoy Effect**

**What it measures**

The decoy effect is what happens when adding a clearly worse option
changes which of the remaining options people prefer. A budget paper
that presents a deliberately weak third choice is steering the reader
toward a target, not informing the decision. The tell in the language is
an option that exists only to make another one look reasonable.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

Read plainly, the score counts weighted bias-language hits per 1,000
words and adds deterministic and emotional language, then subtracts the
credit a rationale earns for naming its own uncertainty and citing
evidence.

w_k is the weight of phrase family k. h_k is the count of hits in that
family. W_words is the document length in words. μ_modal is the level of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the amount of frame repetition. u_uncertain and c_counter
and e_cited are the offsetting credits for stated uncertainty and cited
evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, look for this when a recommendation arrives flanked by an option no one would seriously pick. The flag asks whether each choice on the page is real or there to tilt the room. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the flag rises in pricing and vendor papers where one alternative seems planted. It restores a fair comparison before the preferred option is waved through. |

**IKEA Effect**

**What it measures**

The IKEA effect is the way people overvalue what they helped build,
because their own labor is bound up in it. A team that spent a year on a
tool will defend it past the point the evidence allows. In a written
rationale it reads as pride standing in for proof, warm language about
the work itself rather than cold language about its results.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

Read plainly, the score counts weighted bias-language hits per 1,000
words and adds deterministic and emotional language, then subtracts the
credit a rationale earns for naming its own uncertainty and citing
evidence.

w_k is the weight of phrase family k. h_k is the count of hits in that
family. W_words is the document length in words. μ_modal is the level of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the amount of frame repetition. u_uncertain and c_counter
and e_cited are the offsetting credits for stated uncertainty and cited
evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, raise this when a proposal to keep a homegrown program leans on the effort already spent rather than on what it delivers. The flag returns the question to outcomes. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the flag rises when a build-versus-buy memo defends an internal tool by the work invested in it. It separates sunk effort from present value. |

**Actor-Observer Bias**

**What it measures**

Actor-observer bias is the double standard we apply when explaining
behavior. We explain our own missteps by pointing to the circumstances
we faced, while we explain the same missteps in others by pointing to
their character. In a rationale it shows up as that split in the
wording. A review note that excuses an internal delay as "the timeline
was impossible" but reads a partner's identical delay as "they are
simply unreliable" is judging one situation and one soul. The mind sees
its own context but only the other person's nature, and the prose
quietly grades on two scales.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up the weighted phrase-family hits per 1,000 words, the
force of deterministic words, the heat of the language, and the
repetition of one frame, then subtracts the credit a writer earns for
naming uncertainty and citing real evidence. A high score flags
actor-observer bias when a rationale explains its own conduct by
circumstance and others' conduct by character.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the frame repetition or sensitivity. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the count of counterfactuals.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the executive director and board, read this when a rationale forgives the organization's own slip as bad circumstances but reads the same slip in a partner or person served as a flaw of character. When actor-observer bias shows up, ask the author to apply one standard and to use fair wording about people. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and the board, watch for this when a memo blames internal misses on conditions but blames a competitor's or vendor's misses on who they are. When actor-observer bias shows up, ask the author to judge both by the same rule, weighing circumstance and conduct alike. |

**Risk Compensation**

**What it measures**

Risk compensation is the way a stated safeguard quietly licenses bolder
action in the words that follow it. The bias shows up in a rationale
when a protection is named and then treated as if it has erased the
danger rather than reduced it. A program memo that says "because we
carry liability insurance, we can run the overnight event without extra
staffing" has let the safeguard do the arguing. The insurance is real,
but the sentence leans on it to skip the question of whether the event
is actually safe.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from risk-language phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag risk compensation when a rationale
leans on a named protection without enough qualifying evidence.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, read this flag when a packet justifies a riskier choice by pointing to a control or a reserve fund as if the risk were now gone. Ask the writer to state how much the safeguard actually lowers the exposure and what remains uncovered. The honest answer is usually a range, not a clean all-clear. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, watch for the move where a hedge or a compliance review is cited to wave through a larger bet. The discipline is to name the residual risk in plain numbers and say who owns it if the safeguard underperforms. A protection that lowers exposure by 30% is not a reason to act as though it fell to zero. |

**Hyperbolic Discounting**

**What it measures**

Hyperbolic discounting is the pull toward what pays off now, written
into a rationale as language that shrinks the future. The bias appears
when near-term gains or costs are described in vivid, urgent terms while
the larger long-run consequence is mentioned briefly or not at all. A
budget note that reads "we must cut the training line this quarter to
hit the number, we can rebuild capacity later" has put a sharp present
against a vague "later." The later, left undefined, almost never arrives
on the terms the sentence promised.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from urgency phrase families per 1,000
words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words, the
affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame. It
then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented uncertainty
and cited evidence. Flag hyperbolic discounting when a rationale presses
a present payoff and leaves the long-run cost thin.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag fits the moment a proposal trades a lasting capacity for an immediate relief. Ask for the long-run figure to be stated as plainly as the short-run one, with a date attached to any promised recovery. A cut described as temporary deserves a written test for when it will actually be reversed. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the pattern hides in memos that protect a quarter at the expense of a year. The fix is to put the deferred cost in the same units and on the same page as the present saving. If the later rebuild has no owner and no date, treat it as a cost already incurred. |

**Identifiable Victim Effect**

**What it measures**

The identifiable victim effect is the way a single vivid case can
outweigh a much larger number in the language of a rationale. The bias
shows when one named person or story carries the argument while the
broader population it should be weighed against stays abstract. A grant
rationale that spends three paragraphs on one family and a single line
on the 4,000 people the same money could otherwise reach has let the
story set the priority. The family is real, but the sentence has quietly
demoted the larger group to a statistic.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from vivid-case phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag the identifiable victim effect when
a single story carries weight that the wider numbers do not support.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag belongs in the room when one compelling beneficiary story is steering a funding choice. Honor the story, then ask how the same resources would serve the larger group the rationale barely mentioned. A vivid case is a reason to look closer, not a reason to stop counting. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the same effect appears when one customer anecdote drives a product or market call. Ask for the size of the segment the anecdote represents before the single example becomes the strategy. A memorable account of one user should sit beside the number of users it actually stands for. |

**Forer Effect (Barnum Effect)**

**What it measures**

The Forer effect, also called the Barnum effect, is the readiness to
accept a vague description as if it were precise and tailored. In a
rationale it shows up as sweeping statements that could apply to almost
any organization yet are read as a sharp diagnosis of this one. A
strategy note that says "our culture values excellence and is ready for
the next phase of growth" has said almost nothing testable while
sounding specific. The words feel like insight because they flatter, not
because they describe.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from vague-description phrase families
per 1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal
words, the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single
frame. It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag the Forer effect when general
language is read as a specific finding without evidence to anchor it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag fits a report that praises the organization in terms that would fit any organization. Ask what specific fact would be false if the flattering sentence were wrong. If nothing would change, the sentence is decoration rather than a claim. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, beware investor language and self-assessments that sound bespoke but say almost nothing measurable. Push each glowing phrase toward a number or a falsifiable claim. A description that fits every competitor describes none of them, including you. |

**System Justification**

**What it measures**

System justification is the habit of defending the current arrangement
simply because it exists, written into a rationale as if longevity were
proof. The bias shows when the case for keeping things rests on
tradition or familiarity rather than on whether the arrangement still
works. A board memo that argues "this is how we have always allocated
the budget and it has served us well" has used the past as the whole
argument. That a thing endured tells you it survived, not that it was
ever the best choice.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from status-quo phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag system justification when the
defense of an arrangement rests on its familiarity rather than its
results.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, raise this flag when "we have always done it this way" stands in for a reason. Ask what evidence would justify the arrangement if it were proposed fresh today. A practice worth keeping can survive that question without leaning on its own age. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, watch for strategy that protects an existing model because it is incumbent rather than because it still wins. Ask what a new entrant unburdened by the past would do instead. The honest comparison is to the best alternative, not to the comfort of continuity. |

**Reactance**

**What it measures**

Reactance is the impulse to push back against a recommendation because
it feels like a limit on one's own discretion, and it leaves a mark in
the language of a rationale. The bias shows when an objection focuses on
who is doing the telling or on lost autonomy rather than on the merits
of the proposal. A reviewer note that says "we should not let an outside
model dictate how we run our own programs" has answered the messenger
instead of the evidence. The resistance may be sincere, but the sentence
never engaged the actual claim on the table.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from resistance phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag reactance when a rejection answers
the constraint on autonomy rather than the substance of the
recommendation.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag fits a comment that bristles at being told what to do without addressing whether the advice is right. Separate the question of who decides from the question of what the evidence says. A team can keep its authority and still weigh the recommendation on its merits. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, reactance surfaces when a sound proposal is dismissed because it came from a tool or a rival function. Ask the objector to grant the point of authorship and respond to the substance. The decision can stay in-house while the argument gets a fair hearing. |

**Belief Bias**

**What it measures**

Belief bias is the tendency to judge an argument by whether you like its
conclusion rather than by whether the reasoning holds. In a rationale it
shows when a shaky line of logic passes without challenge because it
lands where the writer already wanted to be. A memo that concludes "the
new site will obviously succeed, so the weak attendance projections must
be too conservative" has bent the evidence to fit the verdict. The
pleasing conclusion has been used to grade the reasoning, which is
exactly backward.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from conclusion-first phrase families
per 1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal
words, the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single
frame. It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag belief bias when a welcome
conclusion is used to excuse weak reasoning.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, raise this flag when an agreeable conclusion seems to carry an argument that would not survive on its own. Ask whether the same logic would be accepted if it pointed the other way. Reasoning that only convinces when you like the answer is not yet reasoning. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, belief bias lets a favored strategy slide past scrutiny its evidence cannot support. Test the argument by imagining it led to a conclusion you dislike. If you would reject it then, the conclusion is doing the work the evidence should be doing. |

**Misinformation Effect**

**What it measures**

The misinformation effect is the way a later, misleading account can
reshape what someone remembers and then reports. In a rationale it shows
when a recollection has clearly absorbed a detail that was introduced
after the fact. A review note that says "as we all agreed in the spring,
the program was already underperforming" may be importing a judgment
that only formed later. The memory feels firsthand, but a piece of it
was planted by what came afterward.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from recollection phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag the misinformation effect when a
remembered account appears to carry detail that was introduced after the
event.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag fits a confident recollection of past consensus that the contemporaneous record may not support. Ask for the minutes or the email from the time rather than the memory of it. What people recall agreeing to often drifts toward what later turned out to be true. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the effect rewrites the history of a decision once its outcome is known. Anchor postmortems to documents written before the result was clear. A clean memory of having seen the risk all along is the warning sign, not the proof. |

**Suggestibility**

**What it measures**

Suggestibility is the way a leading question or a senior voice can shape
what someone reports believing. In a rationale it shows when an account
closely mirrors the wording of the prompt or the boss who asked. A staff
response that echoes the executive's phrasing almost exactly, "yes, the
rollout went smoothly and the team is confident," may be reflecting the
question more than the reality. The answer was available to be given
before the asking was even finished.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from prompt-echo phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag suggestibility when a reported view
mirrors the question or the authority that prompted it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, raise this flag when staff feedback arrives phrased almost as the question was phrased. Gather views in writing and out of the leader's earshot before they harden. A confirmation that only repeats the prompt has told you little you did not already supply. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, suggestibility lets a confident executive unknowingly script the answers they receive. Ask open questions and collect responses before discussion converges. The most useful report is the one that does not sound like the person who requested it. |

**Cryptomnesia**

**What it measures**

Cryptomnesia is the moment a borrowed idea returns feeling brand new
because its origin has slipped from memory. In a rationale it shows when
a proposal is presented as original while plainly tracing an earlier
source. A planning note that offers "a fresh idea, a sliding-scale fee"
when the same approach was piloted and dropped two years earlier has
forgotten its own history. The idea is not new, and the forgetting hides
what was already learned about it.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from originality-claim phrase families
per 1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal
words, the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single
frame. It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag cryptomnesia when an idea is
claimed as new without the history that would place it.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag fits a proposal billed as fresh that the organization has in fact tried before. Ask whether anything like it has been attempted and what happened. Recovering the source also recovers the lessons the first attempt already paid for. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, an idea pitched as novel may be a rerun of a prior effort whose results have been forgotten. Check the archive before funding the rediscovery. Knowing where an idea came from often tells you why it was set aside. |

**Primacy Effect**

**What it measures**

The primacy effect is the outsized hold that whatever comes first keeps
over a judgment. In a rationale it shows when the opening framing
governs the conclusion even after later facts should have moved it. A
board packet that leads with one alarming figure and then reads every
later detail through that alarm has let the first impression set the
verdict. What arrived first was not necessarily what mattered most, yet
it shaped how everything after it was read.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from first-impression phrase families
per 1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal
words, the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single
frame. It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag the primacy effect when the opening
framing carries more weight than later evidence warrants.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, raise this flag when the first item in a packet seems to color everything after it. Try reordering the evidence and ask whether the conclusion still holds. A judgment that depends on what was read first depends on the order, not the facts. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, primacy lets an opening narrative anchor a decision before the full picture lands. Read the strongest counter-evidence first in at least one pass. If the order of presentation can flip the call, the call was never resting on the substance. |

**Recency Effect**

**What it measures**

The recency effect is the outsized hold that whatever came last keeps
over a judgment. In a rationale it shows when the most recent event
dominates the conclusion while the longer record fades. A quarterly
review that pivots its whole assessment on last month's dip, brushing
past two strong years, has let the latest data crowd out the trend. The
most recent number is rarely the most representative one, yet it tends
to speak loudest.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from latest-event phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag the recency effect when the most
recent event carries more weight than the longer record warrants.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag fits a review that swings on the latest month and forgets the years behind it. Ask for the full trend line beside the recent point. One quarter is a data point, not the story. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, recency lets a single recent result, good or bad, reset a judgment it should only adjust. Weigh the latest figure against the longer baseline before acting. A trend earns more trust than the most recent reading taken alone. |

**Implicit Stereotypes**

**What it measures**

Implicit stereotypes are automatic associations that steer a judgment
without the writer ever endorsing them on purpose. In a rationale they
show in word choices that quietly assign traits by group rather than by
the case at hand. A hiring note that calls one candidate "polished and a
natural fit" and another "surprisingly articulate" has let an
association do work the evidence did not. The writer may intend no such
thing, yet the language has already tilted the scale.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from group-association phrase families
per 1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal
words, the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single
frame. It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag implicit stereotypes when language
assigns traits by group rather than by the specific evidence.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, raise this flag when evaluations of people use loaded or uneven language that the facts do not justify. Ask for each judgment to rest on a specific, observable behavior. Descriptions of people deserve the same evidence we demand of any other claim. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, implicit stereotypes shape talent and partnership decisions through wording no one would defend out loud. Hold each characterization to a concrete example before it counts. A word like "fit" should name a behavior, not stand in for an assumption. |

**Leveling and Sharpening**

**What it measures**

Leveling and sharpening is the way a retold account loses its quiet
details while a few features grow louder. In a rationale it shows when a
story has clearly been smoothed into a sharper, simpler version than the
events deserved. A summary that reduces a tangled program failure to
"the vendor let us down" has flattened the caveats and amplified one
cause. The retelling is cleaner than the truth, and the cleanliness is
the distortion.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from simplification phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag leveling and sharpening when a
retold account has been smoothed into a sharper, simpler version than
the facts support.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag fits a clean narrative that has shed the complications of what actually happened. Ask for the details that the tidy version dropped. The caveats that fall away in retelling are often the ones a decision most needs. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, leveling and sharpening turns a messy outcome into a single memorable cause that invites the wrong fix. Go back to the contemporaneous record before accepting the simplified story. A failure with one neat villain usually had several quieter contributors. |

**Source Confusion**

**What it measures**

Source confusion is the error of remembering a claim clearly while
misremembering where it came from or how solid it was. In a rationale it
shows when an assertion is stated with confidence and an authority that
the real source cannot support. A memo that reports "the research shows
our approach is best practice" when the basis was a single conference
remark has upgraded a passing comment into evidence. The claim survived
in memory, but the part that should govern its weight did not.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from attribution phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag source confusion when a claim is
reported with more authority than its actual source can support.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, raise this flag when a confident claim cites "the research" or "the experts" without a traceable source. Ask for the actual reference and check what it really says. A claim is only as strong as the source it can name, not the certainty in its voice. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, source confusion lets a half-remembered figure or study harden into the basis for a decision. Require the citation before the claim carries weight. Where an assertion came from determines how much it should move you, so the source has to be findable. |

**False Memory**

**What it measures**

False memory is the recollection of an event or detail that did not
happen as remembered, held with full confidence. In a rationale it shows
when a writer recounts a decision or a promise that the record does not
support. A note that insists "the board approved this expansion last
year" when the minutes show only a discussion has built an argument on
something that never occurred. The memory is sincere and vivid, which is
exactly what makes it dangerous.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

The score adds up weighted hits from recollection phrase families per
1,000 words, then adds the intensity of certain-sounding modal words,
the affective heat of the writing, and the repetition of a single frame.
It then subtracts the credit a rationale earns for documented
uncertainty and cited evidence. Flag false memory when a recounted event
or detail is not supported by the record.

r_RLH is the rationale-language heuristic risk. h_k is the count of
phrase-family hits. w_k is the weight given to each phrase family.
W_words is the rationale word count. μ_modal is the intensity of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the degree of frame repetition. u_uncertain is the
documented uncertainty. c_counter is the counterfactuals offered.
e_cited is the cited evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag fits a confident claim about a past decision that the minutes do not confirm. Check the written record before acting on the recollection. The more certain a memory of approval feels, the more worth it is to verify. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, false memory can manufacture a commitment or a result that never existed and then drive real spending. Tie consequential claims about the past to documents, not recall. A vivid, certain memory is a reason to check the record, not a substitute for it. |

**Serial Recall Effect**

**What it measures**

The serial recall effect is the way the position of an item in a list
shapes whether it is remembered, with the first and last items holding
fast and the middle fading. A summary that quotes only the opening and
closing points of a long review has let order, not importance, choose
the evidence. The pattern hides whatever sat in the middle.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

Read plainly, the score counts weighted bias-language hits per 1,000
words and adds deterministic and emotional language, then subtracts the
credit a rationale earns for naming its own uncertainty and citing
evidence.

w_k is the weight of phrase family k. h_k is the count of hits in that
family. W_words is the document length in words. μ_modal is the level of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the amount of frame repetition. u_uncertain and c_counter
and e_cited are the offsetting credits for stated uncertainty and cited
evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, watch for this when a meeting summary echoes the first and last speakers and loses the rest. The flag asks whether the middle of the discussion was weighed at all. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the flag rises when a long diligence file is compressed to its bookends. It prompts a deliberate pass through the overlooked middle. |

**Stereotypical Bias**

**What it measures**

Stereotypical bias is the substitution of a learned group expectation
for the evidence of the case in front of you. A hiring note that leans
on what people like this candidate usually do has stopped reading the
candidate. In language it surfaces as group nouns standing where
specific facts should be.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

Read plainly, the score counts weighted bias-language hits per 1,000
words and adds deterministic and emotional language, then subtracts the
credit a rationale earns for naming its own uncertainty and citing
evidence.

w_k is the weight of phrase family k. h_k is the count of hits in that
family. W_words is the document length in words. μ_modal is the level of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the amount of frame repetition. u_uncertain and c_counter
and e_cited are the offsetting credits for stated uncertainty and cited
evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, raise this when a judgment about a person or a partner rests on a group label rather than on what that person or partner has actually done. The flag calls for case-specific evidence. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the flag rises in hiring and customer judgments that lean on a group expectation. It returns the decision to the record of the individual case. |

**Memory Inhibition**

**What it measures**

Memory inhibition is the way recalling one thing can push a related
thing out of reach, so remembering the headline fact quietly buries the
qualifying one. A review that fixes on the single number everyone
repeats may have lost the caveat that came with it. The effect is
invisible from the inside, which is what makes it dangerous.

**How it is calculated**

r_RLH = \[∑\_k w_kh_k/(W_words/1000)\] + μ_modal + a_affect +
\|Δφ_frame\| − (u_uncertain + c_counter + e_cited)

Read plainly, the score counts weighted bias-language hits per 1,000
words and adds deterministic and emotional language, then subtracts the
credit a rationale earns for naming its own uncertainty and citing
evidence.

w_k is the weight of phrase family k. h_k is the count of hits in that
family. W_words is the document length in words. μ_modal is the level of
deterministic modal language. a_affect is the affective intensity.
Δφ_frame is the amount of frame repetition. u_uncertain and c_counter
and e_cited are the offsetting credits for stated uncertainty and cited
evidence.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, watch for this when a much-repeated figure crowds out the condition attached to it. The flag asks the author to restore the qualifier next to the headline. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, the flag rises when a memorable metric travels without its caveat. It reunites the number with the condition that makes it true. |

**Governance Discipline**

*Governance Discipline covers the ways a board or review body fails to
govern itself. The entries describe blind spots in the very process
meant to catch blind spots, and the records that reveal them.*

**Bias Blind Spot**

**What it measures**

The bias blind spot is the habit of spotting distortion in everyone
else's thinking while feeling clear-eyed about one's own. A board reads
a rival nonprofit's failed merger and shakes its head at the obvious
wishful thinking, then approves its own merger on the same kind of thin
evidence an hour later. The mind grants itself an exemption it grants no
one else. This is a risk to the quality of a decision and not a charge
against anyone's character, and it bites hardest in the very rooms that
pride themselves on being rigorous.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this flag is worth raising on consent agendas and program-risk reviews, the moments when a group is most tempted to wave its own judgment through. When it appears, name a person to argue the other side and set a real deadline and an evidence bar before the organization commits money or its good name. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this belongs in audit and strategy and the review of any acquisition, where confidence in one's own analysis runs high. When it appears, ask the minutes to record the dissent and the alternatives weighed, and write down what would have to be true to walk away. |

**Law of Triviality (Bike-Shedding)**

**What it measures**

The law of triviality is the pull toward the small and familiar question
when a large and hard one sits on the same agenda. A board will spend 40
minutes on the wording of a press release and 4 minutes approving a 2
million dollar reserve drawdown, because everyone has an opinion about
words and almost no one feels equipped to weigh the reserves. The easy
item is comfortable and the hard item is frightening, so attention
drifts to the safe ground. The cost is that the decision that truly
moves the future gets the least scrutiny.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, watch for this when the minutes show long debate on a logo or a small line item while a major program risk passes with a nod. When it appears, give the heavy item a named owner and a deadline so it cannot be crowded out by easier talk. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this shows up when a committee polishes a familiar detail and rushes the capital allocation that actually shapes the year. When it appears, set the agenda so the large decision gets the time and the written challenge it deserves. |

**Information Bias**

**What it measures**

Information bias is the urge to gather more before deciding, even when
the new facts cannot change what one will do. A board defers a clear
hiring choice to commission a third reference check, though it has
already agreed it would hire the candidate under any plausible result.
The waiting feels responsible, yet it buys nothing but delay and the
comfort of seeming thorough. The honest test is whether a finding could
actually flip the decision, and if it cannot, the search is costing time
it does not earn.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this appears as a study or a survey requested before a decision that the group has, in truth, already made. When it appears, ask plainly what result would change the choice, and if nothing would, set a deadline and proceed. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the extra diligence stage that delays a launch or a deal without the power to alter it. When it appears, weigh the expected value of the added analysis against the cost of waiting and act once that value falls to near zero. |

**Ambiguity Bias**

**What it measures**

Ambiguity bias is the reflex to back away from an option whose odds are
unclear, even when it may be the wiser bet than the familiar one. A
board renews a known vendor whose results have quietly stalled rather
than try a newer partner whose track record is thinner but whose promise
is greater, because a vague unknown feels worse than a measured
disappointment. The mind treats the absence of a clean probability as a
danger in itself. Sometimes that caution is sound, and sometimes it
quietly forecloses the better path.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this shows up when a promising but unproven program loses to a tired but familiar one only because its outcomes are harder to predict. When it appears, ask someone to argue the unfamiliar option's case and set an evidence bar that judges it on merit rather than on comfort. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the safe incumbent chosen over the new market simply because the new market resists a tidy forecast. When it appears, record the alternatives weighed so an unknown is not mistaken for a loss before the analysis is done. |

**List-Length Effect**

**What it measures**

The list-length effect is the way the sheer number of items on a page
bends attention and memory, apart from what any single item is worth. A
board packet that lists 18 risks can leave each one feeling minor, while
a packet naming only the 2 that matter makes both land with weight.
Length itself becomes a kind of argument, drowning the few things that
should hold the room. The danger is that the most important item gets
the same passing glance as the least.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, watch for the long risk register or the crowded consent agenda that flattens every item into the same low importance. When it appears, pull the decisions that truly matter onto their own page with a named owner and a deadline so length does not bury them. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the dense dashboard where a serious threat reads the same as a routine note. When it appears, separate the few decisions that move capital or product and give each its own written challenge. |

**Spacing Effect**

**What it measures**

The spacing effect is the way ideas met again and again across weeks
stick in the mind more firmly than ideas heard once, whatever their real
merit. A proposal raised at four straight board meetings starts to feel
settled and wise simply because it has been around, while a sharper idea
mentioned once fades. Repetition over time does the quiet work of
persuasion that evidence should be doing. The risk is that the board
mistakes familiarity for proof.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this appears when a recurring agenda item gains support through sheer repetition rather than new evidence. When it appears, ask what fresh facts have arrived since the last hearing and set an evidence bar that repetition alone cannot clear. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the pet initiative that survives quarter after quarter because it keeps reappearing, not because it keeps performing. When it appears, record the disconfirming evidence and a named challenger so familiarity is not read as merit. |

**Modality Effect**

**What it measures**

The modality effect is the way the channel of a message, whether spoken
aloud or shown on a slide or buried in a written packet, shifts how much
weight it carries and how well it is remembered. A risk delivered as a
confident verbal aside in the meeting can eclipse a graver risk left in
a dense appendix no one opened. The medium, not the substance, decides
what the board recalls when it votes. The result is that the loudest
channel wins, even when the quiet one held the more important truth.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, watch for the critical fact that lived only in a slide or only in a spoken remark and so was weighed by how it arrived rather than what it said. When it appears, make sure the material risks reach the board in writing with a named owner before the vote. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the verbal reassurance that outweighs a written warning tucked into an appendix. When it appears, ask that the decisive evidence be set down in the minutes in the same form so the channel stops doing the deciding. |

**Testing Effect**

**What it measures**

The testing effect is the simple truth that a fact one has to recall and
state aloud lodges in memory far better than a fact merely read over. A
board that is asked to restate last quarter's biggest risk in its own
words will carry that risk into the next decision, while a board that
only skimmed it will not. Active retrieval turns a passive packet into
something the room actually owns. The lesson for governance is that a
board remembers what it is made to work for.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, use this in reverse as a tool, by asking members to restate the key risk or assumption before a vote rather than nodding along to the packet. A board that has to say the threat in its own words will hold it when the money is on the line. |
