| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, build the review so directors must recall and challenge the core assumptions aloud rather than passively absorb the deck. The decision that the board has had to defend in the room is the one it will govern well afterward. |

**Cue-Dependent Forgetting**

**What it measures**

Cue-dependent forgetting is the way knowledge a board truly possesses
can vanish at the moment of choice because nothing in the room calls it
back. A director who learned of a donor's quiet conflict of interest in
a hallway months ago may never recall it during the vote, simply because
the boardroom holds no reminder of that conversation. The knowledge was
there, but the trigger was absent. The decision then proceeds as if the
missing fact had never existed.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this shows up when a relevant fact known to someone in the room never surfaces because the agenda gave no prompt to retrieve it. When it appears, build the standing reminders, such as a conflicts check, that pull buried knowledge into the open before the vote. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the prior warning or past commitment that no one recalls because the meeting carried no cue to summon it. When it appears, attach the relevant history to the decision record so the board decides with what it already knows. |

**Google Effect (Digital Amnesia)**

**What it measures**

The Google effect is the way people remember where a fact is stored
rather than the fact itself, so a board can feel informed while the
actual knowledge sits unopened in a shared drive. Directors nod along to
a financial assumption because they know the model exists somewhere, yet
no one has opened it in this meeting or the last. The sense of knowing
outruns the knowing. Live deliberation thins out when the room trusts
the archive to remember on its behalf.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this appears when the board assumes a stored document settles a question that no one has actually reopened and read. When it appears, ask that the key record be opened and walked through in the room before the decision rests on it. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the model or memo everyone references and no one rereads, so a stale figure governs a live decision. When it appears, require that the source be opened and checked against the present facts before the vote. |

**Premature Closure**

**What it measures**

Premature closure is the moment a board stops thinking the instant a
plausible answer appears, before the obvious alternatives have been
tested. A director offers a tidy explanation for a sudden drop in
revenue, the room exhales, and the agenda moves on, though a second
cause may sit just behind the first. The relief of having an answer ends
the search that should have continued. What looked like resolution was
only the comfort of closing the question early.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this is the first comfortable explanation accepted as final while a more troubling cause goes untested. When it appears, name someone to argue the alternative and hold the question open until that case has had a fair hearing. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the early diagnosis of a market shift taken as settled before rival explanations are weighed. When it appears, record the alternatives considered and the evidence that would reopen the question before the matter is closed. |

**Search Satisfying**

**What it measures**

Search satisficing is the habit of stopping at the first adequate
answer, content that something fits, while better or contradictory
evidence still sits within reach. A board reviewing a troubled program
finds one reasonable cause for the trouble and ends the inquiry, leaving
a second and larger problem in the same report unread. The first
satisfactory finding quiets the urge to keep looking. The danger is not
that the answer is wrong but that it is incomplete, and the rest goes
unseen.

**How it is calculated**

r_GD = m_challenge + m_dissent + u_owner_date + a_unbounded + p_closure

The governance-discipline score adds up the marks of missing rigor in a
decision record. A record with no written challenge, no recorded
dissent, an unfilled owner or date, an open-ended analysis, and an early
close earns the highest score and the loudest flag. Read it as a request
to tidy the record before the decision is treated as final, not as a
claim about anyone's intent.

r_GD is the governance-discipline risk.

m_challenge is the missing challenge record.

m_dissent is the missing dissent record.

u_owner_date is the unresolved owner or date field.

a_unbounded is the unbounded-analysis indicator.

p_closure is the premature-closure or search-satisfying marker.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, this shows up when the first plausible cause of a problem ends the review and a second issue in the same file stays buried. When it appears, ask whether anything nearby was left unexamined and set the bar for what counts as a complete look. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, this is the audit or post-mortem that stops at the first finding and misses a larger fault in the same data. When it appears, require a written challenge confirming the search continued past the first acceptable answer. |

**Organizational Learning**

*Organizational Learning covers the patterns that stop an institution
from learning from its own past. Each entry describes how a true record
of what was predicted and what occurred gets rewritten into a more
comforting story.*

**Hindsight Bias**

**What it measures**

Hindsight bias is the mind's quiet habit of treating an outcome, once
known, as something that was always going to happen. Before the result,
the future looked like a fog of competing possibilities. After it, the
fog clears and memory rewrites the past so the answer seems to have been
visible all along. Picture a board reading the closing report on a
service expansion that failed. The room nods that the warning signs were
obvious, yet the original minutes show a careful split decision made
with real uncertainty. This pattern blocks learning because an
organization that believes it already knew the answer feels no need to
study how it actually reasoned, and so it carries the same blind spots
into the next decision.

**How it is calculated**

r_OL = \|p_pre − y\| + ε_cal + r_cert + m_bias + g_note

The score reads as the gap between the forecast and the outcome, plus
calibration error, plus the weight of certainty language added after the
fact, plus any mismatch between which biases were confirmed or
disproved, plus how incomplete the outcome notes are. A high score flags
a record that has rewritten its own decision quality once the result was
in, or that failed to revise its priors for next time.

r_OL is the organizational-learning risk.

p_pre is the pre-outcome forecast.

y is the realized outcome.

ε_cal is the calibration error.

r_cert is the retrospective certainty language.

m_bias is the confirmed or disproved bias-list mismatch.

g_note is the outcome-note completeness gap.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the PCC executive director and board, reach for this after a campaign, an enterprise launch, or a community partnership has run its course. Lay the original forecast beside what actually happened, and notice whether the story in the room has quietly shifted toward we saw this coming. The honest comparison, written into the next board packet without blame to staff or participants, is what lets the organization read its own record clearly. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For a for-profit C-suite and board, hindsight bias bites hardest in post-investment reviews and M&A integration retrospectives, where a known result tempts everyone to call the original call obvious. Judge the decision against the evidence that sat on the table at approval time, not against the answer that arrived later. Only then will the recalibrated forecasts and thresholds you carry into the next deal rest on what the company truly knew. |

**Outcome Bias**

**What it measures**

Outcome bias is the error of grading a decision by how it turned out
rather than by how sound it was when it was made. A good process can
meet bad luck, and a reckless one can stumble into a win. The mind,
seeing only the result, hands out praise and blame accordingly. Imagine
a hiring choice that followed a thorough review yet ended in an early
departure. If the board condemns the process purely because the person
left, it has confused a single outcome with the quality of the
reasoning. An organization that learns this way punishes good judgment
that drew a bad card and rewards luck dressed as skill, so its people
quietly learn to chase results instead of sound thinking.

**How it is calculated**

r_OL = \|p_pre − y\| + ε_cal + r_cert + m_bias + g_note

The score reads as the gap between the forecast and the outcome, plus
calibration error, plus the weight of certainty language added after the
fact, plus any mismatch between which biases were confirmed or
disproved, plus how incomplete the outcome notes are. A high score flags
a record that has rewritten its own decision quality once the result was
in, or that failed to revise its priors for next time.

r_OL is the organizational-learning risk.

p_pre is the pre-outcome forecast.

y is the realized outcome.

ε_cal is the calibration error.

r_cert is the retrospective certainty language.

m_bias is the confirmed or disproved bias-list mismatch.

g_note is the outcome-note completeness gap.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the PCC executive director and board, use this after a campaign, a hiring decision, or a service expansion, especially when the result was disappointing. Ask whether the choice was reasonable given what was known at the time, separate from how it landed. Revising the next board packet on that basis, without blaming staff or participants, keeps the organization from teaching itself to fear sound decisions that happened to fail. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For a for-profit C-suite and board, outcome bias distorts post-investment reviews and risk-event reviews whenever a loss invites a hunt for someone to blame. Weigh the decision against the evidence available at approval time, and let a bad result prompt inquiry rather than automatic condemnation. Incentives and thresholds tuned to process quality, not to the roll of the dice, are what keep capable judgment in the room. |

**Choice-Supportive Bias**

**What it measures**

Choice-supportive bias is the way memory flatters the option we chose
and tarnishes the ones we passed over. The path taken grows more
attractive in recollection than it was on the day, and the rejected
paths grow worse. Consider a director recalling a vendor selected two
years ago, now remembering the winner as the clear front-runner and the
runners-up as obviously weaker, though the scoring sheet had them nearly
tied. This pattern blocks learning because it erases the closeness of
the original call. An organization that remembers every past choice as
obviously correct never examines the moments when it nearly went another
way, and so it cannot tell luck from wisdom.

**How it is calculated**

r_OL = \|p_pre − y\| + ε_cal + r_cert + m_bias + g_note

The score reads as the gap between the forecast and the outcome, plus
calibration error, plus the weight of certainty language added after the
fact, plus any mismatch between which biases were confirmed or
disproved, plus how incomplete the outcome notes are. A high score flags
a record that has rewritten its own decision quality once the result was
in, or that failed to revise its priors for next time.

r_OL is the organizational-learning risk.

p_pre is the pre-outcome forecast.

y is the realized outcome.

ε_cal is the calibration error.

r_cert is the retrospective certainty language.

m_bias is the confirmed or disproved bias-list mismatch.

g_note is the outcome-note completeness gap.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the PCC executive director and board, use this after a partnership or a service expansion where one option was picked over close alternatives. Pull the original comparison and check whether memory has since widened the gap between the winner and the rest. Recording the true closeness in the next board packet, without blame, keeps the organization honest about how near its choices really were. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For a for-profit C-suite and board, choice-supportive bias colors product retrospectives and M&A integration reviews, where the chosen acquisition or feature gets remembered as the obvious winner. Return to the original scoring and preserve how close the contest actually was. Forecasts and thresholds built on an honest memory of the decision, rather than a flattering one, give the next choice a fair footing. |

**Post-Purchase Rationalization**

**What it measures**

Post-purchase rationalization is the mind's work of manufacturing
reasons that make a commitment already made feel wiser than the evidence
warranted. Once the money is spent or the contract signed, discomfort
pushes us to build a case for the choice rather than to test it. Picture
a leadership team that approved a costly platform, then, as problems
mount, assembles fresh justifications for why it was always the right
buy. This pattern blocks learning because the reasons arrive after the
decision and serve to defend it, not to understand it. An organization
busy justifying its commitments stops asking whether they were sound,
and so it repeats the same purchases with the same false confidence.

**How it is calculated**

r_OL = \|p_pre − y\| + ε_cal + r_cert + m_bias + g_note

The score reads as the gap between the forecast and the outcome, plus
calibration error, plus the weight of certainty language added after the
fact, plus any mismatch between which biases were confirmed or
disproved, plus how incomplete the outcome notes are. A high score flags
a record that has rewritten its own decision quality once the result was
in, or that failed to revise its priors for next time.

r_OL is the organizational-learning risk.

p_pre is the pre-outcome forecast.

y is the realized outcome.

ε_cal is the calibration error.

r_cert is the retrospective certainty language.

m_bias is the confirmed or disproved bias-list mismatch.

g_note is the outcome-note completeness gap.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the PCC executive director and board, use this after a campaign or an enterprise launch that drew significant resources. Watch for new reasons that appear only to defend the spend, and set them beside the rationale recorded at approval. A board packet that distinguishes the original case from later justification, written without blame, helps the organization spend its next dollar on evidence rather than on comfort. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For a for-profit C-suite and board, post-purchase rationalization shapes post-investment reviews after a large commitment, where mounting trouble invites ever-better reasons for the original choice. Compare the case made at approval with the case being made now, and treat any widening gap as a signal. Recalibrating forecasts and incentives on the early evidence, not the later defense, protects the next capital decision from inherited self-justification. |

**Fading Affect Bias**

**What it measures**

Fading affect bias is the tendency for the sting of bad experiences to
fade faster than the warmth of good ones, gradually shifting how the
past is judged. Time sands down the sharp edges of trouble while leaving
pleasant memories largely intact. A board looking back on a turbulent
merger may recall mostly the eventual relief, the long stretch of strain
having quietly dimmed. This pattern blocks learning because the
emotional record that should warn an organization away from painful
mistakes grows fainter with each passing quarter. The hard lessons lose
their force, and the institution drifts back toward the very choices
that once cost it dearly.

**How it is calculated**

r_OL = \|p_pre − y\| + ε_cal + r_cert + m_bias + g_note

The score reads as the gap between the forecast and the outcome, plus
calibration error, plus the weight of certainty language added after the
fact, plus any mismatch between which biases were confirmed or
disproved, plus how incomplete the outcome notes are. A high score flags
a record that has rewritten its own decision quality once the result was
in, or that failed to revise its priors for next time.

r_OL is the organizational-learning risk.

p_pre is the pre-outcome forecast.

y is the realized outcome.

ε_cal is the calibration error.

r_cert is the retrospective certainty language.

m_bias is the confirmed or disproved bias-list mismatch.

g_note is the outcome-note completeness gap.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the PCC executive director and board, use this when revisiting a difficult past episode, such as a strained partnership or a hard funding year. Check the contemporaneous notes against present memory, which may have softened the worst of it. Carrying the original difficulty faithfully into the next board packet, without blame, keeps the organization from repeating a painful choice it has half forgotten. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For a for-profit C-suite and board, fading affect bias dulls risk-event reviews and integration retrospectives as the memory of distress recedes. Anchor the review to records written while the trouble was live, not to the calmer recollection of today. Forecasts and thresholds that preserve the true cost of past pain keep the company from sliding back into the decisions that caused it. |

**Peak-End Rule**

**What it measures**

The peak-end rule is the mind's habit of summing up a long experience by
two moments alone, its most intense point and its ending, while the rest
fades. The full arc of effort and difficulty is compressed into a
feeling shaped mainly by the climax and the finish. Think of a year-long
program judged almost entirely by a triumphant closing event, the months
of uneven progress before it barely registering. This pattern blocks
learning because the record an organization keeps of its own work
becomes a caricature of two scenes. Decisions about whether to repeat an
effort rest on how it peaked and how it ended, not on what the whole
journey actually demanded.

**How it is calculated**

r_OL = \|p_pre − y\| + ε_cal + r_cert + m_bias + g_note

The score reads as the gap between the forecast and the outcome, plus
calibration error, plus the weight of certainty language added after the
fact, plus any mismatch between which biases were confirmed or
disproved, plus how incomplete the outcome notes are. A high score flags
a record that has rewritten its own decision quality once the result was
in, or that failed to revise its priors for next time.

r_OL is the organizational-learning risk.

p_pre is the pre-outcome forecast.

y is the realized outcome.

ε_cal is the calibration error.

r_cert is the retrospective certainty language.

m_bias is the confirmed or disproved bias-list mismatch.

g_note is the outcome-note completeness gap.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the PCC executive director and board, use this when judging a long campaign or service effort by its closing moment. Walk back through the full timeline rather than the high point and the finale alone. A board packet that weighs the whole stretch, written without blame, keeps the organization from repeating an effort that ended well but cost far more along the way than memory admits. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For a for-profit C-suite and board, the peak-end rule skews product retrospectives toward a strong launch or a clean wind-down, eclipsing the long middle. Reconstruct the entire course of the project before deciding whether to do it again. Forecasts that account for the full duration of effort, not just its brightest and final moments, keep capital aimed at work that pays across its whole life. |

**Duration Neglect**

**What it measures**

Duration neglect is the mind's tendency to ignore how long an experience
lasted and to judge it instead by its intensity or its ending. Two weeks
of strain and two months of the same strain can leave nearly the same
imprint in memory. A board may recall a prolonged staffing crisis and a
brief one as roughly equal ordeals, the difference in length having
dropped out of the account. This pattern blocks learning because the
true weight of a sustained problem is the time it consumed, and that is
exactly what fades. An organization blind to duration underestimates the
real cost of long troubles and keeps walking into them.

**How it is calculated**

r_OL = \|p_pre − y\| + ε_cal + r_cert + m_bias + g_note

The score reads as the gap between the forecast and the outcome, plus
calibration error, plus the weight of certainty language added after the
fact, plus any mismatch between which biases were confirmed or
disproved, plus how incomplete the outcome notes are. A high score flags
a record that has rewritten its own decision quality once the result was
in, or that failed to revise its priors for next time.

r_OL is the organizational-learning risk.

p_pre is the pre-outcome forecast.

y is the realized outcome.

ε_cal is the calibration error.

r_cert is the retrospective certainty language.

m_bias is the confirmed or disproved bias-list mismatch.

g_note is the outcome-note completeness gap.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For the PCC executive director and board, use this when reviewing a problem that stretched over many months, such as a long break in service. Put the actual length on the record rather than the feeling it left behind. A board packet that counts the time a difficulty consumed, written without blame, keeps the organization from underrating how costly its long troubles truly were. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For a for-profit C-suite and board, duration neglect understates the toll of drawn-out risk events and slow integrations, since the clock fades from memory. Bring the real elapsed time back into the review and weigh it plainly. Forecasts and thresholds that price the full length of a problem, not just its peak, keep the company from repeatedly committing to ordeals it remembers as shorter than they were. |

**Posterior Probability Error**

**What it measures**

Posterior probability error is the mistake of folding a new outcome into
an old belief the wrong way, so the lesson an organization draws is not
the lesson the evidence supports. A board learns that a campaign missed
its target and quietly concludes the whole strategy was doomed, when the
honest reading is that one assumption was off by 15%. The error matters
because it is how a single bad quarter hardens into a permanent and
false rule.

**How it is calculated**

r_OL = \|p_pre − y\| + ε_cal + r_cert + m_bias + g_note

Read plainly, the score adds the gap between the forecast and the
outcome to the calibration error, the amount of after-the-fact certainty
in the language, the mismatch between flagged and confirmed bias, and
any gap in the outcome notes.

p_pre is the probability stated before the outcome was known. y is the
realized outcome. ε_cal is the calibration error. r_cert is the strength
of retrospective certainty in the write-up. m_bias is the mismatch
between bias flagged beforehand and bias confirmed after. g_note is the
completeness gap in the outcome record.

**Where it is used**

|  |  |
|----|----|
| **Nonprofit director and board** | For a nonprofit director and board, run this check after a campaign or a service expansion closes. It asks whether the after-the-fact story matches what the organization actually predicted beforehand, so a single disappointment does not rewrite a sound plan into a cautionary tale. |
| **Company leadership and board** | For company leadership and a board, use this in post-investment and product reviews. It compares the retrospective verdict against the forecast on record, which keeps one loss from teaching a lesson the numbers never taught. |
